Negations of Equal Universals Are Also Equal
Author proves that contradictories of equal universals are also equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relation between the contradictories of two universals given the relation between the originals, and explain the pedagogical reason for its separate mention.

Core Idea
The contradictories of equal universals are also equal — a truth preserved under immediate inference.

In logic, when two universals are co-extensive, their negations (contradictories) are also co-extensive. This principle seems obvious, yet some commentators thought it needed separate proof. In this passage, the author clarifies that the relation between contradictories follows directly from the original relation, but also defines 'partial mutual exclusion' to clarify the taxonomy of relations. Understanding this helps you see how logical relations are preserved under negation, a key tool in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original relation
Two universals are equal if they are true of exactly the same things. For example, 'human' and 'rational' are equal in the classical Aristotelian framework. Note that the author assumes this definition.
2
Formulate the claim about contradictories
The author states that the contradictories of two equal universals (e.g., 'non-human' and 'non-rational') are also equal. This follows from the symmetry of equivalence: if A ↔ B, then by contraposition ¬A ↔ ¬B.
This is an immediate inference requiring no additional premises.
3
Examine the controversy
Some commentators claimed the initial discussion was about the original terms only, not their contradictories. The author rejects this by showing the two pairs are interchangeable: (A,B) and (¬A,¬B) stand in the same relation of equality and contradictoriness.
The hashīsh author emphasizes that specifying only one side is arbitrary without evidence.
4
Understand the pedagogical purpose
The separate mention of the contradictories' relation is not logically necessary but pedagogically useful. By knowing the original relation, students can immediately infer the relation between contradictories without recalculating.
This reflects a deeper principle: logical relations are transitive under negation.
5
Apply the definition of partial mutual exclusion
The passage defines 'partial mutual exclusion' (al-tabāyun al-juz'ī) as covering both complete exclusion and partial overlap. This concept is used later in relations among universals.
Note that this definition unifies two previously separate categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « ونقيضا هما كذلك» يعنى ان نقيضى المتساويين أيضاً متساويان في الخارج وذلك انهم لمارأ وا قول بعضهم ان الكلى مشترك بين كثيرين ظنوا الاشتراك بحسب الخارج على ما هو المتبادر منه فنبه على بطلان زعمهم بتقسيمه الكلى على الممتنع والممكن (محمد على) (٥) لا يخفى ان المتساويين كما ذكر، عبارة عن الكليين اللذين يكون الصدق الكلي بينهما من الجانبين وهذا المعنى متساوى النسبة بالقياس الى عينى المتساويين ونقيضيها فلا وجه للتعرض الى بيان النسبة بين النقيضين ثانياً وهكذا الكلام فى البواق الآتية اللهم الا ان يدعى ان الكلام اولاً انما هو مخصوص بالعينين كما يظهر من بعضهم . و فيه مع انه قول لا يعاضده دليل انه لا معنى لتخصيص الكلام بالعينين لان النقيضين عينان بالنسبة الى العينين والعينان نقيضان بالنسبة الى النقيضين فكما يصح ان يقال: ان الانسان عين واللاانسان نقيض، فكذلك يصح العكس من غير تفاوت كما يشهد به بعض كلمات المحشى ايضاً بعيد هذا. نعم يمكن ان يقال: ان غرضهم من ذلك تسهيل الامر للطلاب بأن يحكموا بعد ملاحظة النسبة بين الشيئين و معرفة انه من اى انواع النسب الاربع بان بين نقيضيهما تساو يا او تباينا من غير ان يحتاجوا الى ملاحظة النسبة بينها ايضاً عليحدة فتأمل (ميرزا محمد علی) (ه) قوله و بين نقيضيهما تباين :جزئى هو ان يصدق كل من المفهومين بدون الاخر في الجملة سواء لم يتصادقا معاً اصلاً كالمتباينين او تصادقا في بعض المواد ولم يتصادقا في بعض آخر كالعموم من وجه فيعم التباين الجزئى، التباين الكلى والعموم من وجه اذ لم يحصل فى ضمن كل منهما ولذا لم يذكره المصنف في نسب الكليات.
His statement 'And their contradictories are likewise' means that the contradictories of two equal [universals] are also equal in external existence. That is because when they saw the statement of some that the universal is common among many, they thought the commonness is with respect to external existence, as is apparent from it, so he alerted to the invalidity of their claim by dividing the universal into the impossible and possible. (Muhammad 'Ali) It is not hidden that the two equals, as mentioned, are the two universals that have complete predication between them from both sides, and this meaning has equal relation with respect to the two equals themselves and their contradictories. So there is no reason to address the relation between the two contradictories a second time. And likewise the statement in the remaining [relations] that follow, unless one claims that the first statement is specifically about the two themselves, as appears from some. And there is, with the fact that it is an opinion not supported by evidence, no meaning to specifying the statement to the two themselves, because the two contradictories are two equals relative to the two equals, and the two equals are contradictories relative to the two contradictories. So just as it is correct to say: 'Human is the equal [of rational] and non-human is the contradictory', so likewise the reverse is correct without difference, as some words of the annotator also testify beyond this. Yes, it can be said that their purpose in that is to ease the matter for students, so that they judge after observing the relation between two things and knowing which of the four types of relations it is, that between their contradictories there is equality or mutual exclusion, without needing to observe the relation between them also separately. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) His statement: 'And between their contradictories is partial mutual exclusion' means that each of the two concepts applies to something without the other in some way, whether they never coincide together at all, like the mutually exclusive, or they coincide in some matters and do not coincide in others, like generality from one aspect. So partial mutual exclusion includes complete mutual exclusion and generality from one aspect, because it does not occur within each of them, and therefore the author did not mention it among the relations of universals.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement discussing the relation between the contradictories of equal universals. The author begins by stating that the contradictories of two equal universals are also equal. The context is a response to some who mistakenly thought that the 'commonness' of a universal refers to external individuals; the author refutes this by dividing universals into impossible and possible. The commentary then argues that since equality is a symmetric relation applying to the two universals themselves, it applies equally to their contradictories, so there is no need to mention it separately. However, some claimed that the initial discussion was specifically about the two universals themselves, not their contradictories. The author rejects this by pointing out that the two contradictories are 'equals' relative to the original two, and the original two are 'contradictories' relative to the contradictories. Thus, specifying only one side is arbitrary. The purpose of the separate mention is pedagogical: to help students quickly determine the relation between contradictories without recalculation. The passage then transitions to a definition of 'partial mutual exclusion' (تباین جزئى) as applying when each universal applies to something the other does not, whether they are completely exclusive or partially overlapping. This clarifies that complete mutual exclusion and partial generality both fall under partial mutual exclusion. The author uses examples from logical discourse.

التباين الجزئى

A) Complete mutual exclusion
B) Equality
C) Absolute generality
D) Partial generality

Key Takeaway
When two universals are equal, their contradictories are also equal, illustrating that logical relations are preserved under immediate inference; this principle is both logically necessary and pedagogically convenient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Use the definition of partial mutual exclusion to classify the relation between 'winged' (مجنح) and 'horse' (فرس): some horses are winged, some not; some winged are horses, some not.

The Reversal of Contradictories in Generality
Author proves that the contradictories of absolute generality-specificity are in the reverse order of generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that the contradictory of a more general universal is more specific than the contradictory of a more specific universal, using the law of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradictories flip the order of absolute generality-specificity.

This lesson reveals a non-intuitive logical relationship: when one universal is absolutely more general than another, their contradictories reverse that order. Understanding this keeps our reasoning about definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Take a more general universal (e.g., animal) and a more specific one (e.g., human). Their contradictories are non-animal and non-human.
The relationship is absolute generality-specificity, not accidental.
2
State the reversal claim
The author claims that non-animal is more specific than non-human – meaning every non-animal is non-human, but not vice versa.
This is the opposite of the original order.
3
Prove the first direction
Assume something is non-animal but not non-human. Then it is human (since not non-human = human). But every human is animal, so it is animal. This contradicts it being non-animal. Thus every non-animal must be non-human.
This uses the law of non-contradiction.
4
Prove the reverse does not hold
If every non-human were also non-animal, then the contradictories would be co-extensive, implying the originals are co-extensive. But they are not (e.g., a horse is non-human but animal). So non-animal is strictly more specific.
A horse serves as a counterexample.
5
Generalize the principle
For any pair of absolutely general-specific universals, the contradictory of the more general is always more specific than the contradictory of the more specific.
This holds across all Islamic sciences where such hierarchies appear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله» و» نقيضا هما بالعكس» اى نقيضا الاعم والاخص مطلقا اعم و اخص مطلقا لكن بعكس العينين فنقيض الاعم اخص ونقيض الاخص اعم بمعني: ان كلّما صدق عليه نقيض الاعم صدق عليه نقيض الاخص وليس كلما صدق عليه نقيض الاخص صدق عليه نقيض الاعم. اما الاول فلانه لو صدق نقيض الاعم على شيء و لم يصدق عليه نقيض الاخص لصدق مع عين الاخص فيصدق عين الاخص بدون عين الاعم هذا خلف ويمتنع الاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين مثلاً لو صدق اللاحيوان على شيء بدون اللاانسان لصدق عليه الانسان هناك صدق الحيوان بدون الانسان هذا خلف. و أما الثاني: فلانه بعد ما ثبت ان كل نقيض الاعم نقيض الاخص لو كان كل نقيض الاخص نقيض الاعم لكان النقيضان متساويين فيكون نقيضا هما لما العينان متساويين كمامر وقد كان العينان اعم واخص مطلقا هذا خلف.
His statement: 'And their contradictories are reversed' means the contradictories of the absolutely more general and more specific are absolutely more general and more specific, but reversed compared to the original two. So the contradictory of the more general is more specific, and the contradictory of the more specific is more general. The meaning: everything to which the contradictory of the more general applies, the contradictory of the more specific applies to it, but not everything to which the contradictory of the more specific applies does the contradictory of the more general apply to it. As for the first: because if the contradictory of the more general applies to something and the contradictory of the more specific does not apply to it, then the specific itself would apply to it, so the specific would apply without the general — this is a contradiction and impossible due to the impossibility of two contradictories coexisting. For example, if 'non-animal' applied to something without 'non-human', then 'human' would apply to it, and then 'animal' would apply to it, so 'animal' would apply without 'human' — this is a contradiction. As for the second: because after it is established that every contradictory of the more general is a contradictory of the more specific, if every contradictory of the more specific were a contradictory of the more general, then the two contradictories would be equal, and thus the two original universals would be equal, but they were absolutely more general and more specific — this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration of how the contradictories of two universals related by absolute generality-specificity are themselves related in the reverse order. The author begins by stating that the contradictory of the more general universal (e.g., 'non-animal') is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific universal (e.g., 'non-human'), and vice versa. The proof has two parts. First, to show that every individual that is non-animal is also non-human: if something were non-animal but not non-human, then it would be human (since of necessity, if it is not non-human, it is human). But if it is human, then it is an animal (since every human is animal). So it would be both animal and non-animal, which is impossible. Thus the contradictory of the more general (non-animal) implies the contradictory of the more specific (non-human). Second, to show that the reverse does not hold universally: if it did, then the two contradictories would be co-extensive, implying the original two universals are also co-extensive, which contradicts their being absolutely more general and specific. This is a crisp logical argument using the law of non-contradiction and the impossibility of two contradictories. The Islamic example uses 'animal' and 'human' as the original pair, with their contradictories 'non-animal' and 'non-human'.

Key Terms
نقيض الأعم
نقيض الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 'Non-human' is more specific than 'non-animal'.
D) 'Non-animal' and 'non-human' are equal.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a more general universal is always more specific than the contradictory of a more specific universal, and this reversal holds universally for absolute generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the universals 'living being' (hayy) and 'animal' (hayawan). If 'living being' is absolutely more general than 'animal', determine the relationship between their contradictories and prove your answer using the method shown in this lesson.
?

Constitutive and Divisive Rules Across Hierarchies
Commentator explains why not every constitutive of lower is constitutive of higher, and why every divisive of lower is divisive of higher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for whether a differentia (constitutive) of a lower genus/species constitutes a higher genus, and whether a division of a lower necessitates a division of the higher.

Core Idea
What constitutes the lower does NOT constitute the higher; but what divides the lower DOES divide the higher.

In the Porphyrian tree of the five universals, relationships between higher and lower levels are not always reversible. This lesson examines two critical rules: first, that a differentia (constitutive) of a lower species, like 'rational' for human, does not apply to the higher genus 'animal'. Second, that a division of the lower, such as animal into rational and non-rational, necessarily forces a corresponding division in its higher part, like the growing body. Understanding these rules is essential for correct definition and classification in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rules in the Matn
The passage begins with 'not the converse' (lā ʿaks) and 'the divisive is the opposite' (al-muqassim bi'l-ʿaks). The first rule states that not every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher. The second states that every divisive of the lower is divisive of the higher. Locate these in the Arabic.
The matn is quoted within the hashya; look for 'ولا عكس' and 'والمقسم بالعكس'.
2
Examine the Constitutive Rule: Rational and Human
The commentator gives the example of 'rational' (nāṭiq) as constitutive of human (the lower species) but not of animal (the higher genus). Understand why: the lower cannot pass its distinguishing traits upward; the higher when specified shrinks into the lower.
The argument relies on the inability to both attach and delete distinguishing traits simultaneously.
3
Analyze the Divisibility Rule: Animal and Growing Body
The second rule is illustrated: if animal (lower) is divided into rational and non-rational, then the growing body (higher part) is necessarily divided as well. This is because a whole's division entails the division of its parts.
The commentator uses the term 'mulāzama' (necessary concomitance) to stress the logical necessity.
4
Understand Why the Converse of the Divisive Rule Fails
The passage explains that what divides the higher does not necessarily divide the lower. For example, body as higher can be divided into inanimate, plant, animal, but this division does not automatically apply to animal (the lower) because the higher must be considered in its absolute sense, stripped of its own particularities.
The key is that the higher is part of the lower only when abstracted from its own divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الكليات الخمس ولا عكس (*) و المقسم بالعكس )) قوله ولا عکس ای كلياً (۱۱۶) بمعنى انه (۱۱۷) ليس كل (۱۱۸) مقوم للسافل مقوماً للعالى فان الناطق مقوم للسائل الذى هو الانسان وليس هو مقوماً للعالى الذي هو الحيوان. قوله « والمقسم بالعكس»: اى كل مقسم للسافل (۱۱۹) مقسم للعالى نفسه (0) قوله « ولا عکس»: ای و مقوم السافل لا يكون مقوماً للعالى لان السافل لا يترقى الى العالي حتى يكسبه مميزاته و خصوصياته، بخلاف العالى فان العالى اذاقيد بقيود و خصص بمخصصات نزع عن نفسه عنوان العلو والعموم وصار هو السائل عيناً والسافل يمكنه بعد حذف مخصصاته وقيوده ان يصير عالياً ولكنه خلاف مفروض البحث هنا فان المميزات اذا لحقت الحقيقة صغرتها عن نفسها قبل لحوقها بها، الحيوان قبل ان يلحقه مائز الناطق كان أوسع دائرة من نفسه عند ما لحقه هذا المائز، فمن هنا تبين ان مميزات السائل لا تسرى إلى العالى لان سراية السائل الى العالى محتاجة الى ان يحذف السافل عن المميزات التي تبعده عن العالى وتصغر دائرته بالنسبة الى عمومه وانتساب المقومات الى السائل تربطه الى مكانه البعيد عن العالى وبين حذف المميزات والحوقها تمانع، فكيف حذف المميزات والحوقها تمانع، فكيف تسرى مقومات السائل الى العالى؟ واما مقومات العالى فيها أنها تنزل به درجة درجة فهى تقربه إلى السافل فضلا عن كون العالى جزء للسافل دخيلا في مقام ذاته فهو يمده بجوهريته والسافل ليس جزء للعالى حتى يمده من هذا الطريق بجوهريته وخصائصه الذاتية ولاعين العالى ولامثل العالى فليست من طريق السافل مندوحة يتوصل بها الى العالى حتى يوصله شيئاً من مميزاته الذاتية. (التقريب ص ۳۵) + (٥) قوله والمقسم بالعكس اى ان ما يقسم السافل بالملازمة يقسم العالي، لان السافل و هو الحيوان مثلا اذا انقسم الى ناطق و غير ناطق فقد أوجب الحيوان نفسه ان ينقسم النامي الى ناطق وغير ناطق لان النامي جزءه والحقيقة الملتئمة من اجزاء اذا انشطرت انشطرت معها اجزاؤها ادهی لیست وراء الاجزاء شيئا . و اما ما يقسم العالى فلا يقسم السافل لان العالى الذي يكون جزء للسافل هو العالى من حيث هو لا بماله من خصوصيات وتشعبات لانه اذا روعيت هذه التشعبات والخصوصيات فيه لا يكون باطلاقه جزء بل بشعبة منه بحيث تلائم هذا السائل و اما الاطلاق على ارساله في الشعب و الخصوصيات فهو مباين له لاجزء منه، مثلا الجسم الذى هو جزء في الجسم النامي هو الجسم من حيث هو مغضوضاً عن كونه جماداً ونباتاً و حيواناً فان هذه الخصوصيات اذار وعيت فيه لم يكن جزء للنامي الا من شعبة النباتية والحيوانية لا مطلقاً فلذا لا ينحفظ ارتباط العالي بالسافل الامع غض النظر عن خصوصياته التى تشطره وتشعبه ومع غض النظر عنها كيف تسرى الى السافل ؟ اذن فتقسيمات العالي مع مراعاة حفظ ارتباطه بالسافل و انه جزء منه واجبة الاغفال و اذا اغفلت فليست نسری، فاعرفه حق معرفته التقريب ص ۳۶۳۵)
On the Five Universals: 'And not the converse (*) and the divisive is the opposite' His saying 'and not the converse' means universally, i.e., not every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher. For 'rational' is constitutive of the lower which is human, but it is not constitutive of the higher which is animal. His saying 'and the divisive is the opposite' means every divisive of the lower is divisive of the higher itself. (0) His saying 'and not the converse': i.e., the constitutive of the lower is not constitutive of the higher, because the lower does not ascend to the higher such that it acquires its distinguishing traits and particularities. Contrary to the higher, for when the higher is bound by restrictions and specified by specifications, it strips itself of the title of height and generality and becomes the lower itself. And the lower can after deleting its specifications and restrictions become higher, but that is contrary to the assumed context of investigation. For when distinguishing traits attach to the reality, they shrink it from what it was before their attachment. Animal, before the differentia 'rational' attaches to it, was wider in scope than itself when this differentia attaches to it. Hence it is clear that the distinguishing traits of the lower do not spread to the higher, because the spreading of the lower to the higher requires that the lower delete the distinguishing traits that distance it from the higher and shrink its scope relative to its generality. And the attribution of constitutives to the lower ties it to its place far from the higher, and between deletion of distinguishing traits and their attachment there is mutual opposition. So how can there be deletion of distinguishing traits and their attachment simultaneously? So how can the constitutives of the lower spread to the higher? As for the constitutives of the higher, they descend with it degree by degree, bringing it closer to the lower, besides the fact that the higher is a part of the lower, entering into its essence. So it supplies it with its essentiality, while the lower is not a part of the higher such that it supplies it from this path with its essentiality and intrinsic properties. Neither the essence of the higher nor the like of the higher is obtained from the path of the lower to reach the higher, such that it could convey to it something of its intrinsic distinguishing traits. (al-Taqrib p. 35) + (5) His saying 'and the divisive is the opposite' means that what divides the lower necessarily divides the higher. For if the lower, such as animal, is divided into rational and non-rational, then animal itself necessitates that the growing (nāmi) be divided into rational and non-rational, because the growing is its part, and a reality composed of parts, when it is split, its parts are split along with it, so much so that there is nothing beyond the parts. As for what divides the higher, it does not divide the lower, because the higher that is a part of the lower is the higher as such, not with its particularities and ramifications. For if these ramifications and particularities are taken into account, it is not absolutely a part, but rather a branch of it that suits this lower. But the absolute release of its branches and particularities is distinct from it, not part of it. For example, body that is part of the growing body is body as such, abstracted from being inanimate, plant, or animal. For if these particularities are considered in it, it is not a part of the growing except from the branch of plantness or animalness, not absolutely. Therefore, the connection of the higher to the lower is not preserved except by disregarding its particularities that split and ramify it. And with disregard of them, how can they spread to the lower? So the divisions of the higher, while preserving its connection to the lower and that it is part of it, must be ignored. And when ignored, they do not spread. So know it with true knowledge. (al-Taqrib pp. 35-36)
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage continues the hashya by discussing two converse rules regarding the constitutive (muqawwim) and divisive (muqassim) of higher and lower genera/species. First, the matn states 'not the converse' (lā ʿaks), meaning that not every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher. The commentator explains this with the example of 'rational' (nāṭiq): it constitutes the lower species 'human', but does not constitute the higher genus 'animal'. The reason is that the lower cannot ascend to the higher to pass on its distinguishing traits; rather, the higher, when specified, shrinks to become the lower. The distinguishing traits of the lower would require the higher to delete its own traits, which is contradictory. Thus, the constitutives of the lower do not apply to the higher. Second, the matn states 'the divisive is the opposite' (al-muqassim bi'l-ʿaks), meaning that every divisive of the lower is also divisive of the higher. The commentator explains this by noting that if the lower (e.g., animal) is divided into rational and non-rational, then its parts (like the growing body, which is a higher genus) are necessarily divided as well, because a whole's division entails the division of its parts. However, the converse does not hold: what divides a higher genus (e.g., body) does not necessarily divide the lower species (e.g., animal), because the higher as part of the lower must be considered in its absolute sense, stripped of its own particularities. The higher's own divisions (e.g., body into inanimate, plant, animal) do not carry down to the lower unless the lower's specific constraints are ignored. The passage illustrates the subtle logical relationships between hierarchical levels in the Porphyrian tree, emphasizing that constitution flows downward but division (in terms of the lower's division) flows upward.

Key Terms
عكس
مقوم السافل
مقسم السافل
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does 'rational' not constitute the genus 'animal'?
A) Because 'animal' is a lower genus than 'human'.
B) Because the lower cannot transmit its distinguishing traits upward to the higher, as the higher would need to delete its own traits.
C) Because 'rational' is not essential to 'animal'.
D) Because 'animal' is a species, not a genus.

Key Takeaway
Differentiae flow downward through the hierarchy, but divisions flow upward: what constitutes the lower does not constitute the higher, but what divides the lower necessarily divides the higher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Use the rule from the passage. (Answer: Yes, because constitutives of the higher descend to the lower; the differentia of animal constitutes human as part of its essence.)

Use the passage's explanation about divisive of higher not dividing the lower. (Answer: No, because the division of Body into these branches does not propagate to Growing Body unless the particularities are disregarded; Growing Body is a part of Body only when Body is abstracted.)

Contradictory of a Particular Compound Proposition
Refines the rule for contradictories of particular compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a particular compound proposition using the correct method of a universal disjunction per individual.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a particular compound requires a universal disjunction per individual.

Logic teaches us that the contradictory of a compound proposition is not simply the negation of its parts, but an exclusive disjunction of their contradictories. However, when the compound is particular, a subtle refinement is needed: a naive disjunction of universal contradictories fails. This passage reveals the precise method, demonstrating its application with a clear example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound and its parts
A compound proposition contains two judgments, often with temporal or other modifiers. For example, 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' has two parts: (1) the conditional 'as long as he is a writer' and (2) the negation 'not perpetually'.
The two parts are not the subject and predicate, but the two components of the judgment.
2
Apply the general rule for compound contradictories
The contradictory of a compound is an exclusive disjunction (man' al-khuluw) of the contradictories of its two parts. For a universal compound like the example, this works directly: the contradictory is 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually'.
Ensure that the disjunction prevents both parts from being false simultaneously.
3
Recognize the problem with particular compounds
If the compound is particular, e.g., 'Some animal is human actually, not perpetually', a simple disjunction of the contradictories of its parts (which are universals: 'No animal is human perpetually' and 'Every animal is human perpetually') fails because both universals can be false together.
This happens when some animals are human perpetually and some are not, making both universal statements false.
4
Apply the correct method for particular compounds
First, note that the contradictory of a particular is a universal. Then, for each individual of the subject, disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts. In the example, we get 'Every animal is either human perpetually or not human perpetually'.
This ensures the contradictory is true when the original is false, and vice versa.
5
Verify with a logic diagram
Test the method: if 'Some animal is human actually, not perpetually' is false, then either no animal is human actually, or every animal is human perpetually. The contradictory 'Every animal is either human perpetually or not human perpetually' will be true precisely when the original is false.
This aligns with the requirement that a contradictory always has opposite truth value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « وللمركبة»: قد علمت ان نقيض كل شيء رفعه فاعلم ان رفع المركب انما يكون برفع احد جزئيه لا على التعيين بل على سبيل منع اذ يجوز ان يكون برفع جزئيه فنقيض القضية المركبة نقيض احد جزئيه على سبيل منع الخلو فنقيض قولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً لا دائماً اى لاشيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل، قضية منفصلة مانعة الخلو و هى قولنا اما بعض الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الاصابع بالامكان حين هو كاتب و اما بعض الكاتب متحرك الاصابع دائماً. و انت بعد اطلاعك على حقايق المركبات ونقائض البسائط تتمكن من استخراج التفاصيل . قوله « ولكن فى الجزئية بالنسبة الى كل فرد «فرد» یعنى لا يكفي في اخذ نقيض القضية المركبة الجزئية الترديد بين نقيضى جزئيها و هما الكليتان، اذ قد يكذب وهما المركبة الجزئية كقوننا بعض الحيوان انسان بالفعل لا دائماً و يكذب كلا نقیضی جزئيها ايضاً و هما قولنا: لاشيء من الحيوان بانسان دائماً وقولنا كل حيوان انسان دائماً وحينئذ فطريق اخذ نقيض المركبة الجزئية ان توضع افراد الموضوع كلها ضرورة ان نقيض الجزئية هي الكلية، ثم يردد بين نقيضي الجزئين بالنسبة الى كل واحد من تلك الأفراد ويقال فى المثال المذكور كل حيوان اما انسان دائماً او لیس بانسان دائماً و حينئذ فيصدق النقيض و هي قضية حملية مرددة المحمول فقوله الى كل فرد فردای من افراد الموضوع
His statement: 'And for the compound': You have known that the contradictory of everything is its negation. Know that the negation of the compound is only by negating one of its two parts, not determinately, but by way of preventing emptiness, for it is possible that it be by negating both parts. So the contradictory of a compound proposition is the contradictory of one of its two parts on the condition of preventing emptiness. Thus the contradictory of our statement: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers actually', is an exclusive disjunctive proposition, which is our statement: 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually.' And after you are informed of the realities of compounds and the contradictories of simples, you will be able to extract the details. His statement: 'But in the particular, with respect to every single individual' means: it is not sufficient in taking the contradictory of a particular compound proposition to disjunct between the contradictories of its two parts, which are the two universals, for both may be false along with the particular compound, like our statement: 'Some animal is human actually not perpetually', and both contradictories of its two parts are false, namely our statements: 'No animal is human perpetually' and 'Every animal is human perpetually'. In that case, the method of taking the contradictory of a particular compound is to posit all the individuals of the subject necessarily, because the contradictory of a particular is a universal; then one disjuncts between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to each one of those individuals, and one says in the mentioned example: 'Every animal is either human perpetually or not human perpetually.' Then the contradictory is true, and it is a predicative proposition with a disjunctive predicate. So his statement 'to every single individual' means to the individuals of the subject.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary refining the understanding of how to find the contradictory of a compound proposition, especially when it is particular. The author first restates the general rule: the contradictory of any thing is its negation. For a compound proposition (e.g., a conditional or a proposition with two temporal modifiers), its negation is achieved by negating one of its two parts, but not any one part arbitrarily; rather, it must be such that it prevents both parts from being false simultaneously (i.e., an exclusive disjunction). The example given is the proposition 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' (which means 'not always, but only during writing'). Its contradictory is an exclusive disjunction: 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually.' This ensures that exactly one part is true. The passage then addresses a special case: particular compound propositions (e.g., 'Some animal is human actually, not perpetually'). The naive approach might be to take the contradictory by disjoining between the contradictories of its two parts (i.e., 'No animal is human perpetually' and 'Every animal is human perpetually'), but both of these universals can be false simultaneously—for example, if some animals are human perpetually and some are not. So that method fails. The correct method, explained here, is to note that the contradictory of a particular proposition is a universal (i.e., 'every' or 'no'), and then for each individual subject, we disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts. In the example, we say 'Every animal is either human perpetually or not human perpetually,' which is a universal categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate. This properly captures the contradictory. The author notes that this explanation presupposes understanding of the realities of compound propositions and the contradictories of simple ones, and that with that knowledge one can derive the details.

نقيض
قضية مركبة
منع الخلو
جزئية

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a particular compound proposition is a universal proposition with a disjunctive predicate over the individuals of the subject.

Forming the Contradictory of a Particular Compound Proposition
Author explains the procedure for deriving the contradictory of a particular compound proposition using an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the contradictory of a particular compound proposition by enumerating subject individuals and alternating the contradictories of the parts.

Core Idea
Contradictory of a particular compound is a universal disjunctive with alternated contradictories per individual.

In logic, we often need to find the opposite of a compound statement. This lesson presents a precise method for constructing the contradictory of a particular compound proposition, as taught in the Islamic logical tradition. Understanding this method is essential for advanced reasoning in fields like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original as a particular compound
Recognize that the proposition is both compound (conditional or disjunctive) and particular in quantity (e.g., 'Some animal is always man').
The original proposition is not explicitly given in the passage but serves as the starting point.
2
Recall that the contradictory of a particular is universal
The text states 'نقيض الجزئية هي الكلية' (the contradictory of the particular is the universal). This means the resulting proposition must be universal in quantity.
This is a standard conversion rule.
3
Enumerate all individuals of the subject
Posit every individual that falls under the subject term. For example, for 'animal', consider each individual animal.
This ensures the universal quantifier covers all cases.
4
For each individual, alternate between the contradictories of the parts
Take the contradictory of each part of the compound (e.g., the two parts of the disjunction) and apply them alternately to each individual. In the example, the parts are 'always man' and 'always not man'.
The alternation is necessary to maintain the contradictory relationship.
5
Combine into a universal disjunctive proposition
The result is a universal categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate. For the example, it is 'Every animal is either always man or always not man'.
This is the final contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التناقض. حيوان انسان دائماً وحينئذ فطريق اخذ نقيض المركبة الجزئية ان توضع افراد الموضوع كلها ضرورة ان نقيض الجزئية هي الكلية، ثم يردد بين نقيضي الجزئين بالنسبة الى كل واحد من تلك الأفراد ويقال فى المثال المذكور كل حيوان اما انسان دائماً او لیس بانسان دائماً و حينئذ فيصدق النقيض و هي قضية حملية مرددة المحمول فقوله الى كل فرد فرد من افراد الموضوع

So his saying: to each individual of the individuals of the subject.

Commentary
This passage defines the method for obtaining the contradictory of a particular compound proposition (المركبة الجزئية). The author first notes that the contradictory of a particular is a universal. The procedure is to consider each individual of the subject, and for each individual, you must take the contradictory of each part alternately. The example given is the universal disjunctive 'Every animal is either always man or always not man'—the contradictory of this would be a particular with alternating negations across individuals. The passage ends by describing the result as a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate. This method is foundational for handling compound propositions in logical reasoning.

Simple Conversion: Definition and Conditions
Author defines simple conversion with conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion, state its two conditions, and identify exceptions to the quality condition using the principle of induction.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps the two extremes; truth and quality are preserved in most, but not all, cases.

In this passage, the commentator defines simple conversion and discusses its two key conditions: preservation of truth and preservation of quality. However, you will see that the quality condition is not absolute; induction shows that in some cases, quality can differ while the conversion remains valid. Let's work through the definition and the exceptions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the Arabic phrase for simple conversion (العكس المستوى) and note that it involves swapping the two extremes (طرفى القضية) of a proposition.
The extremes can be subject/predicate or antecedent/consequent.
2
Understand the truth condition
The text states that 'preserving truth' means if the original is assumed true, then the converse must be true. It does not require both to be actually true.
This is a hypothetical condition: if original true, then converse true.
3
Understand the quality condition
The quality condition means that the conversion preserves the affirmative/negative quality of the original. But the author qualifies this: it is based on induction and holds 'in most matters'.
The author cites induction (الاستقراء) to show that quality is usually preserved.
4
Recognize the exception
The passage gives an example where quality is not preserved: when the predicate is more general than the subject, a particular negative like 'Some animal is not man' can convert to an affirmative like 'Every man is animal' or 'Some man is animal', which changes quality.
This shows that the quality condition is not universal; it has exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (العكس المستوى) as swapping the two extremes (subject and predicate, or antecedent and consequent) of a proposition. It then clarifies two conditions: preservation of truth and preservation of quality. The quality condition is that a true conversion usually preserves the original's quality (affirmative stays affirmative, negative stays negative). However, the author qualifies this using induction: in some cases, conversion can differ in quality and still hold true, e.g., when the predicate is more general than the subject. The example given is puzzling: 'Some animal is not man' seems to convert to 'Every man is animal' or 'Some man is animal', which differ in quality. The passage thus emphasizes that the quality condition is based on induction and has exceptions.

Conversion Rules for Affirmative and Negative Propositions
Author explains conversion rules for affirmatives and negatives, then refines the quality condition as redundant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for affirmative and universal negative propositions and explain why the quality condition is redundant.

Core Idea
Affirmatives convert to particulars; universal negatives convert to universals; quality preservation is implied by truth.

This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali's Hashiya clarifies the rules of conversion (al-'aks) for categorical propositions. It explains why affirmative propositions can only convert to particular affirmatives, why universal negatives convert to universal negatives, and then critically refines the commonly stated condition of preserving quality, showing it is redundant if truth is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion rule for affirmatives
Find the statement that says affirmative propositions only convert to particular affirmatives. Note that this applies to both universal and particular affirmatives.
The reason is that the predicate may be more general than the subject.
2
Identify the conversion rule for universal negatives
Find the statement that says the universal negative converts to a universal negative. The passage initially mentions 'universal negative converts to an affirmative universal' but the commentary corrects it to 'if it is negative, it is negative' meaning the quality stays the same.
So universal negatives convert to universal negatives, preserving both quality and quantity.
3
Analyze the refinement of the quality condition
The author argues that the condition 'preserve quality' is redundant because if the quality changed, the truth would not be preserved. He gives examples: converting 'Every human is a speaking being' to 'Some speaking being is not a human' fails to preserve truth.
The only essential condition is preservation of truth.

And the affirmative only converts to a particular affirmative because of the generality of the predicate and the consequent, and the universal negative converts to an affirmative universal? And if it is negative, it is negative. His statement 'And the affirmative only converts to a particular affirmative' means that the affirmative, whether universal like 'Every human is an animal' or particular like 'Some human is an animal', only converts to the particular affirmative, not to the universal affirmative. As for the truth of the particular affirmative, it is apparent: necessarily, if the predicate is true of what the subject is true of, wholly or partially, then the subject and predicate are true of this individual, so the subject is true of individuals of the predicate in general. And as for the lack of truth of the universal, it is because the predicate in the affirmative proposition may be more general than the subject; so if you convert the proposition, the subject becomes more general, and it is impossible for the more specific to be universally true of the more general. So the converse that must be true in all materials is the particular affirmative. This is the explanation for categoricals; analogize for conditionals. His statement—then his statement, may God correct him: 'the permissibility of generality of the predicate and the consequent' is an explanation of the negative part understood from the mentioned restriction; as for the affirmative, it is obvious. And the verification is that this condition, i.e., preservation of quality, is redundant, even though its mention is frequent in their books, because the condition of preservation of truth suffices for it, since it is apparent that if the quality differs, truth does not remain at all. Do you not see that 'Some speaking being is not a human' is not true in the conversion of our saying 'Every human is a speaking being' while it is true? And similarly, 'Some human is not an animal' is not true with the truth of our saying 'Some animal is a human'? And what appears of truth with difference in quality as in the previously mentioned example and its likes is not due to essence but to particular materials, and you have already known that the intended truth is truth in essence, not otherwise. Yes, if one said 'with preservation of quality and truth' as some did, it would have merit, for making the later suffice for the earlier is permissible, as previously indicated, unlike making the earlier suffice for the later, which is absolutely impermissible, so reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) He only presented the ruling for affirmatives before negatives due to the honor of affirmation and because conversion applies to both parts of it, unlike negatives, for conversion applies only to the universal of them, as will come, and because conversion in them is more apparent, since the two contracts of position and predication are realized in them, so if we make the contract of position a predication and vice versa, the concept of conversion is obtained with minimal effort, unlike negatives, due to the possibility of the absence of the contract of position in them. This is so. But their custom is to present negatives before affirmatives because among them there is what converts universally, and the universal, even if negative, is more honorable than the particular, even if affirmative, because it is more beneficial in the sciences and more precise. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage covers two main points: the conversion rules for propositions and a refinement of the quality condition. First, the author states that affirmative propositions (both universal and particular) convert only to particular affirmatives. He explains that if the predicate is true of the subject in the original, then in the converse the subject (original predicate) is true of at least some individuals of the original subject. However, the converse cannot be universal because the predicate may be more general than the subject, as in 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human', not 'Every animal is a human'. For negatives, the passage initially says 'the universal negative converts to an affirmative universal' and then adds 'and if it is negative, it is negative', which seems contradictory; but the commentary clarifies later that the correct rule is that universal negative propositions (e.g., 'No human is a stone') convert to universal negative propositions (e.g., 'No stone is a human'), preserving both quality and quantity. The commentary then verifies that the condition of quality is redundant because if the quality were different, the truth would not be preserved. For example, converting 'Every human is a speaking being' to 'Some speaking being is not a human' would not preserve truth (since the original is true but the converse false). Any apparent counterexample is due to particular materials, not the essence of conversion. The author suggests that if one says 'with preservation of quality and truth' it is acceptable, because the later (truth) can suffice for the earlier (quality) in a conditional sense, but not vice versa. Finally, there is a note on order: the author chose to present affirmatives first because conversion applies to both their universals and particulars, while negatives only have universal conversion; however, many scholars prefer negatives first because the universal (even if negative) is more beneficial in the sciences than the particular (even if affirmative).

Key Takeaway
Affirmatives convert to particular affirmatives; universal negatives convert to universal negatives; the condition of preserving quality is redundant because truth preservation already requires it.

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions to Particular Affirmative
Explains conversion rule for affirmative propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for affirmative propositions to determine the correct converse in categorical propositions from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Affirmative propositions always convert to particular affirmative, not universal.

The rule of conversion (al-‘aks) is a fundamental immediate inference in logic. For affirmative propositions, whether universal or particular, the valid converse is always a particular affirmative. This lesson unpacks why that is the case, using the example 'Every human is an animal' to illustrate the limitation. Understanding this rule prevents common errors when analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences such as Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original affirmative proposition
Take the given affirmative proposition, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' (universal) or 'Some human is an animal' (particular). Note its subject and predicate.
2
Form the potential converse
Swap the subject and predicate to form a new proposition. For 'Every human is an animal', the converse would be 'Every animal is a human' (universal) or 'Some animal is a human' (particular).
You must consider both universal and particular forms.
3
Test truth preservation
Check whether the converse is necessarily true when the original is true. In 'Every human is an animal', is 'Every animal is a human' always true? No, because not every animal is human. But 'Some animal is a human' is true, since some animals are humans.
Use a counterexample if needed: a dog is an animal but not human.
4
State the conversion rule for affirmatives
The valid conversion of any affirmative proposition is always a particular affirmative. This holds whether the original is universal or particular.
The predicate may be more general; universal converse would require the predicate to be coextensive, which is not guaranteed.
5
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Practice with a Fiqh example: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. The valid converse is 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant', not 'Every forbidden thing is an intoxicant'.
This prevents overgeneralization in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموجبة انما تنعكس جزئية. قوله « والموجبة انما تنعكس جزئية» يعنى ان الموجبة سواء كانت كلية نحو كل انسان حيوان أو جزئية نحو: بعض الانسان حيوان، انما تنعكس إلى الموجبة الجزئية لا الى الموجبة الكلية. اما صدق الموجبة الجزئية، فظاهر، ضرورة انه اذا صدق المحمول على ما صدق عليه الموضوع كلاً او بعضاً تصادق الموضوع والمحمول في هذا الفرد فيصدق الموضوع على افراد المحمول فى الجملة واما عدم صدق الكلية، فلان المحمول في القضية الموجبة قد يكون اعم من الموضوع فلو عكست القضية صار الموضوع اعم و يستحيل صدق الاخص كلياً على الاعم فالعكس اللازم الصدق في جميع المواد هو الموجبة الجزئية، هذا هو البيان فى الحمليات وقس عليه الحال في الشرطات
And the affirmative [proposition] converts only to a particular. [Then the commentator says:] His saying: 'And the affirmative converts only to a particular' means that the affirmative, whether it is universal like 'Every human is an animal' or particular like 'Some human is an animal', converts only to the particular affirmative, not to the universal affirmative. As for the truth of the particular affirmative, it is apparent, necessarily because when the predicate is true of what the subject is true of, either completely or partially, the subject and predicate coincide in this individual, so the subject is true of the individuals of the predicate in some respect. As for the lack of truth of the universal, it is because the predicate in the affirmative proposition may be more general than the subject. So if the proposition were converted, the subject would become more general, and it is impossible for the more specific to be universally true of the more general. Therefore the conversion necessarily true in all materials is the particular affirmative. This is the explanation in categoricals, and analogize to conditionals.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage explains the rule of conversion for affirmative propositions. The author first states the rule: an affirmative proposition converts only to a particular affirmative. Then the commentator expands, clarifying that this holds whether the original affirmative is universal or particular. The justification is that the predicate may be broader in scope (a‘amm) than the subject; if we try to convert to a universal affirmative, we would be making the original subject (which is more specific) universally true of the broader predicate, which fails. The necessary valid conversion is therefore particular affirmative. The commentator uses the example 'Every human is an animal' to illustrate: converting to 'Every animal is a human' is false, but 'Some animal is a human' is true. This rule applies to categorical propositions; the same logic applies to conditionals analogically.

Critique of the Condition of Preserving Quality in Conversion
Argues that the condition of preserving quality is redundant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the condition of preserving quality is redundant in conversion and distinguish between essential truth and accidental truth in propositional conversion.

Core Idea
Preserving truth in conversion automatically preserves quality; the quality condition is superfluous.

In classical Islamic logic, conversion ('aks) of categorical propositions is governed by conditions. Many logicians include the condition that the quality (affirmative or negative) must remain unchanged. This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali's hashya argues that this condition is redundant because the more fundamental condition—preserving truth—already guarantees it. He demonstrates with examples from logic textbooks, showing that if quality changes, truth is necessarily lost, except in accidental cases where truth appears preserved due to specific subject matter. This refinement tightens the conditions and deepens our understanding of essential versus accidental truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition under critique
The condition of preserving quality (بقاء الكيف) is commonly mentioned in logic books. Locate where the author states it is 'superfluous' (مستدرك). Notice that he does not reject it outright but argues it is not needed as a separate condition.
2
Understand the relationship between truth and quality
The author claims that if quality differs, truth does not remain at all. That is, a proposition and its converse must have the same truth value; if quality flips (e.g., affirmative to negative), the truth value cannot be identical. Follow the reasoning that preserving truth (بقاء الصدق) implies preserving quality.
This is based on the logical principle that changing quantity and quality together can change truth.
3
Analyze the first example
Consider the proposition 'Every human is a speaker' (كل إنسان ناطق). Its converse by conversion of the universal affirmative is 'Some speaker is a human', not 'Some speaker is not a human'. The latter would change quality to negative and is false. The author points out that this false converse would not preserve truth, so quality must remain to preserve truth.
The example illustrates that changing quality breaks truth, so preserving truth already requires quality preservation.
4
Analyze the second example
Another example: 'Some animal is a human' (بعض الحيوان إنسان) is true. Its converse 'Some human is an animal' is true and preserves quality (both affirmative). If we tried a converse with changed quality like 'Some human is not an animal' it would be false. This reinforces that truth and quality are linked.
5
Distinguish essential vs. accidental truth
The author notes that apparent cases of truth with quality change are due to particular materials (الخصوص المواد), not essential (من حيث الذات). For instance, some examples might seem to preserve truth despite quality change because the subject matter is peculiar. These are not genuine counterexamples because we consider only truth in respect of essence.
Essential truth is what matters for logical rules; accidental truth arises from specific content, not form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق ان هذا الشرط اى بقاء الكيف مستدرك و ان كثر ايراده في كتبهم، لان اشتراط بقاء الصدق يغنى عنه لظهور انه اذا اختلف الكيف لم يبق الصدق اصلاً. الاترى انه لا يصدق بعض الناطق ليس بانسان فى عكس قولنا كل انسان ناطق مع انه صادق و كذا لا يصدق بعض الانسان ليس يحيوان مع صدق قولنا : بعض الحيوان انسان؟ و ما يترأى من الصدق مع الاختلاف في الكيف كما في المثال المذكور سابقاً و امثاله فهو ليس من حيث الذات بل الخصوص المواد وقد عرفت آنفاً ان المراد من الصدق هو الصدق من حيث الذات لاغير. نعم لوقال: «مع بقاء الكيف و الصدق» كما فعله بعضهم لكان له وجه، فان اغناء المؤخر عن المقدم جايز كما مرت اليه الاشارة سابقاً بخلاف اغناء المقدم عن المؤخر فانه لا يجوز البتة فتأمل.
And the verification is that this condition, namely the preservation of quality, is superfluous, even though its mention is frequent in their books. Because the stipulation of preserving truth suffices for it, since it is obvious that if the quality differs, truth does not remain at all. Do you not see that 'Some speaker is not a human' is not true in the conversion of our statement 'Every human is a speaker' even though it is true, and likewise 'Some human is not an animal' is not true with the truth of our statement 'Some animal is a human'? And what appears as truth with difference in quality, as in the example mentioned previously and its likes, is not on account of essence but due to particular materials. And you have already known that what is meant by truth is truth in respect of essence, not otherwise. Yes, if he said: 'with preservation of quality and truth' as some have done, it would have a justification, for making the later suffice for the former is permissible, as previously indicated, contrary to making the former suffice for the latter, for it is absolutely not permissible. So reflect.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the hashya author (Mirza Muhammad Ali) on a commonly stated condition in conversion: that the quality (affirmative/negative) must be preserved. He argues that this condition is redundant because the condition of preserving truth already implies preservation of quality. If the quality changes, the truth is lost. He gives examples: converting 'Every human is a speaker' to 'Some speaker is not a human' would change quality to negative and is false, even though it might appear true in some specific subject matter, but that is accidental. The essential truth (sidq min hayth al-dhat) is what matters. He then notes that if one stipulates both conditions, it is acceptable because the later can suffice for the former, but not vice versa. This is a typical hashiya refinement: tightening the conditions.

Key Takeaway

Proof That Universal Negative Converts Universally
States universal negative converts universally with reductio proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the reductio proof that a universal negative proposition converts to a universal negative.

Core Idea
A universal negative must convert universally; otherwise a contradiction results.

In this lesson, we examine a fundamental rule in logic: the conversion of universal negative propositions. The author states that 'No S is P' converts to 'No P is S' and provides a clever reductio proof. We will walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and assume the converse is false
Start with a true universal negative, e.g., 'No intoxicant is halal'. Assume its converse 'No halal is an intoxicant' is false.
The assumption is made for reductio.
2
Derive the contradictory of the converse
If the converse is false, its contradictory 'Some halal is an intoxicant' must be true.
This is a standard logical move.
3
Combine with the original true premise
Take the original 'No intoxicant is halal' and the newly derived 'Some halal is an intoxicant' as premises.
Both are now assumed true.
4
Deduce a self-negation (contradiction)
From these premises, using a syllogism, we deduce 'Some halal is not halal', which is a self-negation and impossible.
This shows the assumption leads to absurdity.
5
Conclude that the converse must be true
Since the original is true and the deduction is valid, the false assumption (converse false) is rejected, so the converse is true.
Thus the universal negative converts universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنعكس كلية والالزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه و الجزئية لا تنعكس اصلاً الجواز عموم الموضوع او المقدم. قوله والالزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه: تقريره ان يقال: كلما صدق قولنا لا شيء من الانسان بحجر صدق قولنا لاشيء من الحجر بانسان والا لصدق نقيضه و هو بعض الحجر انسان فنضمه مع الاصل فنقول : بعض الحجر انسان ولاشيء من الانسان بحجر فينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر وهو سلب الشيء عن نفسه و هذا محال منشأه هو نقيض العكس لان الاصل صادق و الهيئة منتجة فيكون نقيض العكس باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً وهو المطلوب.
[The universal negative] converts universally, and the necessary consequence is the negation of a thing of itself. And the particular [negative] does not convert at all; [the condition is] the generality of the subject or the antecedent. His saying: 'and the necessary consequence is the negation of a thing of itself' – its exposition is that one says: Whenever our statement 'No human is a stone' is true, our statement 'No stone is a human' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'Some stone is a human'. Then we combine it with the original and say: 'Some stone is a human and no human is a stone', which yields 'Some stone is not a stone'. That is the negation of a thing of itself, and this is impossible. Its source is the contradictory of the converse, because the original is true and the form is productive. Thus the contradictory of the converse is false, so the converse is true, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage first states the rule: a universal negative proposition converts to a universal negative, and the necessary consequence of that conversion is that if it were false, it would lead to 'negation of a thing of itself' (salb al-shay’ ‘an nafsihi), i.e., a self-contradiction. The commentator then provides a formal reductio proof. Take the universal negative 'No human is a stone'. Assume its converse 'No stone is a human' is false. Then its contradictory, 'Some stone is a human', must be true. Combine this with the original premise: 'Some stone is a human' and 'No human is a stone'. From these two, we can deduce (by a syllogism in the first figure or by simple inference) that 'Some stone is not a stone', which is a contradiction. Therefore the assumption that the converse is false leads to impossibility, so the converse must be true. This proof relies on the principle that if a true premise and a productive syllogistic form yield a false conclusion, the assumption is false. Here the original premise is true, and the syllogistic form yields a contradiction, so the contradictory of the converse is false, hence the converse is true.

Key Terms
السالبة الكلية
سلب الشيء عن نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The conclusion 'Some intoxicant is not an intoxicant'

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a universal negative is proved by reductio: if the converse were false, a self-contradiction follows, so the converse must be true.

Proof of Conversion by Contradiction
Proof of conversion by reductio ad absurdum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of conversion by contradiction for universal negative propositions and identify the self-negation fallacy.

Core Idea
Conversion is proven by the impossibility of a thing being negated of itself.

In logic, conversion (al-aks) allows us to swap the subject and predicate of a true proposition while preserving truth under certain conditions. But how do we know that conversion is valid? The author provides a rigorous proof: if a universal negative is true, its converse must also be true; otherwise, we would be forced to accept a self-contradiction. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule for universal negatives
If 'No J is B' is true, then 'No B is J' must also be true.
2
Assume the converse is false
Suppose 'No B is J' is false, then its contradictory 'Some B is J' is true.
3
Combine with the original premise
Use the original 'No J is B' and 'Some B is J' to form a syllogism.
4
Derive the self-negation
The syllogism yields 'Some B is not B', which is impossible.
5
Conclude the converse is true
Since the contradiction arises from denying the converse, the converse must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والالزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه: تقريره (۱۰) ان يقال: كلما صدق :قولنا لا شيء من الانسان بحجر صدق :قولنا : لاشيء من الحجر بانسان والا لصدق نقیضه (۱۱) و هو بعض الحجر انسان فنضمه مع الاصل (۱۲) فنقول : بعض الحجر انسان ولاشيء من الانسان بحجر فينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر وهو سلب الشيء عن نفسه (۱۳) و هذا محال (۱۴) منشأه هو نقيض العكس لان الاصل صادق (۱۵) و الهيئة منتجة فيكون نقيض العكس باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً وهو المطلوب. قوله «عموم الموضوع» و حينئذ يصح سلب الاخص عن بعض الاعم لكن لا يصح سلب الاعم عن بعض الاخص، مثلاً يصدق: بعض الحيوان ليس بانسان ولا يصدق بعض الانسان ليس بحيوان. قوله «او المقدم» مثلاً يصدق قد لا يكون اذا كان الشيء حيواناً كان انساناً ولا يصدق قد لا يكون اذا كان الشيء انساناً كان حيواناً.
His saying: 'And the necessary consequence is the negation of a thing of itself.' Its explanation: it is said: Whenever our statement 'No human is a stone' is true, our statement 'No stone is a human' is true. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, and it is 'Some stone is a human.' We combine it with the original premise and say: 'Some stone is a human, and no human is a stone.' This yields 'Some stone is not a stone,' which is the negation of a thing of itself. This is impossible. Its source is the contradictory of the converse, because the original premise is true and the syllogistic form is productive. Thus the contradictory of the converse is false, so the converse is true, which is desired. His saying 'generality of the subject': then it is valid to negate the more specific from some of the more general, but it is not valid to negate the more general from some of the more specific. For example, 'Some animal is not human' is true, but 'Some human is not animal' is not true. His saying 'or the antecedent': for example, 'Sometimes it is not the case that if something is an animal, it is a human' is true, but 'Sometimes it is not the case that if something is a human, it is an animal' is not true.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage expands on the rule of conversion (aks) and the necessary consequence that denying conversion leads to self-negation. The commentator explains the proof: if a universal negative 'No J is B' is true, then its converse 'No B is J' must also be true. Assuming the converse is false leads to its contradictory 'Some B is J', which combined with the original yields 'Some B is not B' – a contradiction. Thus the converse is true. The author then clarifies two exceptions: (1) conversion does not hold when the subject is more general than the predicate (e.g., 'Some animal is not human' is true, but its converse is not), and (2) for conditional propositions, the antecedent and consequent may not interchange (e.g., 'Not every animal is human' is true, but its converse is not). These refinements prevent overextension of the rule.

Key Terms
العكس
سلب الشيء عن نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof of conversion, what is the impossible conclusion that arises if we deny the converse?
A) Some stone is a human
B) Some stone is not a stone
C) No stone is a human
D) Some human is a stone

Key Takeaway
Conversion for universal negatives is proven by reductio: denying the converse leads to a contradiction, so the converse must be true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Prove that if 'No khamr is halal' is true, then 'No halal is khamr' must also be true using the self-negation method.
?
Check the validity: 'Every obligation is mandatory' converts to 'Some mandatory is obligation' (by limitation). Is that correct according to conversion rules?

Scope Restrictions on Conversion: Generality of Subject and Antecedent
Restrictions on conversion due to generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when conversion fails due to generality of the subject or antecedent in categorical and conditional propositions.

Core Idea
Conversion fails when the subject is more general than the predicate or when the antecedent is more general than the consequent.

Conversion is not always valid. The author introduces two important restrictions: the subject must not be more general than the predicate in categorical propositions, and similarly for conditionals, the antecedent must not be more general than the consequent. This lesson clarifies these exceptions with examples from everyday logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the generality condition
For a proposition 'Some A is not B', if A is more general than B, conversion may fail.
2
Examine the categorical example
'Some animal is not human' is true; its converse 'Some human is not animal' is false because human is a subset of animal.
3
Examine the conditional example
'Not every animal is human' (some animals are not human) is true; 'Not every human is animal' is false.
4
Generalize the rule
Conversion preserves truth only when the subject/predicate and antecedent/consequent have the same scope, or when moving from more specific to more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والالزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه: تقريره (۱۰) ان يقال: كلما صدق :قولنا لا شيء من الانسان بحجر صدق :قولنا : لاشيء من الحجر بانسان والا لصدق نقیضه (۱۱) و هو بعض الحجر انسان فنضمه مع الاصل (۱۲) فنقول : بعض الحجر انسان ولاشيء من الانسان بحجر فينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر وهو سلب الشيء عن نفسه (۱۳) و هذا محال (۱۴) منشأه هو نقيض العكس لان الاصل صادق (۱۵) و الهيئة منتجة فيكون نقيض العكس باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً وهو المطلوب. قوله «عموم الموضوع» و حينئذ يصح سلب الاخص عن بعض الاعم لكن لا يصح سلب الاعم عن بعض الاخص، مثلاً يصدق: بعض الحيوان ليس بانسان ولا يصدق بعض الانسان ليس بحيوان. قوله «او المقدم» مثلاً يصدق قد لا يكون اذا كان الشيء حيواناً كان انساناً ولا يصدق قد لا يكون اذا كان الشيء انساناً كان حيواناً.
His saying: 'And the necessary consequence is the negation of a thing of itself.' Its explanation: it is said: Whenever our statement 'No human is a stone' is true, our statement 'No stone is a human' is true. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, and it is 'Some stone is a human.' We combine it with the original premise and say: 'Some stone is a human, and no human is a stone.' This yields 'Some stone is not a stone,' which is the negation of a thing of itself. This is impossible. Its source is the contradictory of the converse, because the original premise is true and the syllogistic form is productive. Thus the contradictory of the converse is false, so the converse is true, which is desired. His saying 'generality of the subject': then it is valid to negate the more specific from some of the more general, but it is not valid to negate the more general from some of the more specific. For example, 'Some animal is not human' is true, but 'Some human is not animal' is not true. His saying 'or the antecedent': for example, 'Sometimes it is not the case that if something is an animal, it is a human' is true, but 'Sometimes it is not the case that if something is a human, it is an animal' is not true.

Commentary
This passage expands on the rule of conversion (aks) and the necessary consequence that denying conversion leads to self-negation. The commentator explains the proof: if a universal negative 'No J is B' is true, then its converse 'No B is J' must also be true. Assuming the converse is false leads to its contradictory 'Some B is J', which combined with the original yields 'Some B is not B' – a contradiction. Thus the converse is true. These refinements prevent overextension of the rule.

Key Takeaway
Conversion is invalid when the subject is more general than the predicate, or when the antecedent is more general than the consequent, as demonstrated by examples.

Conversion of Modal Universal Affirmatives (al-Da'imatan)
Author explains conversion rule for necessary and perpetual modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a necessary or perpetual universal affirmative modal proposition into its valid converse and explain the proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
A necessary or perpetual universal affirmative converts to a particular actual temporal proposition, not a modal one.

This lesson examines how conversion (al-aks) works for modal propositions, specifically the two 'perpetual' types: necessary (daruriyya) and perpetual (da'ima). The author, building on earlier discussions of conversion by quantity and quality, now turns to modality. The key insight is that the converse loses the modal force—it becomes an actual particular proposition with a temporal condition (hiniyya mutlaqa). The proof uses reductio ad absurdum: assuming the converse is false leads to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The original proposition is a universal affirmative with modal necessity or perpetuity. For example, 'Necessarily every human is an animal' or 'Perpetually every human is an animal'.
The two types are together called al-da'imatan.
2
State the conversion rule
The converse is a particular affirmative with actuality (bi al-fi'l) and a temporal condition: 'Some animal is actually human at the time it is an animal.' The modal force does not transfer to the converse.
The converse is not modal; it is a temporal absolute proposition.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory is true: a perpetual universal negative: 'Perpetually no animal is human as long as it is animal.' Combine this with the original universal affirmative: 'Every human is an animal.' A valid categorical syllogism yields 'Necessarily or perpetually no human is a human,' which is impossible.
This contradiction shows the converse must be true.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the necessary universal proposition 'Necessarily every living being is created.' Its valid converse is 'Some created being is actually a living being at the time it is created.'
This example is commonly used in Kalam discussions of temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «واما بحسب الجهة »:يعنى ان ما ذكرناه هو بيان انعكاس القضاياء بحسب الكم والكيف واما بحسب الجهة الخ. قوله «الدائمنان» اى الضرورية والدائمة مثلا كلما صدق قولنا بالضرورة او دائماً كل انسان حیوان صدق :قولنا بعض الحيوان انسان بالفعل حين هو حيوان و الا فيصدق نقيضه وهو دائماً لاشيء من الحيوان بانسان مادام حيواناً فهو مع الاصل (١٦) ينتج: لاشيء من الانسان بانسان بالضرورة او دائماً هف.
His saying 'And as for modality': meaning that what we have mentioned is an explanation of the conversion of propositions according to quantity and quality. As for modality, etc. His saying 'the two perpetuals' i.e., the necessary and the perpetual. For example, whenever our statement 'Necessarily or perpetually every human is an animal' is true, our statement 'Some animal is actually human at the time it is an animal' is true. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, which is 'Perpetually no animal is a human as long as it is an animal.' Then combining it with the original yields 'Necessarily or perpetually no human is a human.' That leads to absurdity.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage addresses conversion by modality (al-jihat). After explaining conversion by quantity and quality, the author turns to modal propositions. The two 'daima' types (necessary and perpetual universal affirmatives) convert to an actual particular affirmative with a temporal condition (bi al-fi'l hina huwa kadha). The proof: if the converse were false, its contradictory (a universal negative perpetual) would be true. Combining that with the original universal affirmative yields a universal negative of the predicate of itself, which is impossible. For example, 'Necessarily every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is actually human at the time it is an animal.' This shows how modal force affects conversion.

Key Terms
الدائمتان
الضرورية
الدائمة
الحينية المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessarily some created is a living being.
B) Some created is actually a living being at the time it is created.
C) Perpetually some living being is created.
D) Some living being is necessarily created.

Key Takeaway
Necessary and perpetual universal affirmatives convert to actual particular affirmatives with a temporal condition, not to modal propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
