Conversion of General Conditional Propositions
Author explains conversion of general conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a general conditional proposition (mashruta 'amma or 'urfiyya 'amma) to its valid converse and verify the conversion using the method of contradiction.

Core Idea
General conditionals convert like perpetuals: universal affirmative becomes particular absolute temporal.

In this lesson, we examine how the common and conventional conditional propositions (mashruta 'amma and 'urfiyya 'amma) are converted. These are universal affirmative propositions with a condition that the predicate holds as long as the subject exists. The conversion rule mirrors that of perpetuals: a universal affirmative converts to a particular absolute temporal affirmative. The author provides a logical proof by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two general conditionals
Recognize that both mashruta 'amma and 'urfiyya 'amma are universal affirmatives with a durational condition (as long as the subject exists).
2
State the conversion rule
The rule: a universal affirmative general conditional converts to a particular absolute temporal affirmative. The converse asserts actual existence at the time the subject description holds.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
The author shows that if the converse were false, its contradictory (a universal negative perpetual) would be true. Combining that with the original leads to a contradiction (no writer is a writer while writing). Therefore, the converse must be true.
4
Apply the rule to a new example
Practice with an Islamic example: 'Every fasting person avoids food as long as he is fasting' converts to 'Some food avoider is actually fasting at the time he avoids food.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «والعامتان» اى المشروطة العامة و العرفية العامة ، مثلاً اذا صدق بالضروره او بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً، صدق بعض متحرك الاصابع كاتب بالفعل حين هو متحرك الاصابع والا فيصدق نقيضه وهو دائماً لا شيء من متحرك الاصابع بكاتب مادام متحرك الاصابع و هو مع الاصل ينتج قولنا: بالضرورة او بالدوام لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب مادام كاتباً هف.
His saying 'and the two general conditionals' i.e., the common conditional (mashruta 'amma) and the conventional conditional ('urfiyya 'amma). For example, if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer', then it is true that 'Some mover of fingers is actually a writer at the time he moves his fingers'. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, which is 'Perpetually nothing that moves its fingers is a writer as long as it moves its fingers'. Combining it with the original yields 'Necessarily or perpetually no writer is a writer as long as he is a writer', which is absurd.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage covers the conversion of the two 'amma' (general) conditional propositions: the common conditional (mashruta 'amma) and the conventional conditional ('urfiyya 'amma). Both are universal affirmatives with a condition that the predicate holds as long as the subject exists. The conversion rule is similar to the perpetuals: the universal affirmative converts to a particular absolute temporal affirmative. The proof uses contradiction: assuming the converse false gives a universal negative perpetual, which combined with the original yields a self-contradiction. An example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' converts to 'Some mover of fingers is actually a writer at the time he moves his fingers.'

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
العرفية العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) No fasting person avoids food perpetually.
D) Some food avoider is necessarily fasting.

Key Takeaway
General conditional propositions (common and conventional) convert to particular absolute temporal affirmatives, proven by contradiction.

Conversion of Special Conditionals to Absolute General with Non-Permanence
Author proves that special conditionals convert to absolute general with non-permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a special conditional proposition to its correct converse and verify the non-permanence condition using the double reductio proof.

Core Idea
Special conditionals convert to absolute general only when non-permanence is added.

In Islamic logic, propositions with temporal conditions require precise conversion. The special conditional (mashruta khassa) says the predicate holds as long as the subject description applies, but not perpetually. Its converse is not simply an absolute general; it must include the condition of non-permanence (la dawam) to avoid contradictions. This lesson walks through the proof using a double reductio.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special conditionals
Recognize that 'mashruta khassa' and ''urfiyya khassa' are universal affirmatives with a condition: predicate holds as long as the subject description applies, but not always.
These are modal propositions.
2
Understand the conversion claim
The author states that these convert to absolute general propositions (mutlaqa 'amma) but with an added non-permanence condition. Non-permanence means the predicate does not hold perpetually in the converse.
The converse is an absolute temporal (hiniyya mutlaqa) with non-permanence.
3
Trace the first part of the proof
The special implies the general (if a predicate holds under a condition, it holds at least sometimes). The general converts to an absolute temporal as previously established. So the converse has the form of an absolute temporal.
This covers the 'hiniyya mutlaqa' part.
4
Follow the double reductio for non-permanence
Assume the non-permanence condition is false, meaning the contradictory (permanent) is true. Combine this with the original parts to derive two contradictory results. This shows the assumption is false, so non-permanence must be true.
The example uses 'writer' and 'mover of fingers'.
5
Apply the example
The original: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually' converts to 'Some mover of fingers is actually a writer at the time he moves his fingers, but not perpetually.' Notice the non-permanence: 'not perpetually'.
Practice with other examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « والخاصتان»: اى المشروطة الخاصة و العرفية الخاصة (۱۷) تنعكسان في العكس المستوى والمطلقة العامة، مطلقة عامة. ولا عكس للممكنتين و من السوالب VV الى حينية مطلقة مقيدة باللادوام، اما انعكاسهما الى الحينية المطلقة فلانه كلما صدقت الخاصتان صدقت العامتان (۱۸) و قدمر انه كلما صدقت العامتان صدقت في عكسهما الحينية المطلقة واما اللادوام فبيان صدقه انه أو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه و نضم هذا النقيض الى الجزء الاول من الاصل فينتج نتيجة و نضمه الى الجزء الثاني من الاصل فينتج ما ينافي تلك النتيجة، مثلاً كلما صدق بالضرورة او بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً صدق فى العكس بعض متحرك الاصابع كاتب بالفعل حين هو متحرك الاصابع لا دائماً، اما صدق الجزء الاول فقد ظهر مما سبق و اما صدق الجزء الثاني - اي : اللادوام ومعناه: ليس بعض متحرك الاصابع كاتباً بالفعل - فلانه لو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه و هو قولنا كل متحرك الاصابع كاتب دائماً، فنضمه الى الجزء الاول (۱۹) من الأصل فنقول : كل متحرك الاصابع كاتب دائماً وكل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً ينتج كل متحرك الاصابع متحرك الاصابع : دائماً ثم نضمه الى الجزء الثانى من الاصل ونقول : كل متحرك الاصابع كاتب دائماً ولاشيء من الكاتب يمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل ينتج: لاشيء من متحرك الاصابع بمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل و هذا ينافى النتيجة السابقة فيلزم من صدق نقيض لا دوام العكس اجتماع المتنافيين فيكون باطلاً فيكون لا دوام العكس حقاً وهو المطلوب.
His saying 'and the two specials' i.e., the common special (mashruta khassa) and the conventional special ('urfiyya khassa). They convert, in the simple conversion, to an absolute general proposition. There is no conversion for the two possibles. And from the negatives, to an absolute temporal proposition restricted by non-permanence. As for their conversion to the absolute temporal, it is because whenever the two specials are true, the two generals are true, and it has passed that whenever the two generals are true, their converse is the absolute temporal. As for non-permanence, its truth is explained: if it were not true, then its contradictory would be true, and we combine this contradictory with the first part of the original, yielding a result, and combine it with the second part of the original, yielding something that contradicts that result. For example, whenever it is true by necessity or perpetuity that 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually', then in the converse it is true that 'Some mover of fingers is actually a writer at the time he moves his fingers, but not perpetually.' The truth of the first part has appeared from what preceded. As for the truth of the second part, i.e., non-permanence, meaning that 'It is not the case that some mover of fingers is actually a writer', because if it were not true, its contradictory would be true, namely 'Every mover of fingers is a writer perpetually.' We combine it with the first part of the original and say: 'Every mover of fingers is a writer perpetually, and every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer.' This yields 'Every mover of fingers moves his fingers perpetually.' Then we combine it with the second part of the original and say: 'Every mover of fingers is a writer perpetually, and no writer is a mover of fingers actually.' This yields 'No mover of fingers is a mover of fingers actually.' This contradicts the previous result. So from the truth of the contradictory of the non-permanence of the converse, the conjunction of two contradictories follows, so it is false, so the non-permanence of the converse is true, which is desired.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage deals with the conversion of the two special conditional propositions (mashruta khassa and 'urfiyya khassa). These are universal affirmatives with a condition that the predicate holds as long as the subject description applies, but not perpetually. They convert to an absolute general proposition (mutlaqa 'amma) with an added condition of non-permanence (la dawam). The proof is detailed: first, the specials imply the generals (since if a predicate holds under a condition, it holds at least sometimes). The generals convert to absolute temporal propositions as shown earlier. The non-permanence is proven by a double reductio: assuming non-permanence false leads to two contradictory conclusions. The example uses 'writer' and 'mover of fingers': the original 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually' converts to 'Some mover of fingers is actually a writer at the time he moves his fingers, but not perpetually.' The detailed syllogistic proof ensures the correct modal force is preserved.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashruta al-khassa): Special conditional: a proposition where the predicate holds for the subject as long as the subject description applies, but not outside that temporal scope.
العرفية الخاصة (al-'urfiyya al-khassa): Conventional special: similar to special conditional but with a conventionally understood non-permanent duration.
اللادوام (al-la-dawam): Non-permanence: the condition that the predicate does not hold perpetually for the subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Some knowledgeable person is a scholar necessarily.
D) No scholar is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a special conditional requires adding the non-permanence condition to maintain truth and avoid contradictions.

The Unified Conversion Rule for Five Modal Universals
Author states that five types of absolute propositions all convert to absolute general propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert any of the five modal universal affirmatives into a particular absolute proposition using the reduction-to-absurdity proof.

Core Idea
Five distinct modal universals all convert to one simple particular affirmative.

We have seen that modal propositions carry different modalities like necessity, perpetuity, and conditionality. This passage reveals a surprising simplification: all five types of universal affirmative modal propositions convert to the same kind—a simple particular affirmative without modal qualifier. This unifies the conversion rules for these modalities and streamlines immediate inference in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five modal types
From the context of the lesson, the five propositions are the modal universal affirmatives: necessary perpetual (ḍarūriyya), absolute general (mutlaqa ʿāmma? no, that's the converse), common conditional (ʿāmma sharṭiyya), conventional conditional (khāṣṣa sharṭiyya), and temporal/mixed? The passage does not list them explicitly but refers to 'these five' from the previous discussion. Recognize that they share a universal affirmative form with different modal qualifiers.
The exact identification is not crucial; the rule applies to any universal affirmative with one of the five modalities.
2
State the conversion claim
The claim is that each of these five universals converts to a particular absolute (mutlaqa ʿāmma), i.e., a simple particular affirmative without modal condition, meaning 'actually' (bi al-fiʿl). For example, 'Necessarily every J is B' converts to 'Some B is actually J'.
This is a single rule covering all five modalities.
3
Understand the proof strategy
The proof is by contradiction. Assume the converse (some B is actually J) is false. Then its contradictory (no B is J perpetually) is true. Combine this with the original universal (every J is B with a modality) using a syllogism: if every J is B and no B is J, then no J is J, which is absurd. Therefore the assumption is false, so the converse must be true.
The proof works regardless of the specific modality of the original, as long as it implies 'every J is B' in actuality.
4
Apply the rule
Given any universal affirmative proposition with one of the five modalities, you can immediately assert the particular absolute as its valid converse. No need to retain the modality in the converse.
This simplifies conversion tables in logic.

Combining it with the original yields 'No J is J', which leads to absurdity.

They all convert to a single type: absolute general particular (mutlaqa 'amma), i.e., a particular affirmative without modal qualifier beyond actuality. The proof is generic: if the universal affirmative with any of these modalities is true, then assuming the converse (a particular absolute) is false leads to a universal negative perpetual, which contradicts the original by producing a self-negation. No Conversion for Possibles? Al-Farabi vs. Ibn Sina
Author declares no conversion for possibles, contrasts Farabi and Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Farabi's and Ibn Sina's interpretations of possible propositions and determine whether conversion holds under each.

Core Idea
Conversion of modal propositions depends on the ontological status of the subject – possible or actual.

The passage presents a classic disagreement in Islamic logic: do possible propositions (al-mumkinatan) convert? The answer depends on how we understand the subject's existence. Al-Farabi takes the subject as possible, Ibn Sina as actual. This difference yields opposite results for conversion. Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base claim
The author begins by quoting the base text: 'And there is no conversion for the two possibles.' This is the doctrine being examined.
2
Understand the underlying interpretative principle
The truth of a proposition's predicate about its subject in the sciences is understood differently: al-Farabi takes the subject's existence as possible (bi al-imkan), while Ibn Sina takes it as actual (bi al-fi'l).
This is the key to the whole disagreement.
3
Analyze al-Farabi's view
For al-Farabi, 'Every J is B possibly' means: whenever J is possibly true of something, B is possibly true of it. This leads to conversion: some possible B is possible J.
Conversion seems to hold under this interpretation.
4
Analyze Ibn Sina's view
For Ibn Sina, 'Every J is B possibly' means: everything of which J is actually true, B is possibly true of it. The subject is actual, predicate possible. Conversion would be different here and likely fails.
The text breaks off, but the implication is that conversion does not hold under Ibn Sina's view.
5
Draw the conclusion
Thus, the rule 'no conversion for possibles' is true under Ibn Sina's interpretation but not under al-Farabi's. The author sides with Ibn Sina in the base text.
This shows how logical rules are tied to ontological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the meaning of 'Every J is B possibly' in al-Farabi's view is that whenever J is possibly true of something, B is possibly true of it. This would entail conversion then, namely that some of what B is possibly true of, J is possibly true of. In the Chief's view, the meaning of 'Every J is B possibly' is that everything of which J is actually true, B is possibly true of. Its conversion according to the Chief's method is: ...

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial case of possible (mumkina) propositions. The author notes that in the sciences, the truth of a proposition's predicate about its subject is understood differently: al-Farabi takes possibility as the mode for both subject and predicate (subject's existence is possible), while Ibn Sina takes the subject as actually existing. Under al-Farabi's interpretation, the possible universal would allow conversion (some possible B is possible J). But under Ibn Sina's interpretation (subject actual, predicate possible), conversion is not straightforward and may fail. The text breaks off before completing the explanation. This highlights how the conversion rule depends on the ontological commitment of the modal interpretation.

Key Terms
الممكنتين (al-mumkinatan): The two possible propositions: possible universal affirmative and possible particular affirmative? Or two types?
بالامكان (bi al-imkan): By possibility: the modal force of contingency, neither necessary nor impossible.
بالفعل (bi al-fi'l): actual: the subject is considered existent in actuality when the modality is possible; used by Ibn Sina.

Conversion Rules for Modulated Categorical Propositions
Author explains conversion of absolute, possible, eternal, general, and specific propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for absolute general, possible, eternal, general, and specific propositions, and prove the conversion for specific propositions using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Not all propositions convert equally: possibility propositions have no valid converse in Ibn Sina's system.

In this lesson, we dive into the rules of simple conversion for various types of modulated categorical propositions. The author explains how absolute general, eternal, general, and specific propositions convert, while possibility propositions (according to Ibn Sina) do not. A proof by reductio is provided for the specific case. Mastering these rules is essential for valid immediate inferences in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Work through the conversion of absolute general
Take the author's claim: 'An absolute general converts to an absolute general.' Test it with the example: 'Some writer is a finger-mover actually' converts to 'Some finger-mover is a writer actually'. Verify that if the original is true, the converse must be true (by definition of particular propositions).
This is straightforward and serves as the baseline.
2
Examine the impossibility of converting possibility propositions
According to Ibn Sina, 'Every donkey is possibly a mount of Zayd' does NOT convert to 'Some mount of Zayd is possibly a donkey' because Zayd's actual mount is a horse, so the converse is false. The author contrasts this with al-Farabi’s view.
The key is that possibility is predicated on actual existence of the subject.
3
Apply conversion to eternal propositions
For 'No man is a stone necessarily', the conversion is 'No stone is a man necessarily'. Use the author's proof: assume the negation 'some stone is a man actually' and combine with the original to derive a contradiction (some stone is not a stone).
Eternal propositions preserve modality in conversion.
4
Convert general propositions
For 'No writer is stationary-fingered as long as he is a writer', the converse is 'No stationary-fingered is a writer as long as he is stationary-fingered'. The author shows that if false, an absurdity follows.
General propositions convert to conventional general.
5
Convert specific propositions and understand the non-permanence condition
For 'No writer is stationary-fingered as long as he is a writer, not always', the converse is 'No stationary-fingered is a writer as long as he is stationary-fingered, not always in some part' (i.e., some stationary-fingered is actually a writer). The author proves this by reductio, combining two derived propositions to get a contradiction.
The non-permanence condition 'not always' is crucial.

Source Text

قوله « والمطلقة العامة مطلقة عامة»: اى هذه القضايا الخمس تنعكس كل واحدة منها الى مطلقة عامة فيقال : لو صدق كل ج ، ب باحدى الجهات الخمس لصدق بعض ب ، ج بالفعل و الا لصدق نقيضه و هو: لاشيء من ب ، ج دائماً و هو مع الاصل ينتج : لاشيء من ج ، ج هف.

قوله تنعكس الدائمتان دائمة مطلقة»: أي: الضرورية المطلقة الدائمة المطلقة و تنعكسان دائمة مطلقة مثلا اذا صدق قولنا : لاشيء من الانسان بحجر بالضرورة او بالدوام صدق: لاشيء من الحجر بانسان دائماً والا لصدق نقيضه و هو: بعض الحجر انسان بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر هف.
قوله «والعامتان» اى المشروطة العامة و العرفية العامة تنعكسان عرفية عامة مثلا اذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لاشيء الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً صدق بالدوام لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب مادام ساكن الاصابع والا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا : بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الاصابع بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج: بعض ساكن الاصابع ليس بساكن الاصابع بالفعل حين هو ساكن الاصابع هف.
قوله « والخاصتان عرفية»: اى المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان الى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام فى البعض وهو اشارة الى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية فنقول : اذا صدق بالضرورة او بالدوام لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً صدق: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب مادام ساكنا لا دائما في البعض اى بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب بالفعل اما الجزء الاول، فقد مربيانه من انه لازم للعامتين وهما لازمتان للخاصتين ولازم اللازم لازم و اما الجزء الثانى فلانه لو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه وهو: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً و هذا مع لادوام الاصل و هو: ان كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ينتج: لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب (دائماً خ ل بالفعل هف. و انما لم يلزم اللادوام فى الكل، لانه قد يكذب في مثالنا هذا كل ساكن اصابع كاتب بالفعل
Regarding the simple conversion and the absolute general, it converts to an absolute general. And there is no conversion for the two possibility propositions. And from the negatives to a temporary absolute restricted by non-permanence. As for their conversion to the absolute temporary: because whenever the two specific propositions are true, the two general propositions are true, and it has been stated that whenever the two general propositions are true, then in their conversion the absolute temporary is true. As for the non-permanence, the proof of its truth is that if it were not true, its negation would be true, and we combine this negation with the first part of the original, producing a result, and we combine it with the second part of the original, producing something that contradicts that result. For example, whenever it is true necessarily or permanently that 'every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not always', then in conversion it is true that 'some finger-mover is actually a writer when he is a finger-mover, not always'. As for the truth of the first part, it has become clear from what preceded. As for the truth of the second part, i.e., the non-permanence, meaning 'not some finger-mover is actually a writer' — because if it were not true, its negation would be true, which is our statement 'every finger-mover is always a writer'. So we combine it with the first part of the original and say: 'Every finger-mover is always a writer and every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer', producing 'every finger-mover is a finger-mover always'. Then we combine it with the second part of the original and say: 'Every finger-mover is always a writer and nothing that is a writer is actually a finger-mover', producing 'nothing that is a finger-mover is actually a finger-mover'. This contradicts the previous result. Therefore, from the truth of the negation of the non-permanence of the conversion, the combination of two contradictories would follow, so it is false, hence the non-permanence of the conversion is true, which is what was desired.
His statement: 'And the absolute general converts to an absolute general': i.e., these five propositions each convert to an absolute general. So it is said: if 'every J is B' is true with one of the five modes, then 'some B is actually J' is true, otherwise its negation would be true, namely 'nothing of B is J always', and this together with the original produces 'nothing of J is J' (absurd).
His statement: 'And there is no conversion for the two possibility propositions': Know that the truth of the predicate's description of the subject in propositions considered in the sciences is by possibility according to al-Farabi and by actuality according to the Shaykh (Ibn Sina). So the meaning of 'every J is B possibly' according to al-Farabi is that whenever J is possibly true of something, B is possibly true of it, and its conversion follows then, namely that some of that of which B is possibly true, J is possibly true of it. According to the Shaykh, the meaning of 'every J is B possibly' is: everything of which J is actually true, B is possibly true of it, and its conversion according to the Shaykh's method is: some of that of which B is actually true, J is possibly true of it. There is no doubt that the truth of the original does not necessitate the truth of the converse. For example, if we suppose that Zayd's actual mount is restricted to the horse, then 'every donkey is actually a possible mount of Zayd' is true, but its converse, namely 'some actual mount of Zayd is a donkey possibly', is not true. So the author, having chosen the Shaykh's doctrine because it is what is predominant in custom and language, ruled that there is no conversion for the two possibility propositions.
His statement: 'The two eternal propositions convert to an eternal absolute': i.e., the necessary absolute and the eternal absolute convert to an eternal absolute. For example, if our statement 'nothing of man is a stone necessarily or permanently' is true, then 'nothing of stone is a man always' is true; otherwise its negation would be true, namely 'some stone is actually a man', and this together with the original produces 'some stone is not a stone' (absurd).
His statement: 'And the two general propositions': i.e., the conditional general and the conventional general convert to a conventional general. For example, if it is true necessarily or permanently that 'nothing of a writer is stationary-fingered as long as he is a writer', then it is true permanently that 'nothing of stationary-fingered is a writer as long as he is stationary-fingered'; otherwise its negation would be true, namely our statement 'some stationary-fingered is actually a writer when he is stationary-fingered', and this together with the original produces 'some stationary-fingered is not actually stationary-fingered when he is stationary-fingered' (absurd).
His statement: 'And the two specific propositions convert to a conventional general': i.e., the conditional specific and the conventional specific convert to a conventional general negative universal restricted by non-permanence in some part, which refers to an absolute general affirmative particular. So we say: if it is true necessarily or permanently that 'nothing of a writer is stationary-fingered as long as he is a writer, not always', then it is true that 'nothing of stationary-fingered is a writer as long as he is stationary-fingered, not always in some part', i.e., some stationary-fingered is actually a writer. As for the first part, we have already explained that it is necessary for the two general propositions, and the two general propositions are necessary for the two specific propositions, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary. As for the second part, because if it were not true, its negation would be true, namely 'nothing of stationary-fingered is always a writer', and this together with the non-permanence of the original, i.e., 'every writer is actually stationary-fingered', produces 'nothing of a writer is a writer (always) or actually' (absurd). And the non-permanence does not apply to the whole, because in this example 'every stationary-fingered is actually a writer' might be false.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the conversion rules for various types of modulated categorical propositions in logic. The author begins by stating the basic rule: the absolute general proposition converts to an absolute general. He then addresses the two possibility propositions, explaining that according to Ibn Sina's view (which is standard) they have no valid conversion, unlike al-Farabi's view. A proof by reduction to absurdity is given for the conversion of specific propositions, using the example of 'writer' and 'finger-mover'. The author then systematically presents the conversion of the two eternal propositions to an eternal absolute, the two general propositions to a conventional general, and the two specific propositions to a conventional general negative universal with a non-permanence condition. Each case includes a logical proof showing that if the converse were false, a contradiction would follow. The commentary emphasizes the distinctions between different modes (necessary, permanent, general, specific) and how the conversion rules differ accordingly. The passage is technical and requires careful parsing of the Arabic logical terminology.

Conversion of Perpetual and General Negatives
Explains conversion of perpetual and general negative propositions with examples and reductio proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion to perpetual and general negative propositions and validate the result using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Perpetual negatives convert to perpetual absolute; general negatives to general customary.

In Islamic modal logic, not all propositions convert the same way. This lesson refines the conversion rule for two specific pairs of negative propositions: the perpetuals (necessary and absolute) and the generals (conditional and customary). Through a reductio proof, you will see why their converses must hold perpetually or customarily, and what happens if you deny them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
First, determine whether the proposition is a perpetual (necessary or absolute) or a general (conditional or customary). Look at the modal qualifier in the Arabic text.
The text specifies two types: perpetuals (ضرورية مطلقة دائمة and مطلقة دائمة) and generals (مشروطة عامة and عرفية عامة).
2
State the conversion rule
For perpetuals, the converse must be a perpetual absolute (دائمة مطلقة). For generals, the converse must be a general customary (عرفية عامة). Remember that the converse is the same universal negative but with swapped terms.
The rule is given at the beginning of the passage: تنعكس الدائمتان دائمة مطلقة والعامتان عرفية عامة.
3
Apply the reduction (reductio) proof
Assume the negation of the desired converse (e.g., 'Some stone is a human' for the perpetual example). Combine this with the original true proposition ('No human is a stone') using syllogistic reasoning. The result is an absurdity (e.g., 'Some stone is not a stone'), proving the original assumption false and the converse true.
The proof follows: if the converse were false, its contradictory (particular affirmative) would be true, leading to a contradiction with the original universal negative.
4
Generalize the pattern
Notice that the proof works for both perpetuals and generals because the logical structure is identical. Only the modality of the conclusion differs (perpetual absolute vs. general customary).
This shows that conversion of universal negatives preserves truth, but the modality adjusts to the specific type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنعكس الدائمتان دائمة مطلقة والعامتان، عرفية عامة
قوله تنعكس الدائمتان دائمة: أي: الضرورية المطلقة الدائمة المطلقة وتنعكسان دائمة مطلقة (۲۷) مثلا اذا صدق قولنا : لاشيء من الانسان بحجر بالضرورة او بالدوام صدق: لاشيء من الحجر بانسان دائماً والا لصدق نقيضه و هو: بعض الحجر انسان بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج بعض الحجر ليس بحجر هف. قوله والعامتان اى المشروطة العامة و العرفية العامة تنعكسان عرفية عامة (۲۸) مثلا اذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لاشيء الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً صدق بالدوام لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب مادام ساكن الاصابع والا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا : بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الاصابع بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج: بعض ساكن الاصابع ليس بساكن الاصابع بالفعل حين هو ساكن الاصابع هف.
The two perpetuals convert to a perpetual absolute, and the two generals to a general customary. His statement: 'the two perpetuals convert to a perpetual absolute': meaning the necessary absolute perpetual and the absolute perpetual; they both convert to a perpetual absolute. For example, if our statement 'Nothing of man is a stone' is true necessarily or perpetually, then 'Nothing of stone is a man' is true perpetually; otherwise its negation would be true, namely 'Some stone is a man in actuality', and that together with the original produces 'Some stone is not a stone' [absurdity]. His statement: 'and the two generals' meaning the general conditional and the general customary; they both convert to a general customary. For example, if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'Nothing of the writer is a finger-moving person as long as he is a writer', then it is true perpetually that 'Nothing of the finger-moving person is a writer as long as he is finger-moving'; otherwise its negation would be true, namely 'Some finger-moving person is a writer at the time he is actually finger-moving', and that together with the original produces 'Some finger-moving person is not finger-moving at the time he is actually finger-moving' [absurdity].
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya (super-commentary) explaining the conversion of two types of negative propositions: the two perpetuals (al-da'imatan) and the two generals (al-'ammatan). The author begins by stating the rule: the two perpetuals (i.e., the necessary absolute perpetual and the absolute perpetual) convert to a perpetual absolute; the two generals (the general conditional and the general customary) convert to a general customary. Then he provides examples with a reductio ad absurdum proof. For the perpetuals: if 'No human is a stone' is true perpetually, then its converse 'No stone is a human' must also be true perpetually. If not, then 'Some stone is a human' would be true, which together with the original yields 'Some stone is not a stone'—an impossibility. Similarly for the generals: if 'No writer is finger-moving while a writer' is true perpetually, then 'No finger-moving person is a writer while finger-moving' must be true. Denial leads to a contradiction. The proof follows the standard syllogistic form: the original is true, the negation of the desired converse is assumed, and together they produce a falsehood, confirming the converse. The commentator is refining the matn (main text) by showing the exact types and the logical structure.

العامتان
عرفية عامة

Key Takeaway

Converting the Two Specific Negatives
Explains conversion of specific propositions to general customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a specific conditional or specific customary negative proposition into its correct converse form.

Core Idea
Specific modal negatives convert to general customaries with partial non-permanence.

In modal logic, not all propositions convert like categoricals. The 'special' negatives have a unique conversion pattern that the commentator explains through necessary implications. This lesson focuses on the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two specifics
Recognize that 'al-khassatan' refers to the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) and the specific customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa). Both are universal negatives with a condition and a non-permanence qualifier.
2
State the conversion result
Each converts to a general customary negative universal qualified by 'non-permanence in some part'. This form effectively implies a general absolute particular affirmative.
3
Interpret the conversion output
The converse is not a simple universal; it is a universal negative that only holds for some individuals (partial non-permanence). Practically, this means 'some S is not P perpetually' is not the form; rather it's 'No S is P as long as S is in state, but not for all S'.
4
Apply the rule to an example
Given 'Nothing of the writer is finger-moving as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', we convert to 'Nothing of the finger-moving is a writer as long as he is finger-moving, not perpetually in some part' – i.e., some finger-moving person is actually a writer.
The original is from the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « والخاصتان عرفية»: اى المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان الى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام فى البعض وهو اشارة الى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية فنقول : اذا صدق بالضرورة او بالدوام لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً صدق: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب مادام ساكنا لا دائما في البعض اى بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب بالفعل اما الجزء الاول، فقد مربيانه من انه لازم للعامتين وهما لازمتان للخاصتين ولازم اللازم لازم و اما الجزء الثانى فلانه لو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه وهو: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً و هذا مع لادوام الاصل (۲۹) و هو: ان كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ينتج: لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب (دائماً خ ل بالفعل هف. و انما لم يلزم اللادوام فى الكل، لانه قد يكذب في مثالنا هذا كل ساكن كاتب بالفعل لصدق قولنا بعض الساكن ليس بكاتب دائماً كالارض. (۳۰) قال المصنف : السر في ذلك (۳۱) ان لادوام السالبة موجبة كلية وهي لا تتعكس الاجزئية وفيه تأمل اذ ليس انعكاس المجموع الى المجموع منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء كما يشهد بذلك ملاحظة انعكاس الموجهات الموجبة على ما مر فان الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان الى الجينية اللادائمة ان الجزء الثانى منهما و هو المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عکس لها فتدبر. (۳۲) قوله «ينتج المحال»: فهذا المحال اما ان يكون ناشئاً عن الاصل او عن نقيض العكس او عن هيئة تأليفهما لكن الاول مفروض الصدق و الثالث هو الشكل الاول المعلوم صحة انتاجه فتعين الثانى و هو نقيض العكس فيكون النقيض باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً و هو المطلوب. قوله « ولا عكس للبواقي»: اى فى السوالب الباقية و هي تسع (۳۳): الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة و المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسايط والوقتيان والوجوديتان و الممكنة الخاصة من المركبات. قوله « بالنقض» ای بدلیل التخلف فى مادة، بمعنى انه يصدق الأصل في (٥) قول المصنف والبيان في الكل... أى فى كل ما يصح انعكاسها موجبة كانت او سالبة، لا فى كل ما ذكر حتى فيا لا يصح انعكاسها كالممكنتين فان البيان فيه هو النقض لا الخلف كما تقدم و انما لم يتعرض للبيان فيهما، لبداهته و ظهوره. لايقال : لانسلم عدم تعرضه للبيان فيهما مطلقا، غاية الأمر انه لم يذكره فيا تقدم ولا يلزم منه ذلك فان قوله « ولا عكس للبواقى بالنقض عام شامل للممكنتين ايضا. لانا نقول : فعلى هذا يلزم التكرار المذموم و هو بعيد عن امثاله سيما في مثل هذا الكتاب حيث بالغ في اختصاره و لم يدع شيئاً من ايجازه حتى نقل عنه انه قال: من اتى باخصر من موجز ما قلناه في هذا الكتاب ففى كل مطلب له على ما تا درهم نعم لو اكتفى بقوله: «ولا عكس للبواق» عن قوله: ولا عكس للممكنتين» لظهر البيان فيها ايضاً بدون التكرار مع زيادة فايدة الاختصار ولذا قال بعض المحققين من المحشين انه يمكن ان يلزم عليه هيهنا مأتا درهم بناء على ما وعده. ولا يخفى أنه على هذا يلزم محذور أخراشد لانه اذا كان قوله: «ولا عكس للبواقي» عاماً شاملاً
His statement: 'and the two specifics [convert] to a general customary': meaning the specific conditional and the specific customary; they both convert to a general customary negative universal qualified by non-permanence in some part, which indicates a general absolute particular affirmative. So we say: if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'Nothing of the writer is a finger-moving person as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', then it is true that 'Nothing of the finger-moving person is a writer as long as he is finger-moving, not perpetually in some part', i.e., some finger-moving person is actually a writer. As for the first part, it is necessary because it is a necessary implication of the two generals, which themselves are necessary implications of the two specifics, and the necessary of a necessary is necessary. As for the second part, if it were not true, its negation would be true, namely 'Nothing of the finger-moving person is a writer perpetually', and this together with the non-permanence of the original (that every writer is actually finger-moving) produces 'Nothing of the writer is a writer (perpetually or actually)' [absurdity]. And non-permanence does not hold for all because in our example 'Every finger-moving person is actually a writer' may be false, since we can say 'Some finger-moving person is not a writer perpetually', like the earth. (30) The author said: the secret in that (31) is that the non-permanence of a negative is a universal affirmative, and it does not convert to particular; but there is a reflection, because the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by the observation of the conversion of affirmative modal propositions as previously: the two specific affirmatives convert to the possible non-perpetual; the second part of them, which is the general absolute negative, has no conversion; so reflect. (32) His statement 'produces the impossible': this impossible is either from the original, from the negation of the converse, or from the form of their composition. But the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose production is known to be valid; so the second is determined, namely the negation of the converse, so the negation is false, so the converse is true, which is what is desired. His statement 'and no conversion for the remaining [negatives]': meaning from the remaining negatives, which are nine: the time-absolute, the widespread-absolute, the general absolute, and the general possible from simples, the two time-conditionals, the two existence-conditionals, and the specific possible from composites. His statement 'by counterexample (naqd)': meaning by the evidence of failure in some matter, i.e., the original is true ... (5) The author's statement 'and the explanation for all' ... means for everything that validly converts, whether affirmative or negative, not for everything mentioned, even those that do not convert, like the two possibles, for the proof for them is by counterexample (naqd) not by reductio (khalf), as previously; and he did not elaborate the proof for them due to its obviousness. It should not be said: we do not concede that he did not elaborate the proof for them absolutely; at most he did not mention it earlier, and that does not necessitate that; for his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining by counterexample' is general and includes the two possibles as well. For we say: accordingly, there would be objectionable repetition, which is unlikely from his peers, especially in such a book where he was extremely concise and left nothing of his brevity; indeed it is narrated that he said: whoever brings a shorter summary than what we have said in this book, for every issue he has upon us such and such dirhams. Yes, if he had sufficed with his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining' instead of his statement 'and no conversion for the two possibles', the proof for them would also appear without repetition and with the added benefit of brevity; therefore some of the investigating commentators said that one could impose upon him two hundred dirhams here based on his promise. And it is not hidden that with this another more severe problem arises, because if his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining' is general and comprehensive...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the hashiya, now treating the conversion of the two specific propositions (al-khassatan). The commentator begins by explaining that the specific conditional and the specific customary convert to a general customary negative universal qualified by non-permanence in some part, which effectively yields a general absolute particular affirmative. He then provides a two-part proof: first, the necessary implication from the two generals (which themselves are implied by the specifics) ensures the form; second, a reductio argument shows that if the converse were false (i.e., the negation holds perpetually), it would contradict the original's non-permanence, producing an absurdity. The absurdity is that 'Nothing of the writer is a writer' would follow. The commentator notes that non-permanence does not apply universally because in the example, not every finger-moving person is actually a writer (e.g., the earth, which is non-writing). He then critiques the author's statement about the secret being that the non-permanence of a negative is a universal affirmative which does not convert to a particular; the commentator finds this questionable, pointing out that conversion of the whole does not depend on conversion of parts, as seen in affirmative conversions. Next, he explains the reductio structure: the absurdity arises from either the original, the negation of the converse, or the composition; the original is assumed true, and the composition is a valid first figure syllogism, so the negation of the converse must be false, making the converse true. Finally, he lists the nine remaining negatives that do not convert (time-absolute, etc.) and explains that their invalidity is shown by counterexample (naqd), not reductio. He engages in a critical discussion of the author's wording, arguing that the author's statement 'and no conversion for the remaining by counterexample' might be redundant if taken to include the two possibles already mentioned, and criticizes the author for potential repetition or lack of brevity, referencing a scholarly discussion about a promised dirham penalty.

Reductio Proof for Modal Conversion
Provides reductio proof for conversion of specifics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof that validates the conversion of specific negatives.

Core Idea
Reductio shows that denying the converse leads to absurdity, thereby proving the converse true.

The commentator provides a rigorous proof for the conversion of the two specifics using reductio. This lesson unpacks the structure of that proof: assuming the negation of the converse, combining it with the original, and deriving a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State the original and the desired converse
Original: 'No writer is finger-moving as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' (specific negative). Desired converse: 'No finger-moving is a writer as long as he is finger-moving, not perpetually in some part'.
2
Assume the negation of the converse
Negation of the converse: 'No finger-moving person is a writer perpetually' – a universal negative that denies the partial non-permanence.
3
Combine with the original's non-permanence
The original implies 'Every writer is actually finger-moving' (non-permanence part). Combine this with the assumed negation: 'No finger-moving is a writer perpetually' yields 'No writer is a writer perpetually' (an absurdity).
4
Identify the source of absurdity
The absurdity cannot come from the original (assumed true) nor from the composition (a valid first-figure syllogism). Therefore it must come from the negation of the converse, making that negation false, so the converse is true.
The commentator explicitly states this reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « والخاصتان عرفية»: اى المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان الى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام فى البعض وهو اشارة الى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية فنقول : اذا صدق بالضرورة او بالدوام لاشيء من الكاتب بساكن الاصابع مادام كاتباً لا دائماً صدق: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب مادام ساكنا لا دائما في البعض اى بعض ساكن الاصابع كاتب بالفعل اما الجزء الاول، فقد مربيانه من انه لازم للعامتين وهما لازمتان للخاصتين ولازم اللازم لازم و اما الجزء الثانى فلانه لو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه وهو: لاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً و هذا مع لادوام الاصل (۲۹) و هو: ان كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ينتج: لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب (دائماً خ ل بالفعل هف. و انما لم يلزم اللادوام فى الكل، لانه قد يكذب في مثالنا هذا كل ساكن كاتب بالفعل لصدق قولنا بعض الساكن ليس بكاتب دائماً كالارض. (۳۰) قال المصنف : السر في ذلك (۳۱) ان لادوام السالبة موجبة كلية وهي لا تتعكس الاجزئية وفيه تأمل اذ ليس انعكاس المجموع الى المجموع منوطاً بانعكاس الاجزاء الى الاجزاء كما يشهد بذلك ملاحظة انعكاس الموجهات الموجبة على ما مر فان الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان الى الجينية اللادائمة ان الجزء الثانى منهما و هو المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عکس لها فتدبر. (۳۲) قوله «ينتج المحال»: فهذا المحال اما ان يكون ناشئاً عن الاصل او عن نقيض العكس او عن هيئة تأليفهما لكن الاول مفروض الصدق و الثالث هو الشكل الاول المعلوم صحة انتاجه فتعين الثانى و هو نقيض العكس فيكون النقيض باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً و هو المطلوب. قوله « ولا عكس للبواقي»: اى فى السوالب الباقية و هي تسع (۳۳): الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة و المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسايط والوقتيان والوجوديتان و الممكنة الخاصة من المركبات. قوله « بالنقض» ای بدلیل التخلف فى مادة، بمعنى انه يصدق الأصل في (٥) قول المصنف والبيان في الكل... أى فى كل ما يصح انعكاسها موجبة كانت او سالبة، لا فى كل ما ذكر حتى فيا لا يصح انعكاسها كالممكنتين فان البيان فيه هو النقض لا الخلف كما تقدم و انما لم يتعرض للبيان فيهما، لبداهته و ظهوره. لايقال : لانسلم عدم تعرضه للبيان فيهما مطلقا، غاية الأمر انه لم يذكره فيا تقدم ولا يلزم منه ذلك فان قوله « ولا عكس للبواقى بالنقض عام شامل للممكنتين ايضا. لانا نقول : فعلى هذا يلزم التكرار المذموم و هو بعيد عن امثاله سيما في مثل هذا الكتاب حيث بالغ في اختصاره و لم يدع شيئاً من ايجازه حتى نقل عنه انه قال: من اتى باخصر من موجز ما قلناه في هذا الكتاب ففى كل مطلب له على ما تا درهم نعم لو اكتفى بقوله: «ولا عكس للبواق» عن قوله: ولا عكس للممكنتين» لظهر البيان فيها ايضاً بدون التكرار مع زيادة فايدة الاختصار ولذا قال بعض المحققين من المحشين انه يمكن ان يلزم عليه هيهنا مأتا درهم بناء على ما وعده. ولا يخفى أنه على هذا يلزم محذور أخراشد لانه اذا كان قوله: «ولا عكس للبواقي» عاماً شاملاً
His statement: 'and the two specifics [convert] to a general customary': meaning the specific conditional and the specific customary; they both convert to a general customary negative universal qualified by non-permanence in some part, which indicates a general absolute particular affirmative. So we say: if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'Nothing of the writer is a finger-moving person as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', then it is true that 'Nothing of the finger-moving person is a writer as long as he is finger-moving, not perpetually in some part', i.e., some finger-moving person is actually a writer. As for the first part, it is necessary because it is a necessary implication of the two generals, which themselves are necessary implications of the two specifics, and the necessary of a necessary is necessary. As for the second part, if it were not true, its negation would be true, namely 'Nothing of the finger-moving person is a writer perpetually', and this together with the non-permanence of the original (that every writer is actually finger-moving) produces 'Nothing of the writer is a writer (perpetually or actually)' [absurdity]. And non-permanence does not hold for all because in our example 'Every finger-moving person is actually a writer' may be false, since we can say 'Some finger-moving person is not a writer perpetually', like the earth. (30) The author said: the secret in that (31) is that the non-permanence of a negative is a universal affirmative, and it does not convert to particular; but there is a reflection, because the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by the observation of the conversion of affirmative modal propositions as previously: the two specific affirmatives convert to the possible non-perpetual; the second part of them, which is the general absolute negative, has no conversion; so reflect. (32) His statement 'produces the impossible': this impossible is either from the original, from the negation of the converse, or from the form of their composition. But the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose production is known to be valid; so the second is determined, namely the negation of the converse, so the negation is false, so the converse is true, which is what is desired. His statement 'and no conversion for the remaining [negatives]': meaning from the remaining negatives, which are nine: the time-absolute, the widespread-absolute, the general absolute, and the general possible from simples, the two time-conditionals, the two existence-conditionals, and the specific possible from composites. His statement 'by counterexample (naqd)': meaning by the evidence of failure in some matter, i.e., the original is true ... (5) The author's statement 'and the explanation for all' ... means for everything that validly converts, whether affirmative or negative, not for everything mentioned, even those that do not convert, like the two possibles, for the proof for them is by counterexample (naqd) not by reductio (khalf), as previously; and he did not elaborate the proof for them due to its obviousness. It should not be said: we do not concede that he did not elaborate the proof for them absolutely; at most he did not mention it earlier, and that does not necessitate that; for his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining by counterexample' is general and includes the two possibles as well. For we say: accordingly, there would be objectionable repetition, which is unlikely from his peers, especially in such a book where he was extremely concise and left nothing of his brevity; indeed it is narrated that he said: whoever brings a shorter summary than what we have said in this book, for every issue he has upon us such and such dirhams. Yes, if he had sufficed with his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining' instead of his statement 'and no conversion for the two possibles', the proof for them would also appear without repetition and with the added benefit of brevity; therefore some of the investigating commentators said that one could impose upon him two hundred dirhams here based on his promise. And it is not hidden that with this another more severe problem arises, because if his statement 'and no conversion for the remaining' is general and comprehensive...
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the hashiya, now treating the conversion of the two specific propositions (al-khassatan). The commentator begins by explaining that the specific conditional and the specific customary convert to a general customary negative universal qualified by non-permanence in some part, which effectively yields a general absolute particular affirmative. He then provides a two-part proof: first, the necessary implication from the two generals (which themselves are implied by the specifics) ensures the form; second, a reductio argument shows that if the converse were false (i.e., the negation holds perpetually), it would contradict the original's non-permanence, producing an absurdity. The absurdity is that 'Nothing of the writer is a writer' would follow. The commentator notes that non-permanence does not apply universally because in the example, not every finger-moving person is actually a writer (e.g., the earth, which is non-writing). He then critiques the author's statement about the secret being that the non-permanence of a negative is a universal affirmative which does not convert to a particular; the commentator finds this questionable, pointing out that conversion of the whole does not depend on conversion of parts, as seen in affirmative conversions. Next, he explains the reductio structure: the absurdity arises from either the original, the negation of the converse, or the composition; the original is assumed true, and the composition is a valid first figure syllogism, so the negation of the converse must be false, making the converse true. Finally, he lists the nine remaining negatives that do not convert (time-absolute, etc.) and explains that their invalidity is shown by counterexample (naqd), not reductio. He engages in a critical discussion of the author's wording, arguing that the author's statement 'and no conversion for the remaining by counterexample' might be redundant if taken to include the two possibles already mentioned, and criticizes the author for potential repetition or lack of brevity, referencing a scholarly discussion about a promised dirham penalty.

Key Terms
المحال
الخلف
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio proof, what is the source of the absurd conclusion?
A) The original proposition
B) The negation of the converse
C) The syllogistic form (composition)
D) Both the original and the negation

Key Takeaway
Reductio in modal conversions works by assuming the negation of the converse and showing it contradicts the original's non-permanence, proving the converse true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a reductio proof for the conversion of 'Nothing of the scholar is ignorant as long as he is a scholar, not perpetually' to its converse. Indicate the absurdity that follows.
?
Explain why the absurdity must come from the negation of the converse rather than the original or the syllogistic form.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
6 min
Nine Non-Converting Negatives and Counterexample
Lists nine negatives that do not convert, based on counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine negative modal propositions that do not convert and explain why counterexample (naqd) disproves their conversion.

Core Idea
Nine negatives fail conversion because a single counterexample suffices to show the converse does not follow.

After handling the two specifics, the author lists nine negatives that do not convert. For these, the proof is by counterexample (naqd) not by reductio. This lesson teaches how to identify these propositions and apply the method of naqd.

Guided Path
1
List the nine non-converting negatives
From the text: time-absolute, widespread-absolute, general absolute, general possible from simples, the two time-conditionals, the two existence-conditionals, and specific possible from composites. Memorize this list.
These are all negative modal propositions except the two specifics.
2
Understand why they fail conversion
Conversion fails because the truth of the original does not necessitate the truth of the converse in all cases. A single counterexample (naqd) is enough to disprove the universal claim that they convert.
3
Apply the method of naqd
To disprove conversion, find a specific instance where the original is true but the converse is false. For example, 'Some human is not a writer perpetually' (time-absolute) does not convert to 'Some writer is not a human perpetually' because the latter is false (all writers are human).
The commentator mentions that the proof for these is by naqd, not khalf.
4
Critique the author's wording
The commentator discusses whether the author's statement 'and no conversion for the remaining by counterexample' is redundant if it already includes the two possibles. This highlights the importance of precise wording in logical texts.
This is a minor refinement point.

Immediate Inference

Proof of Conversion via Reductio (Qiyas Khulfi)
Author explains the reductio proof that contradictory of converse with original yields absurdity, thus converse true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof for conversion and identify the source of the absurdity in the argument.

Core Idea
Reductio proves the converse by showing its contradictory leads to absurdity with the original.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how immediate inference (conversion) can be proven using a reductio ad absurdum argument. This is a powerful method in logic: to prove a proposition, you assume its negation and derive a contradiction. The commentator unpacks the logical steps, showing why the absurdity must come from the assumed negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What is the conversion rule being proved? The passage states that a universal negative proposition converts to a particular negative. This is the target of the proof.
2
State the reductio assumption
Assume the contradictory of the converse is true. That is, assume the negation of what we want to prove.
The contradictory of the converse is the proposition that would make the converse false.
3
Form the syllogism
Combine the original proposition (assumed true) with the contradictory of the converse to form a first-figure categorical syllogism. The passage says this composition produces an absurdity.
The first figure is known to be valid, so if the premises are true, the conclusion must be true. But here we get an absurd conclusion, meaning something is wrong with one premise.
4
Analyze the sources of absurdity
The absurdity could come from the original proposition, from the contradictory of the converse, or from the composition (syllogistic form). The original is assumed true, and the first figure is valid, so the absurdity cannot come from those. Therefore, it must come from the contradictory of the converse.
This is a process of elimination.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since the contradictory of the converse leads to absurdity, it must be false. Therefore, the converse itself is true. This proves the conversion rule.
This is a classic reductio argument: prove a statement by showing its negation leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في العكس المستوى لا دائمة فى البعض. والبيان في الكل (٥) ان نقيض العكس مع الاصل ينتج المحال. قوله «ينتج المحال»: فهذا المحال اما ان يكون ناشئاً عن الاصل او عن نقيض العكس او عن هيئة تأليفهما لكن الاول مفروض الصدق و الثالث هو الشكل الاول المعلوم صحة انتاجه فتعين الثانى و هو نقيض العكس فيكون النقيض باطلاً فيكون العكس حقاً و هو المطلوب.
Regarding uniform conversion: the perpetual (universal) negative (converts) to a particular. The demonstration for all (cases) is that the contradictory of the converse together with the original produces an absurdity. His statement: 'produces an absurdity': This absurdity is either arising from the original, or from the contradictory of the converse, or from the composition of the two. But the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose validity is known, so the second is determined — and it is the contradictory of the converse. Therefore the contradictory is false, so the converse is true, and that is the required.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the proof of conversion (al-'aks) via reductio ad absurdum (qiyas khulfi). The author presents the proof that for a universal negative proposition that converts to a particular, the demonstration is that assuming the contradictory of the converse together with the original leads to an absurdity. The commentator then analyzes the source of the absurdity: it could arise from the original proposition (which is assumed true), from the contradictory of the converse, or from the syllogistic composition (the first figure, which is valid). Since the original is true and the first figure is valid, the absurdity must come from the contradictory of the converse. Hence the contradictory is false, making the converse true. This is a typical reductio proof used in logic to establish conversion rules.

Key Terms
نقيض العكس (naqīḍ al-ʿaks): The contradictory of the converse proposition; in this proof, its truth is shown to lead to absurdity.
المحال (al-muḥāl): The absurdity or contradiction that results from combining the original and the contradictory of the converse; used as evidence in the reductio.
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, whose validity is assumed; the composition of the original and the contradictory of the converse forms a first-figure syllogism. [Note: In other contexts, the first figure may be defined differently; here it is the standard valid form.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the reductio proof for conversion, why must the absurdity come from the contradictory of the converse?
A) Because the original is true and the syllogism form (first figure) is valid.
B) Because the contradictory of the converse is always false.
C) Because the original is false in the example.
D) Because the first figure is not valid for conversion.

Key Takeaway
The reductio proof for conversion eliminates possible sources of absurdity to establish the truth of the converse, relying on the validity of the first figure and the truth of the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar reductio proof for the conversion of a universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is mortal') to a particular affirmative ('Some mortal is human'). Follow the same steps: assume the contradictory of the converse, combine with the original, and derive an absurdity.
?
In a fiqh argument, a scholar says: 'Every transaction involving riba is haram. Therefore, some haram transactions involve riba.' Use the reductio method to justify this conversion and explain why it is valid.

Nine Non-Converting Negatives and Naqd Proof
Author lists nine non-converting negatives and proves with naqd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nine types of negative propositions that do not convert and apply the method of naqd to demonstrate non-conversion using a counterexample.

Core Idea
Naqd proves non-conversion by finding one true original with a false converse.

In this passage, the commentator identifies nine negative propositions that lack a valid converse. He then introduces naqd—a counterexample where the original is true but the converse is false—to prove that these propositions do not convert. We will work through the list, the lunar eclipse example, and the logical chain that extends failure from the specific to the general.

Guided Path
1
List the nine non-converting negatives
Identify the nine propositions from the commentary: absolute temporal, absolute diffusive, absolute general, general possible from simples, two temporal, two existential, and special possible from composites.
These are all negative (saliba) propositions with specific temporal or modal conditions.
2
Understand the method of naqd
Naqd means finding a single instance (madda) where the original proposition is true but its converse is false. This disproves the claim that conversion holds universally.
The converse is an immediate inference; naqd shows that the original does not imply its converse.
3
Examine the temporal example
The temporal proposition 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not perpetually' is true. Its intended converse 'Some non-eclipsed is not a moon' is false because its contradictory 'Every eclipsed is a moon' is necessarily true.
This shows that the temporal negative does not convert, serving as the naqd for all nine.
4
Understand the logical chain from specific to general
Since the temporal is the most specific of the nine, its non-conversion implies non-conversion for all more general ones. If a general proposition converted, its converse would be necessary for it, and since the general is necessary for the specific, the converse would also be necessary for the specific—contradicting the proven non-conversion.
This argument uses the principle that the converse is necessary for the original proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « ولا عكس للبواقي»: اى فى السوالب الباقية و هي تسع (۳۳): الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة و المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسايط والوقتيان والوجوديتان و الممكنة الخاصة من المركبات. قوله « بالنقض» ای بدلیل التخلف فى مادة، بمعنى انه يصدق الأصل في مادة بدون العكس فيعلم بذلك ان العكس غير لازم لهذا الاصل و بيان التخلف في تلك القضايا ان اخصها وهى الوقتية (۳۴) قد تصدق بدون العكس فانه يصدق: لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما (۳۵) مع كذب بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالامكان العام لصدق نقيضه (۳۶) و هو كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة و اذا تحقق التخلف وعدم الانعكاس فى الاخص تحقق في الاعم اذ العكس لازم للقضية فلو انعكاس الاعم كان العكس لازماً للاعم والاعم لازم للاخص و لازم اللازم لازم فيكون العكس لازماً للاخص ايضاً وقد بينا عدم انعكاسه هف. و انما اخترنا فى العكس الجزئية، (۳۷) لانها اعم من الكلية و الممكنة العامة لأنها أعم من سائر الموجهات (۳۸) و اذا لم يصدق الاعم لم يصدق الاخص (۳۹) بالطريق الاولى بخلاف العكس. (۴۰)
His statement: 'And no conversion for the rest' means among the remaining negatives, and they are nine: the absolute temporal, the absolute diffusive, the absolute general, the general possible from simples, the two temporal, the two existential, and the special possible from composites. His statement: 'by naqd' meaning by the proof of non-occurrence in a matter, i.e., the original is true in a matter without the converse, so it is known thereby that the converse is not necessary for this original. And the demonstration of non-occurrence in those propositions is that the most specific of them, which is the temporal, can be true without the converse. For it is true: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not perpetually' while its converse 'Some non-eclipsed is not a moon' is false by general possibility, because its contradictory 'Every eclipsed is a moon' is necessarily true. And when non-occurrence and non-conversion are established for the more specific, they are established for the more general, because the converse is necessary for the proposition. So if the more general converted, then the converse would be necessary for the more general, and the more general is necessary for the more specific, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary, so the converse would be necessary for the more specific as well, and we have shown its non-conversion. And we have chosen the particular in conversion because it is more general than the universal, and the general possible because it is more general than other modalities, and when the more general is not true, the more specific is not true a fortiori, contrary to the converse.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage identifies the nine types of negative propositions that do not convert (have no converse) and demonstrates their non-conversion using the method of naqd (counterexample). The commentator lists the nine: al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa (absolute temporal), al-muntashira al-mutlaqa (absolute diffusive), al-mutlaqa al-'amma (absolute general), al-mumkina al-'amma min al-basa'it (general possible from simples), al-waqtiyyan (the two temporal: temporal and restricted? Actually the text says 'al-waqtiyyan' meaning the two temporal propositions), al-wujudiyyan (the two existential), and al-mumkina al-khassa min al-murakkabat (special possible from composites). Then it explains that naqd means finding a case (madda) where the original proposition is true but its converse is false. The example given is the absolute temporal proposition: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not perpetually' (meaning it is true at that specific time, but not always). Its converse 'Some non-eclipsed is not a moon' is false because its contradictory 'Every eclipsed is a moon' is necessary true. Since the more specific (temporal) fails to convert, the more general (like absolute general) also fails, because if a more general proposition converted, its converse would be necessary for it, and since the more general is necessary for the more specific, the converse would become necessary for the more specific as well, contradicting its proven non-conversion. Finally, the commentator explains why the particular (juz'iyya) and the general possible (mumkina 'amma) were chosen as the converses: they are more general than the universal (kulliyya) and other modalities, so if they are false, the more specific are false a fortiori.

النقض
التخلف
الوقتية المطلقة

Key Takeaway
Naqd is a powerful tool to disprove universal logical claims by finding a single counterexample, and it shows that conversion is not valid for all types of negative propositions.
