Interpreting 'al-Bawaqi' Without Redundancy or Contradiction
Author defends separate mention of two possibles against redundancy objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether an interpretation of a logical term like 'al-bawaqi' is consistent with the author's conciseness and the established rules of conversion.

Core Idea
Contextual consistency and logical inference expose the intended scope of ambiguous terms.

This passage from the hashia tackles an apparent redundancy in the author's text. At stake is the correct interpretation of 'al-bawaqi' (the rest). The commentator shows that a perceived redundancy leads to a logical contradiction about conversion, forcing a refined reading. You will learn how to apply such critical refinement to logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguity
Locate 'al-bawaqi' in the passage and note which propositions could be included. The objection assumes it includes the two possibles, but the commentator argues this causes problems.
2
Extract the redundancy objection
If 'al-bawaqi' already includes the two possibles, why does the author separately mention them? This would be 'al-takrar al-madhmum' (blameworthy repetition), contradicting the author's extreme conciseness.
The author reportedly promised a reward for anyone who could be more concise.
3
Recognize the logical consequence
The commentator shows that a general 'al-bawaqi' would also imply that the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive propositions do not convert. But we know from the author's own previous statements that eleven positive propositions do convert, including these two. So the general interpretation leads to a false conclusion.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the general interpretation leads to contradiction with established facts.
4
See the commentarial resolution
The hashia writer concludes that 'al-bawaqi' must be limited to negatives, so the separate mention of the two possibles is necessary. Alternatively, 'al-bawaqi' might exclude the absolute temporal and diffusive, thus avoiding the contradiction.
This shows how a hashia refines textual interpretation based on logical consistency and authorial style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لايقال : لانسلم عدم تعرضه للبيان فيهما مطلقا، غاية الأمر انه لم يذكره فيا تقدم ولا يلزم منه ذلك فان قوله « ولا عكس للبواقى بالنقض عام شامل للممكنتين ايضا. لانا نقول : فعلى هذا يلزم التكرار المذموم و هو بعيد عن امثاله سيما في مثل هذا الكتاب حيث بالغ في اختصاره و لم يدع شيئاً من ايجازه حتى نقل عنه انه قال: من اتى باخصر من موجز ما قلناه في هذا الكتاب ففى كل مطلب له على ما تا درهم نعم لو اكتفى بقوله: «ولا عكس للبواق» عن قوله: ولا عكس للممكنتين» لظهر البيان فيها ايضاً بدون التكرار مع زيادة فايدة الاختصار ولذا قال بعض المحققين من المحشين انه يمكن ان يلزم عليه هيهنا مأتا درهم بناء على ما وعده. ولا يخفى أنه على هذا يلزم محذور أخراشد لانه اذا كان قوله: «ولا عكس للبواقي» عاماً شاملاً مادة بدون العكس فيعلم بذلك ان العكس غير لازم لهذا الاصل و بيان التخلف في تلك القضايا ان اخصها وهى الوقتية (۳۴) قد تصدق بدون العكس فانه يصدق: لاشيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما (۳۵) مع كذب بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالامكان العام لصدق نقيضه (۳۶) و هو كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة و اذا تحقق التخلف وعدم الانعكاس فى الاخص تحقق في الاعم اذ العكس لازم للقضية فلو انعكاس الاعم كان العكس لازماً للاعم والاعم لازم للاخص و لازم اللازم لازم فيكون العكس لازماً للاخص ايضاً وقد بينا عدم انعكاسه هف. و للموجبات والسوالب و قدبين من الموجبات انعكاس احدى عشر قضية و لم يبين انعكاس الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتين منها، لزم ان لا يكون الوقتية المطلقة و المنتشرة المطلقة منها ايضاً عكس كما هو ظاهر و الواقع خلافه هذا بخلاف ما اذا خص قوله هذا بالسوالب واحتيج إلى قوله هنالك : «ولا عكس للممكنتين ضرورة ان عدم ذكر الشيء اعم من عدمه. اللهم الا ان يقال انه لما اسقط بيان الوقتية و المنتشره المطلقتين في هذه المباحث كان المراد من البواقى بناء على التعميم غير الوقتية و المنتشرة المطلقتين من ساير القضاياء المذكورة في المباحث المشهورة المعتبرة. (محمد على)
One might object: We do not concede that he did not address the demonstration for them (the two possibles) at all. The utmost is that he did not mention it previously, but that does not entail (non-addressing). For his statement 'And no conversion for the rest by naqd' is general and includes the two possibles as well. We respond: On this (interpretation) there would be blameworthy repetition, and that is far from his like, especially in such a book where he went to extremes in conciseness and left nothing (uncompressed) in his brevity. It is even reported that he said: 'Whoever brings something more concise than the summary I have made in this book, for every topic he has, on me are two hundred dirhams.' Indeed, if he had sufficed with his statement 'And no conversion for the rest' instead of his statement 'And no conversion for the two possibles,' the demonstration for them would appear without repetition with the added benefit of conciseness. Hence some of the verifiers among commentators said that here he could be liable for two hundred dirhams based on his promise. And it is not hidden that on this (interpretation) another, more severe problem follows: because if his statement 'And no conversion for the rest' is general and comprehensive, then (the moon example follows)... and for the positives and negatives: it has been shown that eleven positive propositions convert, and he did not show the conversion of the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive among them. It would follow that the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive do not convert either, as is apparent, but the reality is otherwise. This is contrary to the case where he specified this statement for negatives and needed his statement there: 'And no conversion for the two possibles' because the non-mention of something is more general than its non-existence. Unless it is said that since he omitted the demonstration of the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive in these discussions, the intended meaning of 'the rest' based on generalization is other than the absolute temporal and absolute diffusive from the other well-known propositions considered in the standard discussions. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) addressing an objection about the phrase 'al-bawaqi' (the rest). The commentator first presents an objection: the author (al-Sullam?) did not fail to provide a demonstration for the two possible propositions (al-mumkinatan) because his statement 'no conversion for the rest' is general and includes them. The response argues that if 'al-bawaqi' is taken in its general sense, it would include the two possibles, but then the author's separate statement 'no conversion for the two possibles' would be redundant. This redundancy contradicts the author's extreme conciseness, as he famously promised a reward for anyone who could be more concise. Some commentators even suggest the author might be liable for two hundred dirhams. The objection is refined further: if 'al-bawaqi' is general, another problem arises because the demonstration for the temporal (waqtiyya) would be repeated, and also for positives, it would imply that the absolute temporal and diffusive do not convert, contrary to fact. The resolution is that the author's 'al-bawaqi' is specifically for negatives, and the separate mention of the two possibles is necessary because non-mention is more general than non-existence; but it is also suggested that the author may have omitted the demonstration for the temporal and diffusive, intending 'al-bawaqi' to exclude them. This shows the nuanced interpretive work by the hashia writer in reconciling the author's statements.

الممكنتين
التكرار المذموم
الوقتية
المنتشرة
العكس

Failure of Conversion for Temporary Propositions
Author explains failure of conversion for temporary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a temporary modal proposition fails to convert and demonstrate why using the lunar eclipse example.

Core Idea
Temporary propositions can be true without their converse being true.

In modal logic, not all categorical propositions convert straightforwardly. The author introduces the temporary proposition (al-waqtiyya) and shows that it may be true while its converse is false. This lesson walks through the lunar eclipse example to see how conversion fails for time-bound statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporary proposition
Find the proposition that is true only at a specific time. Here: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature' is true, but note the qualification 'not always'.
The proposition is specific to the quadrature phase of the moon.
2
Form the converse
Swap subject and predicate: 'Some eclipsed thing is not the moon'. The author claims this converse is false under general possibility.
General possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) allows exceptions.
3
Check the truth of the converse
The contradictory of the converse is 'Every eclipsed thing is the moon necessarily', which is true. Hence the original converse is false.
This shows the temporary proposition does not entail its converse.
4
Generalize the failure
Since failure occurs for the most specific type, it extends to more general types by the principle of necessary consequence.
If conversion fails for the specific, it fails for the general.

A matter without conversion, so it is known by that that the conversion is not necessary for this original. And the explanation of failure in those propositions: the most specific of them, which is the temporary, may be true without its converse. For it is true: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' while the converse 'Some eclipsed is not a moon' is false under general possibility, because its opposite 'Every eclipsed is a moon necessarily' is true. And when failure and non-conversion are verified for the more specific, they are verified for the more general, for the converse is necessary for the proposition. And we chose the particular for the converse, because it is more general than the universal, and the possible general because it is more general than the other modal propositions. Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the conversion (عكس) of modal propositions. It begins by stating that the most specific type, namely the temporary proposition (وقتية), can be true without its converse. An example from lunar eclipses is given: 'Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature, not always' is true, but its converse 'Some eclipsed is not a moon' is false under general possibility because its contradictory 'Every eclipsed is a moon necessarily' is true. This demonstrates that conversion is not necessary for the specific type. Then it argues that if conversion fails for the more specific, it also fails for the more general. The reasoning is: if the more general did convert, then the converse would be a necessary consequent of the general, and since the general is a necessary consequent of the specific, the converse would also be necessary for the specific, which is false. Hence, the author chooses the particular (جزئية) as the universal converse because it is more general than the universal, and the possible general (ممكنة عامة) because it is more general than other modal statements. The principle is: if the more general is not true, then the more specific is not true by first priority. The passage concludes with a remark about the conversion of eleven affirmative propositions and the omission of the temporary and indefinite universal, clarifying that the intended 'remainder' excludes these two types, otherwise reality would contradict.

العكس
الامكان العام

Justification for Choosing the Particular as Universal Converse
Author justifies choosing the particular as the universal converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the particular proposition is chosen as the universal converse for modal propositions using the principle of generality.

Core Idea
The particular is the safest converse because it is more general and avoids false conversions.

The author chooses the particular (juz'iyya) as the universal converse for modal propositions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: the particular is more general than the universal, and the possible general (al-mumkina al-'amma) is more general than other modals. Using the principle that if the more general is false the more specific is false, the particular converse guards against error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's choice
The author explicitly says 'we chose the particular for the converse' and adds 'the possible general because it is more general'.
The particular is a proposition like 'Some S is P' (or 'Some S is not P' for negatives).
2
Understand the generality hierarchy
The particular is more general than the universal: if a universal is true, the particular is automatically true, but not vice versa. Similarly, the possible general is broader than other modal claims.
More general = less restrictive, easier to be true.
3
Apply the principle of priority
The author argues: if the more general converse is not true, then the more specific (e.g., universal) cannot be true by 'first priority' (bi'l-tariq al-awla).
This ensures that if the particular fails, the universal fails, so choosing the particular is a safe lower bound.
4
Contrast with the universal
If the universal were chosen, it could be false while the original is true, leading to invalid inference. The particular avoids this problem.
The choice preserves truth across modal conversions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Contraposition: Two Definitions in Islamic Logic
Author defines contraposition and distinguishes two definitions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the ancient and later definitions of contraposition and apply each to a given categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Contraposition is defined differently: ancients preserve quality, later scholars change it.

In this passage, the author defines contraposition (عكس النقيض) and immediately presents two competing definitions: that of the ancients, who keep the quality of the proposition unchanged, and that of the later scholars, who reverse it. Understanding this difference is crucial when studying immediate inference in Islamic logic, as it affects how you convert propositions in real argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find in the passage the definitions given by the ancients and the later scholars. Note that the ancients preserve both truth and quality, while the later scholars preserve truth but change quality.
The key phrase for ancients is 'مع بقاء الصدق والكيف', for later scholars 'مع مخالفة الكيف'.
2
Apply to an example (ancient method)
Take the example given: 'Every J is B'. Following the ancients, exchange the negations of the terms while keeping quality affirmative. The result is 'Every not-B is not J'.
3
Apply the same example (later method)
Now apply the later definition: make the negation of the second term first and the original first term second, then change the quality. For 'Every J is B', convert to 'Nothing not-B is J' (or 'No not-B is J').
Note that the later result is negative, whereas the ancient result remains affirmative.
4
Compare the results
Observe that both methods produce true converses if the original is true, but they differ in quality. The ancient converse is affirmative, the later converse is negative.
This difference is important when you encounter contraposition in classical texts, as the author may follow one school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: عكس النقيض: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين مع بقاء الصدق والكيف عكس النقيض قوله تبديل نقيضي الطرفين اى جعل نقيض الجزء الاول من الاصل جزء ثانياً من العكس و نقيض الجزء الثانى جزء اولاً . ومع قوله مع بقاء الصدق اى ان كان الاصل صادقاً كان العكس صادقاً بقاء الكيف ای ان كان الاصل موجباً كان العكس موجباً و ان كان سالياً كان العكس سالباً، مثلاً قولنا كل ج ، ب ينعكس بعكس النقيض الى قولنا: كل ما ليس ب ليس ج و هذه طريقة القدماء و اما المتأخرون فقالوا : عكس النقيض هو: جعل نقيض الجزء الثانى اولاً و عين الجزء الاول ثانياً مع مخالفة الكيف ای ان كان الاصل موجباً كان العكس سالباً وبالعكس و يعتبر بقاء الصدق كما مر فقولنا كل ج ، ب ينعكس الى قولنا : لاشيء مماليس ب ، ج والمصنف : لم يصرح بقولهم: وعين الاول ثانياً للعلم به ضمناً ولا باعتبار بقاء الصدق في التعريف الثانى لذكره سابقاً، فحيث لم يخالفه فى هذا التعريف علم اعتباره هيهنا ايضاً. ثم انه بين المصنف احكام عكس النقيض على طريقة القدماء ، اذ فيه غنية
Chapter: Contraposition: exchanging the negations of the two terms while preserving truth and quality. Contraposition. His statement 'exchanging the negations of the two terms' means making the negation of the first part of the original a second part of the converse, and the negation of the second part a first part. And with his statement 'while preserving truth' means if the original is true, the converse is true; preserving 'quality' means if the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative, and if it is negative, the converse is negative. For example, our statement 'Every J is B' converts by contraposition to our statement 'Every not-B is not J'. And this is the method of the ancients. As for the later scholars, they said: contraposition is making the negation of the second part first, and the original first part second, while changing the quality, i.e., if the original is affirmative, the converse is negative, and vice versa, and preservation of truth is considered as before. So our statement 'Every J is B' converts to our statement 'Nothing which is not-B is J'. And the author did not explicitly state their statement 'and the original first part second' because it is known implicitly, nor did he mention preservation of truth in the second definition because it was mentioned earlier. So since he did not differ in this definition, it is known that it is considered here as well. Then the author explained the rulings of contraposition according to the method of the ancients, for therein is sufficiency.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines contraposition (عكس النقيض) and distinguishes between two definitions. It begins with the chapter heading and then explains the first definition: exchanging the negations of the two terms while preserving truth and quality. The commentator clarifies that this means making the negation of the first term of the original the second term of the converse, and the negation of the second term the first. Preserving truth means if the original is true, the converse is true; preserving quality means if the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative, and if negative, the converse is negative. An example is 'Every J is B' converts to 'Every not-B is not J'. This is the method of the ancients. The later scholars define it differently: making the negation of the second term first and the original first term second, while changing the quality. So 'Every J is B' converts to 'Nothing not-B is J'. The author did not explicitly mention keeping the original first term as second because it is understood, nor did he mention preservation of truth because it was already mentioned earlier. The passage concludes that the author then explained the rules of contraposition according to the ancients, as that is sufficient.

Contraposition (Aks al-Naqid): Rules for Categorical Propositions
Author explains contraposition conversion rules and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of contraposition to categorical propositions and correctly identify which types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) yield valid contrapositives.

Core Idea
Contraposition turns a proposition inside out, but not every proposition survives the turn.

In this lesson, we dive into the rules of contraposition as refined by the Yazdi Hashiya. Contraposition is a form of immediate inference that swaps subject and predicate after negating both, often used in logical proofs. The text compares contraposition to simple conversion and highlights critical exceptions, especially for particular propositions. Mastering these rules ensures sound reasoning when transforming propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall simple conversion rules
Simple conversion moves the subject and predicate without negating. In simple conversion, the universal negative converts as itself, the particular negative does not convert, the universal affirmative converts as particular, and the particular affirmative converts as itself.
These rules are the reference point for contraposition.
2
Parallel for contraposition: affirmatives
In contraposition, the rule for affirmatives mirrors the rule for negatives in simple conversion. So the universal affirmative (e.g., 'All J are B') contraposes as itself (i.e., 'All non-B are non-J'), but the particular affirmative does not contrapose at all.
The text gives the example: 'Some animal is not human' is true, but its contrapositive 'Some human is not animal' is false, showing the failure.
3
Parallel for contraposition: negatives
In contraposition, the rule for negatives mirrors the rule for affirmatives in simple conversion. Thus the universal negative (e.g., 'No J are B') contraposes as a particular negative (i.e., 'Some non-B are not non-J'), and the particular negative contraposes as itself.
The text illustrates with: 'Nothing of humanity is without animal' (true) but its contrapositive 'Nothing of animal is without humanity' is false, confirming the limitation.
4
Test with additional examples
Work through the same pattern with Islamic examples. For instance, the universal affirmative 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' should contrapose to 'Everything not forbidden is not an intoxicant' (valid). Try the particular affirmative 'Some Muslims are wealthy' — does it have a valid contrapositive?
This step applies the rule and checks understanding.

The gloss: Or if one makes the contrapositive of the second [subject] first while changing the quality, and the rule of affirmatives here is the rule of negatives in simple conversion, and vice versa, and the demonstration is the demonstration of the seeker of perfection, and he omitted what the later scholars mentioned, because detailed discussion of it and what is in it cannot be accommodated in this space. His saying 'here' means in contraposition. His saying 'in simple [conversion]' means: just as the universal negative converts in simple conversion as itself, and the particular does not convert at all, similarly the universal affirmative in contraposition converts as itself, and the particular does not convert at all, because our statement 'some animal is not human' is true and our statement 'some human is not animal' is false. And likewise the nine among the modal propositions, namely the two absolute temporals, the two temporals, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute general, do not convert. And the remaining [modalities] convert according to what preceded for negatives in simple conversion. His saying 'and vice versa' means the rule of negatives here is the rule of affirmatives in simple conversion: just as the affirmative in simple conversion does not convert except as particular, similarly the negative here does not convert except as particular, because the contradictory of the predicate in the negative may be more general than the subject, and it is not permissible to negate the more specific's contradictory from the very more general universally. For example, it is correct: 'Nothing of humanity is without animal' and it is not correct: 'Nothing of animal is without humanity' because its contradictory 'some animal is not human' is true, like the horse. And likewise according to modality: the two perpetual and the two general convert to absolute temporal, and the two special convert to absolute temporal non-perpetual, and the two temporal, the two existential, and the absolute general [convert to] absolute general, and there is no conversion for the two possible, analogous to affirmatives in simple conversion. His saying 'and the demonstration is the demonstration' means: just as the required matters in simple conversion were established by reductio, similarly here. His saying 'and the contradictory is the contradictory' means the matter of counterexample here is the matter of counterexample. His saying 'and he has explained the conversion of the two special' — as for the explanation of the conversion of the two special from the particular negative in simple conversion to the special general, it is to say: whenever it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'some J is not B as long as J is not always', i.e., 'some J is B actually', then it is true that 'some B is not J as long as B is not always', i.e., 'some B is J actually'. That is by the method of hypothesis: one posits an individual of the subject, namely some J, D, and D is B by the decree of the non-perpetuity of the original, and D is J actually due to the truth of the descriptive attribute on the individual actually according to the verification. Thus it is true that 'some B is J actually', which is the non-perpetuity of the converse. Then we say: D is not J as long as B, otherwise it would be J at some times of its being B, so it would be B at some times of its being J, because when two attributes coincide in an individual, each is established at the time of the other partially. And the rule of the original was that it is not B as long as J. So it is true that some B, i.e., D, is not J as long as B, which is the first part of the converse. Thus the converse is proven with both its parts. So understand. And as for the explanation of the conversion of the two special from the particular affirmative in contraposition to the special general, it is to say: if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'some J is B as long as J is not always', i.e., 'some J is not B actually', then it is true that 'some of what is not B is not J as long as it is not B not always', i.e., 'not some of what is not B is not J actually', and that is by the method
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is from the Yazdi Hashiya, a super-commentary on the logic text. It refines and explains the rules of contraposition (aks al-naqid) for categorical propositions. The author begins by stating a general rule: in contraposition, the rule for affirmative propositions is the same as the rule for negatives in simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi), and vice versa. He explains that just as in simple conversion a universal negative converts as itself (i.e., remains universal) while a particular does not convert at all, similarly in contraposition a universal affirmative converts as itself (remains universal) while a particular affirmative does not convert at all. He provides the example: 'Some animal is not human' is true, but its contrapositive 'Some human is not animal' is false, so particular affirmative contraposition fails. He then discusses modal propositions: nine specific modalities (including the two absolute temporals, the two temporals, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute general) do not convert, while the remaining ones (the two perpetual and the two general) do convert according to the rules previously given for negatives in simple conversion. He notes that the rule for negatives here is like the rule for affirmatives in simple conversion: the negative universal contraposits as universal, but the negative particular does not contraposit as particular because the contradictory of the predicate might be more general than the subject, and negating a more specific contradictory from the whole of a more general subject is invalid. He gives the example: 'Nothing of humanity is without animal' is true, but its contrapositive 'Nothing of animal is without humanity' is false because some animal is not human (e.g., a horse). The commentary then explains that the demonstration for these conversion rules is through reductio (khalf), similar to how the rules for simple conversion are established. The author then delves into the special case of the two special modalities (al-khassatan: the wujudiya and the dawamiya? Actually, in this context, 'khassatan' refers to the particular affirmative and particular negative of the special modalities that do convert via the method of hypothesis (ifitiraḍ). He explains the conversion of the special modalities from the particular negative in simple conversion to the 'urfiyya khassa (special universal) using a hypothetical example: if 'some J is not B as long as J is not always' is true, then 'some B is not J as long as B is not always' is true. The proof involves positing an individual D that is J and is B actually, then showing that D is not J as long as B, which gives the converse. Similarly, for the particular affirmative in contraposition: if 'some J is B as long as J is not always' is true, then its contrapositive 'some of what is not B is not J as long as it is not B not always' is true. This passage is a careful refinement of the rules of contraposition, clarifying the exceptions and providing detailed proofs.

العكس المستوى
الخاصتان

Conversion of Contradiction for Particular Propositions: The Method of Hypothesis
Proof of inversion of special cases in 'aks al-naqīḍ via hypothesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of hypothesis (iftirāḍ) to prove the validity of 'aks al-naqīḍ for particular negative and particular affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
The method of hypothesis reveals the hidden structure of conversion for particular propositions.

In classical Islamic logic, certain particular propositions are exceptions to the rule that particular propositions do not convert. This lesson explores how scholars using the method of hypothesis (iftirāḍ) prove that both the particular negative and the particular affirmative do indeed convert in the conversion of contradiction. You will learn the step-by-step reasoning that demonstrates this counterintuitive result.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
The passage deals with two special cases (al-khāṣṣatān): the particular negative (e.g., 'some J is not B as long as J is not always') and the particular affirmative (e.g., 'some J is B as long as J is not always'). Recognize that these are exceptions to the general rule that particular propositions do not convert in 'aks al-naqīḍ.
The temporal qualifier 'as long as…not always' indicates non-perpetuity (lā dawām).
2
Understand the method of hypothesis (iftirāḍ)
The proof assumes a specific individual D that instantiates the subject term. For example, from 'some J is not B' we pick a D that is J. This allows reasoning about times when D has or lacks certain properties.
The hypothesis is not arbitrary; it must satisfy the truth conditions of the original proposition.
3
Follow the proof for the particular negative
Assume the original: 'some J is not B as long as J is not always'. Let D be that J. By the original, D is B in actuality (since 'not always' implies at some times it is B). Also D is J in actuality. Then we show that D is not J as long as B, by reductio. This yields the first part of the converse: 'some B is not J as long as B is not always'.
The second part of the converse is simply the original assumed fact that D is B in actuality.
4
Follow the proof for the particular affirmative
Assume the original: 'some J is B as long as J is not always'. Let D be that J. By the original, D is not B in actuality (since 'not always' implies at some times it is not B). Also D is J in actuality. Then we show that D is not J as long as not B. This yields the first part of the converse: 'some non-B is not J as long as non-B is not always'. The second part is that D is not B in actuality.
The proof mirrors the negative case but with negation.
5
Compare the two proofs
Both proofs rely on the hypothesis method and the notion of non-perpetuity (lā dawām). The symmetry shows that both special cases are valid. Note that the converse is also a special conditional proposition, not a simple categorical.
This technique is foundational for handling temporal qualifications in immediate inferences.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof from a hashiya (super-commentary) on logic, specifically addressing the conversion of contradiction ('aks al-naqid) for particular propositions. The author is explaining the inversion of two special cases (al-khāṣṣatān): the particular affirmative and the particular negative. The passage first states the topic, then provides separate proofs for each using the method of hypothesis (iftirāḍ). The commentary begins by clarifying the meaning of 'the contradiction is the contradiction' — i.e., the absence of entailment here is the same as that discussed elsewhere. Then it notes that the universal conversion was established by reductio (khalf), and here it is similar.

The first proof handles the particular negative. Suppose it is true with necessity or perpetuity that 'some J is not B as long as J is not always,' meaning some J is actually B (since 'as long as J is not always' indicates that at some times it is not B). Then we want to show the converse: 'some B is not J as long as B is not always.' The proof proceeds by assuming a particular entity D that is that 'some J' (the subject). By the ruling of the original (non-perpetuity), D is actually B. Also, D is J in actuality because the attributive description 'J' applies to it. Thus we have that D is both J and B, but the original claim said that some J is not B perpetually? Actually careful: The original claim is that some J is not B as long as J is not always — this means that during the time J exists, it is not always B. So there are times when that J is not B. The hypothesis picks a specific instance D that is J and when it is B? The proof is intricate. The author then shows that the converse 'some B is not J as long as B is not always' holds by constructing the two parts: first, D is not J as long as B (since at the time it is B, it might not be J), and second, D is B and J at different times. The conclusion is that the conversion is valid.

The second proof is for the particular affirmative in the conversion of contradiction. It starts similarly: if 'some J is B as long as J is not always' is true, meaning some J is not B in actuality, then we want to show that 'some of what is not B is not J as long as it is not B is not always.' The proof uses a similar hypothesis method, assuming D is that particular J which is not B. Then D is not J as long as it is not B, etc. The passage also includes a marginal gloss repeating the hypothesis method for clarity.

Overall, the passage demonstrates the technical method of proving conversion rules for quantified propositions in Aristotelian logic, using the technique of hypothesis (iftirāḍ) and reductio. The examples are purely logical with variables, but the context is the Islamic logical tradition where such conversions are used in syllogistic reasoning.

Proof of Conversion of Universal Negative
Proof of the conversion of the universal negative using hypothetical positing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof of the conversion of the universal negative proposition using hypothetical positing (iftirāḍ) and the rule of permanence (dawām al-aṣl).

Core Idea
The converse of 'No J is B' is proved by positing an individual and using temporal permanence.

In this lesson, we examine a refined proof for the 'aks (converse) of the universal negative proposition. The author, Yazdi, introduces a hypothetical positing (iftirāḍ) to test the logical consequences of the original proposition. This proof is a critical refinement that clarifies the role of temporal permanence in immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The universal negative is 'No J is B'. It is assumed to hold for all times, i.e., any J is always not B.
2
Understand the hypothesis (iftirāḍ)
We posit a specific individual d from the subject class J. So we assume 'd is J actually' and 'd is not B actually' due to the permanence of the original.
This is a hypothetical instantiation, not a real individual.
3
Derive an intermediate conclusion
From the hypothesis, it follows that 'some of what is not B is J actually' (d is J). This is necessary for the converse because affirmation of the converse requires negation of negation.
4
Derive the first part of the converse
The author argues that 'd is not J as long as it is not B'. Otherwise, if d were J at some time when it is not B, it would contradict the original rule that d is B as long as it is J.
This is the first part of the converse: 'some of what is not B is not J as long as it is not B'.
5
Complete the converse
The second part of the converse is the conclusion that the converse itself holds. The author states that the converse is established with both its parts.
The full converse of 'No J is B' is 'Some of what is not B is not J as long as it is not B'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الافتراض و هو أن يفرض ذات الموضوع أعني بعض ج، د. فـ «د» ج بالفعل على مذهب الشيخ و هو التحقيق و د ليس ب بالفعل بحكم دوام الأصل. فصدق بعض ما ليس ب ج بالفعل. و هو ملزوم لدوام العكس لأن الإثبات يلزمه نفي النفي. ثم نقول د ليس ج مادام ليس ب و إلا كان ج في بعض أوقات كونه ليس ب فيكون ليس ب في بعض أوقات كونه ج كما مر. و قد كان حكم الأصل أنه ب مادام ج. فصدق أن بعض ما ليس ب و هو د ليس ج مادام ليس ب و هو الجزء الأول من العكس فيثبت العكس بكلا جزئيه.
The hypothesis is that one posits the essence of the subject, meaning some of ج is د. So 'د is ج actually' according to the doctrine of the Shaykh and it is the verified view, and 'د is not ب actually' by virtue of the permanence of the original. So it is true that 'some of what is not ب is ج actually'. And this is necessary for the permanence of the converse because affirmation necessitates the negation of negation. Then we say: 'د is not ج as long as it is not ب'; otherwise it would be ج at some times of its being not ب, so it becomes not ب at some times of its being ج, as previously. And the rule of the original was that 'it is ب as long as it is ج'. So it is true that 'some of what is not ب — and it is د — is not ج as long as it is not ب', and that is the first part of the converse. Thus the converse is established with both of its parts.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) of the standard proof for the conversion (aks) of the universal negative proposition (al-salib al-kullī). The author, Yazdi, is commenting on earlier proofs and clarifying the reasoning. He begins by stating the hypothesis (iftirāḍ): we posit a specific individual (dhāt al-mawḍūʿ) which is some of ج, namely د. So we assume that د is ج actually (bil-fiʿl), following the doctrine of the Shaykh (Ibn Sīnā), and that د is not ب actually, based on the rule of permanence (dawām) of the original proposition (the universal negative). The original proposition is 'No ج is ب', which by its permanence implies that any individual of ج is always not ب. Therefore, from this hypothesis, it follows truly that 'some of what is not ب is ج actually' (i.e., د is ج). This conclusion is necessary (malzūm) for the permanence of the converse, because affirmation (ithbāt) entails the negation of negation (nafy al-nafy). The author then proceeds to derive the first part of the converse. He says: 'د is not ج as long as it is not ب'; otherwise, if it were ج at some times when it is not ب, then by the original rule, it would be not ب at some times when it is ج, which would contradict the original. So the first part of the converse is: 'some of what is not ب – and it is د – is not ج as long as it is not ب'. This is the first part of the converse. The second part is alluded to, and the author concludes that the converse is established with both parts. This is a technical proof that relies on the rule of permanence (dawām al-aṣl) and the logical principle that affirmation entails negation of negation.

Distinguishing Hasr from Ikhtisas in Fronting
Author critiques the claim that fronting a dependent always yields hasr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hasr (exclusion) and ikhtisas (specification) in fronted constructions and apply al-Subki's definitions.

Core Idea
Hasr negates all alternatives; ikhtisas singles out without negating.

In Arabic grammar and logic, fronting a grammatical element often signals emphasis. But does it always mean that everything else is excluded? Scholars debate this. In this lesson, we unpack the critical distinction between hasr and ikhtisas as presented by Taqi al-Din al-Subki and cited by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Find the terms 'hasr' (الحصر) and 'ikhtisas' (الاختصاص) in the passage. Note how the author says many people confuse them.
The passage begins by referencing a common misunderstanding.
2
Extract al-Subki's definitions
Locate the quote attributed to Taqi al-Din al-Subki. Write down exactly how he defines hasr and ikhtisas.
Hasr involves negation of the unmentioned; ikhtisas does not.
3
Apply the definitions to an example
Consider the Quranic verse 'ونوحًا هدينا' (And Noah We guided). Is this hasr or ikhtisas? Use al-Subki's definitions to decide.
The verse singles out Noah after mentioning guidance for all, so it is ikhtisas, not hasr.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Why is it important to keep these concepts separate? What confusion arises if they are conflated?
Conflation can lead to misinterpretation of texts that use fronting for emphasis rather than exclusivity.

قوله تقديم الظرف لقصد الحصر: قداشتهر كلام الناس في ان تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص وقديفهم كثير من الناس من الاختصاص الحصر وليس كذلك و انما الاختصاص شيء والحصر شيء آخر والفضلاء لم يذكرون ذلك الحصر وانما اعتبروا بالاختصاص. والفرق بينهما ان الحصر نفى غير المذكور و اثبات المذكور والاختصاص قصد الخارجي من جهة خصوصه من غير تعرض لنفى غيره قاله التقى الدين السبكي. و قوله الفضلا لم يذكروا في ذلك لفظ الحصر، برده ما قاله المحشى في هذا المقام و غير واحد من ذوى الافهام و ما ذكره من التفرقة بين الحصر والاختصاص خلاف المشهور ، فان المشهور انها مترادفان، ثم ادعاء الاختصاص في تقديم المعمول ليس كلياً بل اغلبى فاعتراض ابن الحديد بقوله تعالى: «كلا هديناه ونوحاً هدينا ليس بشيء... (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: 'Fronting the adverb for the purpose of hasr (exclusion). It is well-known among people that fronting the dependent (al-ma'mul) indicates ikhtisas (specification), and many people understand from ikhtisas hasr, but that is not so. Rather, ikhtisas is one thing and hasr is another. The scholars (al-fudala') did not mention hasr in that context; they only considered ikhtisas. The difference between them is that hasr negates what is not mentioned and affirms what is mentioned, while ikhtisas is the intention of the external thing from the aspect of its specificity without addressing the negation of others. This was stated by Taqi al-Din al-Subki. And his saying: 'The scholars did not mention the word hasr in that context' is rebutted by what the commentator (al-Muhashshi) said in this context and by many of those with understanding. And what he mentioned of the distinction between hasr and ikhtisas contradicts the well-known view, for the well-known is that they are synonymous. Moreover, the claim that ikhtisas is indicated by fronting the dependent is not universal but applies in most cases. So the objection of Ibn al-Hadid by His saying: 'We guided all, and Noah We guided' is nothing. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the rhetorical effects of fronting (taqdim) grammatical elements. The author responds to a previous claim that fronting a dependent element (such as an adverb) indicates hasr (exclusion, i.e., that the mentioned item alone is the case). He explains that the common view among people is that fronting indicates ikhtisas (specification), but some confuse ikhtisas with hasr. The author, relying on Taqi al-Din al-Subki, distinguishes: hasr negates everything else, while ikhtisas only singles out the item without necessarily negating others. For example, 'We guided all' and then singling out Noah does not negate guidance for others. The author then rebuts the earlier commentator who claimed that scholars did not mention hasr in this context. He says that many scholars, including the commentator himself elsewhere, have discussed hasr. He also notes that the distinction between hasr and ikhtisas is not universally accepted; the well-known position is that they are synonymous. Finally, he addresses an objection from Ibn al-Hadid using the Qur'anic verse 'We guided all, and Noah We guided' — which seems to both specify and exclude? But the author dismisses the objection as baseless.

Key Terms
الحصر
الاختصاص
تقديم المعمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Subki as cited in the passage, how does ikhtisas differ from hasr?
A) Ikhtisas requires negation of the unmentioned; hasr does not.
B) Ikhtisas specifies without negation; hasr negates all others.
C) Both terms are identical in meaning.
D) Ikhtisas is a type of hasr.

Key Takeaway
Hasr and ikhtisas are distinct: hasr excludes all alternatives, ikhtisas only singles out the mentioned item.

Critique of the Claim that Fronting Yields Hasr
Author critiques the claim that fronting a dependent always yields hasr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's critique of the claim that fronting always yields hasr, and evaluate the dismissal of Ibn al-Hadid's objection.

Core Idea
Fronting indicates ikhtisas, not necessarily hasr; scholarly consensus differs.

The previous lesson established the distinction between hasr and ikhtisas. Now we delve into the author's critical rebuttal of a commentator who insisted that scholars never mentioned hasr in this context. We also examine the dismissal of Ibn al-Hadid's Qur'anic counterexample. This lesson sharpens your ability to navigate scholarly disagreements on logical-grammatical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Find the commentator's claim that the author rejects. The passage says 'الفضلاء لم يذكروا في ذلك لفظ الحصر' – the scholars did not mention the word hasr in this context.
The author rebuts this by pointing out that the commentator himself and others have discussed hasr.
2
Extract the author's counterargument
What reasons does the author give to reject the commentator's claim? He mentions that the commentator's own remarks elsewhere and 'many of those with understanding' contradict it.
The author also notes that the distinction between hasr and ikhtisas is against the well-known view that they are synonyms.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Hadid's objection
Ibn al-Hadid uses the verse 'كلا هديناه ونوحاً هدينا' (We guided all, and Noah We guided) to argue that fronting 'Noah' after a universal statement must indicate hasr. How does the author respond?
The author dismisses it as 'nothing' – likely because the verse is an example of ikhtisas, not hasr.
4
Assess the overall critique
Do you think the author's rebuttal is convincing? Why might the well-known view (synonymy) be more widely accepted?
Consider the function of fronting in context and the risk of overgeneralizing hasr.

The Four Relations and Their Domain: Universals Only
Refining the domain of the four relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the four relations (tasawi, umum mutlaq, umum min wajh, tabayun) only hold between two universals, and refute the objection that partial opposition makes the division non-exhaustive.

Core Idea
The four relations are exhaustive only for universal–universal pairs; other pairs reduce to fewer relations.

Why do logic manuals specify 'two universals' (الكليان) when defining the four relations between concepts? This passage refines the scope: the four relations fully apply only when both terms are universals. For two particulars only opposition is possible, and for a universal and particular only opposition and absolute generality occur. The passage also addresses an objection about partial opposition (مباينة جزئية) supposedly being a fifth relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about 'two universals'
The author states that the four relations are considered between two universals, not between any two concepts. The word 'الكليان' (the two universals) is used inclusively to cover all universals, but only those with external referents.
The base text originally used 'الكليان'; the commentator explains why.
2
Understand the rationale for limiting to universals
If the text said 'two concepts', one might erroneously think the four relations apply to two particulars or a universal-particular pair. For two particulars (e.g., 'Zayd' and 'Amr'), only opposition exists. For a universal and a particular (e.g., 'Human' and 'Zayd'), only opposition and absolute generality (the universal is broader or opposite) are possible. Thus the four relations are only fully realized when both are universals.
Example: Two universals 'Human' and 'Animal' have partial generality (umum min wajh).
3
Refine the notion of opposition
The commentator notes that if 'opposition' means logical opposition (mutually exclusive in co-extensiveness), two particulars are not true opposites because their negatives are singular propositions, not universal. If opposition is taken broadly (existential separation), the four relations could apply to other pairs, but the primary purpose is the study of universals.
This refinement strengthens the original argument by clarifying what counts as opposition.
4
Address the objection about partial opposition
Objectors claim that 'partial opposition' (مباينة جزئية) is a relation not among the four, so the division is non-exhaustive. The answer: partial opposition is not a relation between two universals; it holds between a universal and a particular (e.g., 'Human' and 'White' as a particular instance). Hence it does not belong in the classification meant for universal pairs.
Thus the four relations are indeed exhaustive for all pairs of universals.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'i.e. two universals' means that the word 'the two universals' in his statement is for universal scope, so it includes all universals. But the discussion is only about universals that have external referents, as will be indicated. Then the four relations are considered between two universals, not between two concepts, because concepts are either two universals, two particulars, or a universal and a particular. If he said 'two concepts', it might be imagined that all types of relations apply to each of the three divisions, but that is not so. Rather, they occur only between two universals, meaning there are two specific universals between which there is opposition, and other two between which there is equality, etc. As for two particulars, only opposition occurs between them. As for a particular and a universal, only opposition and absolute generality occur, because if the particular is a particular of that universal, it is absolutely more specific; if not, it is opposite. So when he said 'the two universals', it is known that the case of the other two divisions is not like that. This is understood from the statements of some. However, the truth is that the reason for specification is to indicate that the primary purpose is to know the states of relations of universals with each other. As for the claim that two particulars only have opposition, if what is meant is opposition in terms of co-extensiveness (as is correct), we do not concede that they are opposites, for the reference of opposition (as the commentator will mention) is to two universal negatives, and the two negatives derived from two particulars are singular. Similarly, for the universal and particular. And if what is meant is that opposition is more general, whether in terms of co-extensiveness or in terms of existence, then we do not concede that two particulars cannot be other than opposites, because the four relations would then run between them and between the universal and particular. Commentary
This passage explains why the text specifies 'two universals' (الكليان) instead of 'two concepts' (المفهومان). The reason is that the four relations (equality, inclusion, mutual opposition, partial opposition) apply only to universals. If 'two concepts' were used, one might assume the relations apply also to two particulars or a universal-particular pair. For two particulars, only opposition exists; for a universal and particular, only opposition and absolute generality. The commentator then refines: if 'opposition' means logical opposition (mutually exclusive in co-extensiveness), then two particulars are not necessarily opposites, as their negatives are singular, not universal. If 'opposition' is broader (including existential separation), then the four relations can still be considered. Finally, the passage addresses an objection that partial opposition (مباينة جزئية) is not among the four relations, making the division non-exhaustive. The answer: the four relations are only between two universals, and partial opposition as a relation between two concepts does not exist as such; it would be a relation between a universal and a particular, not between two universals.

How Hypothetical Truth Resolves the 'Nothing' and 'Impossible' Problem
Author presents objection and resolution for empty universals using hypothetical truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of hypothetical truth (sidq farḍī) to demonstrate that two empty universals can have the relation of equality under assumption.

Core Idea
Hypothetical truth saves the four relations for universals with no instances.

In Islamic logic, every two universals must have one of four relations. But what about 'nothing' and 'impossible'? They are universals yet have no instances. This lesson shows how logicians solved this puzzle by extending truth to hypothetical cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Recognize that 'nothing' (la shay') and 'impossible' (la mumkin) are universal concepts with no real instances. Under the standard four relations (equality, complete difference, absolute generality, generality from one aspect), they appear to have no relation because none of the criteria are met.
The objector argues that since these two have no individuals, they cannot be predicated of anything, so no relation holds.
2
Examine the First Attempted Answer
Consider restricting the rule to universals that have some actual instances in reality. This would exclude 'nothing' and 'impossible', but it contradicts the principle that the negatives of equal terms are equal, because 'thing' and 'possible' are equal yet their negatives ('nothing' and 'impossible') are not equal.
The first answer fails because it violates a core rule.
3
Explore the Preferred Answer: Hypothetical Truth
Instead of restricting the rule, extend the notion of truth (sidq) to include hypothetical truth (sidq farḍī). Under this extended sense, we assume that if something were true of 'nothing', it would be true of 'impossible', and vice versa. Thus, they are equal under hypothesis.
This preserves the universality of the rule without contradiction.
4
Understand the Digression on Truth
The passage clarifies that the four relations apply to terms (based on predication, using 'on') and to propositions (based on correspondence, using 'in'). Hypothetical truth works for both, but the focus here is on terms.
This digression shows the technical precision of the solution.
5
Apply the Concept
Test with another pair of empty universals, like 'resurrection' and 'day of judgment'. Under hypothetical truth, they become equal because anyone assumed to be resurrected is assumed to face judgment, and vice versa.
This confirms the solution's applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الكليات الفرضية التي ليس لها أفراد في نفس الأمر لا في الخارج ولا في الذهن لكن هذا ينافي كلية قولهم: إن نقيضي المتساويين متساويان كما سيأتي فإن بين الشيء والممكن المذكور تساوياً وليس بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاشيء واللاممكن تساو على ما ذكر. فالأولى في الجواب: أن يذهب إلى تعميم الصدق المعتبر في حدود النسب الأربع إلى الصدق الحقيقي والفرضي ولا شك أن الصدق الكلي الفرضي متحقق بين اللاشيء واللاممكن من الطرفين ضرورة بمعنى: أن كل ما فرض أنه يصدق عليه اللاشيء، يصدق عليه اللاممكن وبالعكس. ثم لا يخفي: أن النسب الأربع كما تحقق في المفردات وما في حكمها من المركبات التقييدية، كذلك تحقق في القضايا، إلا أنها تلاحظ في المفردات بحسب الصدق أي الحمل على شيء كما ستعرف أنها وفي القضايا بحسب الصدق أي التحقق والوجود في الواقع ونفس الأمر بمعنى مطابقتها له والصدق بمعنى الحمل يستعمل به «على» فيقال مثلاً: الحيوان صادق على الإنسان أي محمول عليه وبمعنى التحقق والوجود يستعمل بـ «في» فيقال مثلاً: صدقت هذه القضية في الواقع ونفس الأمر والصدق بمعنى الحمل على شيء لا يتصور في القضايا، ضرورة أن قولنا مثلاً: زيد ضارب من حيث هو هو لا يحمل على مفرد ولا على قضية كما أنه بمعنى التحقق والوجود أي المطابقة لنفس الأمر لا يتصور في المفردات وما في حكمها، أما فيها فظاهر وأما فيما هو في حكمها فلما سيأتي في تعريف القضية إن شاء الله تعالى لكن لما كان المقصود منها هنا ما يكون بحسب الصدق والحمل لا مطلقا خصها بالكليات فلا محذور (ميرزا محمد علي) (١٤) ينتقض ذلك بمثل اللاشيء واللاممكن بالإمكان، فاللاشيء لا يصدق شيء منها على شيء من أفراد اللاممكن وبالعكس فلو جعلوهما متباينين لوجب أن يكون بين نقيضيها تباين جزئي على ما سيأتي وهو باطل. ويمكن أن يجاب بتخصيص الدعوى بالكليات التي لها مصداق في الخارج وتعميم القواعد إنما يجب
There remains something: that the judgment that every two universals must have one of the four relations between them is not without weakness, for 'nothing' and 'impossible by general possibility' are two universal concepts, and there is not between them any of the four relations. As for that there is not between them complete difference, because the two completely different things, as will come, must have between their negatives a partial difference, and 'thing' and 'possible' are absolutely equal. As for that there is not between them equality, because they do not apply to anything at all so that they would coincide. As for that there is not between them absolute generality or generality from one aspect, because, as will come, the specific of the general must apply along with the negative of the more specific, and neither of them applies along with the negative of the other, which would entail the conjunction of contradictories. It can be answered: that the meaning is that every two universals each of which applies to something in reality, one of the four relations must be realized between them. And it does not harm that it is said that the rules of this art should be universal and comprehensive, for generalizing rules is only obligatory according to the purposes sought from this art, and they have no purpose in hypothetical universals that have no individuals in reality, neither externally nor in the mind. But this contradicts the universality of their statement: 'The negatives of equal terms are equal' as will come, for between 'thing' and the mentioned 'possible' there is equality, but between their two negatives, which are 'nothing' and 'impossible', there is not equality according to what was mentioned. So the better answer is: to extend the truth considered in the definitions of the four relations to real truth and hypothetical truth, and there is no doubt that the universal hypothetical truth is realized between 'nothing' and 'impossible' from both sides necessarily, meaning: everything that is assumed to be true of 'nothing' is true of 'impossible', and conversely. Then it is not hidden: that the four relations, just as they are realized in terms and what is in their ruling of restrictive compounds, also are realized in propositions, except that they are considered in terms according to truth i.e., predication on something, as you will know, and in propositions according to truth i.e., realization and existence in reality and the actual state of affairs, meaning their correspondence to it. And truth in the sense of predication is used with 'on', so one says e.g., 'animal is true of human', i.e., predicated. And in the sense of realization and existence it is used with 'in', so one says e.g., 'this proposition is true in reality and the actual state of affairs'. And truth in the sense of predication on something is not conceivable in propositions, necessarily, for our statement e.g., 'Zayd is hitting' as such is not predicated on a term or a proposition. Just as it in the sense of realization and existence i.e., correspondence to the actual state of affairs is not conceivable in terms and what is in their ruling. As for terms, it is apparent, and as for what is in their ruling, it will come in the definition of proposition, God willing. But since what is intended here is what is according to truth and predication, not absolutely, he specified it to universals, so no problem. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (14) This is refuted by the like of 'nothing' and 'impossible by possibility', for 'nothing' is not true of anything from the individuals of 'impossible', and conversely; so if they made them completely different, there would have to be partial difference between their negatives, as will come, which is false. And it can be answered: by restricting the claim to universals that have instances in the external, and generalizing the rules is only obligatory.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage comprises two related objections and answers. The first objection (the 'remaining thing') argues that the four relations do not apply to 'nothing' (la shay') and 'impossible by general possibility' (la mumkin) because they are universals but have no instances, so they cannot be said to have any of the four specific relations. The objector supports this by showing that each relation fails for them. The answer first tries to restrict the rule to universals that have actual instances, but that contradicts the rule that the negatives of equal terms are equal, because 'thing' and 'possible' are equal but their negatives are not equal. So the preferred answer is to extend the notion of truth (sidq) to include hypothetical truth: if we assume something is true of 'nothing', it is true of 'impossible' and vice versa, so they are equal under hypothetical truth. The passage then digresses to explain that the four relations apply both to terms (according to predication) and to propositions (according to correspondence), clarifying the usage of sidq in each context. The second objection (14) returns to the same example: 'nothing' and 'impossible' are not equal in predication, so they cannot be said to be 'different' because that would require a partial difference between their negatives, which is false. The answer given is to restrict the claim to universals with external instances, which resolves the issue. The passage thus shows the ongoing refinement of logical rules to handle edge cases.

Key Terms
اللاشيء
اللاممكن بالإمكان العام
الصدق الفرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best way to handle the four relations for 'nothing' and 'impossible'?
A) Restrict the rule to universals with actual instances.
B) Use hypothetical truth to show they are equal under assumption.
C) Deny that they are universals.
D) Ignore the problem as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The four relations hold for all universals, including empty ones, when truth is understood as including hypothetical predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universals 'resurrection' and 'day of judgment'. Do they have any of the four relations? Use hypothetical truth to determine if they are equal under assumption. Explain your reasoning.
?

Refining the Four Relations: Hypothetical Truth
Author raises objection and gives two solutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze an objection to the four-relations principle and evaluate two responses, including the concept of hypothetical truth.

Core Idea
Even 'nothing' and 'not-possible' have a relation if we consider hypothetical truth.

The principle that every two universals have one of four relations seems obvious, but the concepts 'nothing' and 'not-possible' pose a challenge. In this passage, the author presents the objection and two solutions, the second of which introduces a crucial distinction: hypothetical truth. Let's see how logical relations can apply even to concepts with no actual instances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the counterexample: 'nothing' (اللاشيء) and 'not-possible in the general sense' (اللاممكن بالامكان العام). Why does this pair seem to break the rule that every two universals have one of the four relations?
The objection argues that none of the four relations hold.
2
Examine the first answer
Find the first solution: restrict the principle to universals that have actual instances in reality. The author says 'what is meant is that between every two universals each of which is true of something in reality, one of the four relations must hold'.
This answer limits the scope to avoid the counterexample.
3
Identify the problem with the first answer
The author points out a contradiction: the principle that 'the contradictories of two equal things are equal' is violated. 'Thing' and 'possible' are equal, but their contradictories 'nothing' and 'not-possible' are not equal. So the first answer is inconsistent.
This shows the first answer fails because it conflicts with another established rule.
4
Understand the second answer
The author proposes to generalize the meaning of 'truth' in the four relations to include hypothetical truth (صدق فرضى). Even if 'nothing' and 'not-possible' have no actual instances, they can be considered true of the same hypothetical individuals, thus satisfying a relation (equality).
This preserves the principle without restricting its scope.
5
Apply the concept
Think of another pair of universals that might only be connected through hypothetical truth, such as 'eternal' and 'not-eternal' in Kalam discussions about the attributes of Allah.
This helps solidify the utility of hypothetical truth in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى هنا شيء وهو ان الحكم بان كل كليين لابد ان يتحقق بينها احدى النسب الاربع، لا يخلو عن ضعف، فان اللاشيء واللاممكن بالامكان العام مفهومان كليان وليس بينهما واحدة من النسب الأربع، أما ان ليس بينهما تباين كلي فلان المتباينين على ما سيأتى يجب ان يكون بين نقيضيهما تباين جزئى والشيء و الممكن متساويان البتة و اما ان ليس بينهما تساو، فلانها لا يصدقان على شيء اصلاً حتى يتصادقان و اما ان ليس بينهما عموم مطلق ولا من وجه فلما سيأتى من ان عين العام يجب ان يصدق مع نقيض الاخص ولا يصدق شيء منهما مع نقيض الآخر والالزم اجتماع النقيضين. ويمكن ان يجاب بان المراد ان كل كليين يصدق كل واحد منهما على شيء من الاشياء في نفس الأمر لابد ان يتحقق بينهما احدى النسب الاربع ولاينا فى ذلك بما ذكروا من انه يجب ان يكون قواعد هذا الفن عامة شاملة فان تعميم القواعد انما يجب بحسب الاغراض المطلوبة من هذا الفن ولاغرض لهم في : الكليات الفرضية التي ليس لها افراد فى نفس الأمر لا فى الخارج ولا في الذهن لكن هذا ينا في كلية قولهم: ان نقيضى المتساويين متساويان كما سيأتى فان بين الشيء والممكن المذكور تساوياً وليس بين نقيضيهما و هما اللاشيء واللا ممكن تساو على ما ذكر. فالاولى في الجواب : ان يذهب الى تعميم الصدق المعتبر في حدود النسب الاربع الى الصدق الحقيقي والفرضى ولاشك ان الصدق الكلى الفرضى متحقق بين اللاشيء واللاممكن من الطرفين ضرورة بمعنى: ان كل ما فرض انه يصدق عليه اللاشيء، يصدق عليه اللاممكن وبالعكس.
There remains a thing: the judgment that between every two universals one of the four relations must hold is not free from weakness. For 'nothing' and 'not-possible in the general sense' are two universal concepts and between them there is none of the four relations. As for there not being total exclusion between them, because for two totally exclusive things, as will come, there must be partial exclusion between their contradictories, and 'thing' and 'possible' are completely equal. As for there not being equality, because they are not true of anything at all such that they would be coextensive. As for there not being absolute inclusion or inclusion from one aspect, because, as will come, the essence of the universal must be true with the contradictory of the more specific, and neither of them is true with the contradictory of the other, which would entail the conjunction of contradictories. And it can be answered that what is meant is that between every two universals each of which is true of something in reality, one of the four relations must hold, and this is not contradicted by what they mentioned that the rules of this art must be general and comprehensive, for the generalization of rules is only required according to the purposes sought from this art, and they have no purpose concerning hypothetical universals that have no individuals in reality, neither externally nor mentally. But this contradicts the universality of their statement: 'The contradictories of two equal things are equal' as will come, for between 'thing' and 'possible' there is equality, but between their contradictories, 'nothing' and 'not-possible', there is no equality as mentioned. So the best answer is to generalize the truth considered in the limits of the four relations to real and hypothetical truth, and there is no doubt that universal hypothetical truth is realized between 'nothing' and 'not-possible' from both sides necessarily, meaning: everything that is supposed to be true of 'nothing' is true of 'not-possible', and vice versa.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange: an objection and two responses regarding the claim that every two universals must have one of the four logical relations (total exclusion, equality, absolute inclusion, inclusion from one aspect). The objection uses the counterexample of 'nothing' and 'not-possible', which appear to have no relation. The first response restricts the claim to universals that have actual instances, but this is criticized because it contradicts another principle about contradictories of equal terms. The second response generalizes the notion of 'truth' (صدق) to include hypothetical truth: even if 'nothing' and 'not-possible' have no actual instances, they can be considered true of the same hypothetical individuals, thus satisfying the requirement. The author follows the order: first stating the objection, then giving the first answer with a critique, and finally proposing the better answer of hypothetical truth.

Key Terms
النسب الأربع
صدق فرضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Restricting the principle to universals with actual instances.
B) Generalizing truth to include hypothetical truth.
C) Abandoning the four relations entirely.
D) Rejecting the concept of 'nothing' as a universal.

Key Takeaway
The four relations can be extended to hypothetical universals by considering hypothetical truth, solving the counterexample of 'nothing' and 'not-possible'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universals 'eternal' (قديم) and 'not-eternal' (حادث). Do they have one of the four relations? Apply the principle with hypothetical truth if needed.

Understanding Equality: Asleep and Awake
Clarifies equality with asleep/awake example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two universals are equal by checking the condition of non-simultaneous truth.

Core Idea
Equality requires each universal to be true of all individuals of the other, not necessarily at the same time.

The relation of equality between two universals is often misunderstood as requiring simultaneous truth. The example of 'asleep' and 'awake' illustrates that equality holds even when the two cannot be true of the same individual at once. This distinction is crucial for correctly classifying relations in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Take the pair 'asleep' and 'awake' as given. Note that they seem mutually exclusive temporally.
These are terms analyzed in the passage.
2
Recall the definition of equality
Equality (tasāwī) means each universal is true of every individual of the other. Check if 'asleep' is true of all individuals of 'awake' and vice versa.
The definition is given in the commentary.
3
Notice the temporal issue
At any given moment, no one is both asleep and awake simultaneously. So if equality required simultaneous truth, these would not be equal.
4
Apply the clarification
Equality does not require simultaneous truth. It requires that each is true of all individuals of the other at the time the other is true of that individual. For example, a sleeping person is 'awake in general' (i.e., they have the capacity to be awake).
The text says: 'each is true of all individuals of the other at the time when the other is true of it.'
5
Verify with the example
Every individual of 'asleep' is an individual of 'awake' in the sense of ability (in general), and every individual of 'awake' is an individual of 'asleep' in general. Thus they are equal.
The passage offers two interpretations: absolute equality and equality of 'in general' terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
