(Mirza Muhammad Ali) (14) This is refuted by the likes of 'nothing' and 'not-possible in the general sense', for 'nothing' is such that none of its individuals is true of any individual of 'not-possible' and vice versa. If they were made totally exclusive, there would have to be partial exclusion between their contradictories, as will come, which is false. And it can be answered by restricting the claim to universals that have a reference in external reality, and the generalization of rules is only required in the four relations according to human capacity and the purposes sought from the art, and they have no purpose concerning hypothetical universals. (Abd al-Rahim) (15) That is, each of them is more general in one aspect, in that it includes it and others in some way, and more specific in another aspect, in that it is included in the other while also including others. (Muhammad Ali) (16) 'Animal' includes 'white' and others and vice versa. So in consideration that each of them includes the other, it is more general than it, and in consideration that it is included in it, it is more specific than it; for this reason it is called 'the more general and more specific from one aspect'. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (17) If you say: 'Asleep' and 'awake' are equal and there is no universal truth from both sides. I say: What is considered in equals is that each of them is true of all individuals of the other, and it does not follow from that that they are true together at one time. So 'awake' and 'asleep' are each universally true of the other, except that this is not at one time, and that does not harm their being equals. And some said that equality is only between 'asleep in general' and 'awake in general'; the sleeper during sleep is true of him that he is awake in general, even if he is not true of him that he is awake during sleep; similarly, the awake person during his wakefulness is true of him that he is asleep in general. And equals are such that each is true of all individuals of the other at the time when the other is true of it. And measure on that the truth considered in absolute inclusion and inclusion from one aspect. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (18) 'Al-marji‘' with kasra of the jim here is a verbal noun (masdar mīmī), not a place noun, and it is valid to be transitive with 'ila'. If you say that the words of the author in his commentary on al-Talkhīṣ indicate that 'al-marji‘' is a place noun and is validly transitive with 'ila' – as he said in explaining the author's statement: 'and eloquence, its reference (marji‘) is to avoidance of mistakes, which is what must be achieved so that it can be obtained, as they said: the reference (marji‘) of truth and falsehood is to the conformity of judgment to reality or its non-conformity, i.e., that by which they are realized and obtained' – I say: There is no doubt that a place noun cannot be transitive with anything because it is purely inert, not fit for governing. As for the author's words, it can be on the way of usage (istikhdām) as some investigators mentioned, for 'marji‘' has two meanings: the first is 'returning', the second is 'place of returning'. What is meant first is its first meaning, and by its pronoun the second meaning. So the aforementioned interpretation is for its second meaning, and by that its first meaning becomes clear. (Muhammad Ali) (19) His statement: 'And the reference (marji‘) of total exclusion is to two universal negatives' – 'al-marji‘' with kasra of the jim is a verbal noun meaning returning, not a place noun meaning place of returning, and that is evidenced by its being transitive with 'ila'. The verbal noun (masdar mīmī) comes on the pattern maf‘al with fatha of the ‘ayn from every verb, exceptionally, like marja‘, maghfira, mafāza, ma‘dhira, ma‘ṣiya. And know that what the commentator (al-muḥashshī) mentioned about the reference of the four relations is only according to quantity and quality. As for modality, the reference of total exclusion is to two universal perpetual negatives, because total exclusion between two universals is that they are not true together at all, whether possible or not. And the reference of equality is to two universal absolute general affirmatives, because equality between two universals is that they are actually true together, whether that truth is necessary or not. And the reference of absolute inclusion is to one universal absolute general affirmative and one particular negative for it. And the reference of inclusion from one aspect is to one particular absolute general affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives. So the affirmatives are absolute general, and the negatives are perpetual. (Abd al-Rahim) (20) His statement: 'And the reference of inclusion from one aspect is...'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage comprises several marginal notes that refine and expand the definition of the four relations. (14) repeats the earlier objection about 'nothing' and 'not-possible' and offers a response restricting the rule to universals with actual instances, arguing that logical rules are tailored to human purposes and need not cover hypotheticals. (15-16) clarify 'inclusion from one aspect' with an example: 'animal' and 'white' each include the other and also include things not included by the other. (17) addresses a potential misunderstanding about 'equality': the terms 'asleep' and 'awake' are equal even though they cannot be true of the same individual at the same time; equality only requires that each is true of all individuals of the other at some time, not simultaneously. (18) analyzes the word 'marji‘' (reference) used in defining the relations: it is a verbal noun meaning 'returning', not a place noun, and it can be transitive with 'ila'. This linguistic point is important because the phrase 'marji‘ al-tabāyun ilā sālibatayn' means 'the reduction of total exclusion to two negatives' rather than 'the place of reference'. (19) then applies this: each relation is reduced to a specific combination of categorical propositions, with modality added: total exclusion reduces to two universal perpetual negatives; equality to two universal absolute affirmatives; absolute inclusion to one universal affirmative and one particular negative; inclusion from one aspect to one particular affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives. This maps the relations onto categorical forms for rigorous analysis.

Key Terms
تساوي
نائم
مستيقظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of equality (tasāwī) between two universals?
A) They must be true of each other at the same time.
B) Each must be true of all individuals of the other, but not necessarily simultaneously.
C) They must have the same extension and intension.
D) They must be synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Equality between universals does not require simultaneous truth; it only requires that each is true of every individual of the other at the time the other is true of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the terms 'ḥarām' and 'maḥẓūr' (both meaning 'forbidden'). Are they equal? Check if each is true of all individuals of the other (considering legal contexts). Explain your reasoning.

The Word 'Marji‘': Verbal Noun vs. Place Noun
Analyzes 'marji‘' as verbal noun vs. place noun

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether 'marji‘' functions as a verbal noun or a place noun based on its transitive use with 'ilā'.

Core Idea
The word 'marji‘' (reference) is a verbal noun (masdar mīmī), not a place noun, as shown by its transitive use with 'ilā'.

The precise understanding of the term 'marji‘' is essential for correctly interpreting the reduction of the four relations. The commentator clarifies that 'marji‘' is a verbal noun meaning 'returning' or 'reduction', not 'place of returning'. This distinction affects how we read phrases like 'marji‘ al-tabāyun ilā sālibatayn'.

Guided Path
1
Locate 'marji‘' in the text
Find the word 'marji‘' (with kasra on the jim) in the passage. Notice it is used transitively with 'ilā'.
The passage explicitly says: 'al-marji‘ with kasra of the jim here is a verbal noun.'
2
Identify the linguistic test
The key test is whether the word can govern an object via 'ilā'. Place nouns are inert (jāmid) and cannot be transitive; verbal nouns can.
The text argues: 'it is valid to be transitive with 'ila'.'
3
Contrast with place noun interpretation
If 'marji‘' were a place noun, it would mean 'place of returning' and would not be transitive. The author's words in al-Talkhīṣ might seem to treat it as a place noun, but the commentator explains that via istikhdām (using one word in two senses) the apparent conflict is resolved.
The commentator mentions that some scholars reconcile using istikhdām.
4
Apply the correct meaning
When you see 'marji‘ al-tabāyun ilā sālibatayn', understand it as 'the reduction of total exclusion to two negatives' (i.e., the returning/transformation).
This is a technical logical operation.

(Mirza Muhammad Ali) (14) This is refuted by the likes of 'nothing' and 'not-possible in the general sense', for 'nothing' is such that none of its individuals is true of any individual of 'not-possible' and vice versa. If they were made totally exclusive, there would have to be partial exclusion between their contradictories, as will come, which is false. And it can be answered by restricting the claim to universals that have a reference in external reality, and the generalization of rules is only required in the four relations according to human capacity and the purposes sought from the art, and they have no purpose concerning hypothetical universals. (Abd al-Rahim) (15) That is, each of them is more general in one aspect, in that it includes it and others in some way, and more specific in another aspect, in that it is included in the other while also including others. (Muhammad Ali) (16) 'Animal' includes 'white' and others and vice versa. So in consideration that each of them includes the other, it is more general than it, and in consideration that it is included in it, it is more specific than it; for this reason it is called 'the more general and more specific from one aspect'. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (17) If you say: 'Asleep' and 'awake' are equal and there is no universal truth from both sides. I say: What is considered in equals is that each of them is true of all individuals of the other, and it does not follow from that that they are true together at one time. So 'awake' and 'asleep' are each universally true of the other, except that this is not at one time, and that does not harm their being equals. And some said that equality is only between 'asleep in general' and 'awake in general'; the sleeper during sleep is true of him that he is awake in general, even if he is not true of him that he is awake during sleep; similarly, the awake person during his wakefulness is true of him that he is asleep in general. And equals are such that each is true of all individuals of the other at the time when the other is true of it. And measure on that the truth considered in absolute inclusion and inclusion from one aspect. Reducing the Four Relations to Categorical Propositions
Maps each relation to categorical propositions with modality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to restate each of the four relations (total exclusion, equality, absolute inclusion, inclusion from one aspect) as a combination of categorical propositions with specified modality.

Core Idea
The four relations between universals can be neatly expressed as specific conjunctions of affirmative and negative categorical propositions with modality.

One of the key operations in Islamic logic is the reduction of the four relations to quantified propositions. This allows logicians to analyze relations using the well-established rules of categorical syllogistics. The marginal notes provide the complete mapping, including the modality (absolute general vs. perpetual) for each proposition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four relations
Total exclusion (tabāyun), equality (tasāwī), absolute inclusion (umūm muṭlaq), and inclusion from one aspect (umūm min wajh).
2
Reduce total exclusion
Total exclusion means the two universals never overlap. It reduces to two universal perpetual negatives: both are denied of all individuals of the other.
Passage: 'the reference of total exclusion is to two universal perpetual negatives'
3
Reduce equality
Equality means each is true of all individuals of the other. It reduces to two universal absolute general affirmatives.
Passage: 'the reference of equality is to two universal absolute general affirmatives'
4
Reduce absolute inclusion
One universal includes all of the other but not vice versa. It reduces to one universal absolute general affirmative and one particular negative.
Passage: 'the reference of absolute inclusion is to one universal absolute general affirmative and one particular negative for it'
5
Reduce inclusion from one aspect
Partial overlap. It reduces to one particular absolute general affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives.
Passage: 'the reference of inclusion from one aspect is to one particular absolute general affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives'

جزئيتين دائمتين. فالموجبات مطلقة عامة و السوالب دائمة (عبد الرحيم) (۲۰) قوله ومرجع العموم من وجه ....
(Mirza Muhammad Ali) (14) This is refuted by the likes of 'nothing' and 'not-possible in the general sense', for 'nothing' is such that none of its individuals is true of any individual of 'not-possible' and vice versa. If they were made totally exclusive, there would have to be partial exclusion between their contradictories, as will come, which is false. And it can be answered by restricting the claim to universals that have a reference in external reality, and the generalization of rules is only required in the four relations according to human capacity and the purposes sought from the art, and they have no purpose concerning hypothetical universals. (Abd al-Rahim) (15) That is, each of them is more general in one aspect, in that it includes it and others in some way, and more specific in another aspect, in that it is included in the other while also including others. (Muhammad Ali) (16) 'Animal' includes 'white' and others and vice versa. So in consideration that each of them includes the other, it is more general than it, and in consideration that it is included in it, it is more specific than it; for this reason it is called 'the more general and more specific from one aspect'. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (17) If you say: 'Asleep' and 'awake' are equal and there is no universal truth from both sides. I say: What is considered in equals is that each of them is true of all individuals of the other, and it does not follow from that that they are true together at one time. So 'awake' and 'asleep' are each universally true of the other, except that this is not at one time, and that does not harm their being equals. And some said that equality is only between 'asleep in general' and 'awake in general'; the sleeper during sleep is true of him that he is awake in general, even if he is not true of him that he is awake during sleep; similarly, the awake person during his wakefulness is true of him that he is asleep in general. And equals are such that each is true of all individuals of the other at the time when the other is true of it. And measure on that the truth considered in absolute inclusion and inclusion from one aspect. (Shaykh Abd al-Rahim) (18) 'Al-marji‘' with kasra of the jim here is a verbal noun (masdar mīmī), not a place noun, and it is valid to be transitive with 'ila'. If you say that the words of the author in his commentary on al-Talkhīṣ indicate that 'al-marji‘' is a place noun and is validly transitive with 'ila' – as he said in explaining the author's statement: 'and eloquence, its reference (marji‘) is to avoidance of mistakes, which is what must be achieved so that it can be obtained, as they said: the reference (marji‘) of truth and falsehood is to the conformity of judgment to reality or its non-conformity, i.e., that by which they are realized and obtained' – I say: There is no doubt that a place noun cannot be transitive with anything because it is purely inert, not fit for governing. As for the author's words, it can be on the way of usage (istikhdām) as some investigators mentioned, for 'marji‘' has two meanings: the first is 'returning', the second is 'place of returning'. What is meant first is its first meaning, and by its pronoun the second meaning. So the aforementioned interpretation is for its second meaning, and by that its first meaning becomes clear. (Muhammad Ali) (19) His statement: 'And the reference (marji‘) of total exclusion is to two universal negatives' – 'al-marji‘' with kasra of the jim is a verbal noun meaning returning, not a place noun meaning place of returning, and that is evidenced by its being transitive with 'ila'. The verbal noun (masdar mīmī) comes on the pattern maf‘al with fatha of the ‘ayn from every verb, exceptionally, like marja‘, maghfira, mafāza, ma‘dhira, ma‘ṣiya. And know that what the commentator (al-muḥashshī) mentioned about the reference of the four relations is only according to quantity and quality. As for modality, the reference of total exclusion is to two universal perpetual negatives, because total exclusion between two universals is that they are not true together at all, whether possible or not. And the reference of equality is to two universal absolute general affirmatives, because equality between two universals is that they are actually true together, whether that truth is necessary or not. And the reference of absolute inclusion is to one universal absolute general affirmative and one particular negative for it. And the reference of inclusion from one aspect is to one particular absolute general affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives. So the affirmatives are absolute general, and the negatives are perpetual. (Abd al-Rahim) (20) His statement: 'And the reference of inclusion from one aspect is...'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage comprises several marginal notes that refine and expand the definition of the four relations. (14) repeats the earlier objection about 'nothing' and 'not-possible' and offers a response restricting the rule to universals with actual instances, arguing that logical rules are tailored to human purposes and need not cover hypotheticals. (15-16) clarify 'inclusion from one aspect' with an example: 'animal' and 'white' each include the other and also include things not included by the other. (17) addresses a potential misunderstanding about 'equality': the terms 'asleep' and 'awake' are equal even though they cannot be true of the same individual at the same time; equality only requires that each is true of all individuals of the other at some time, not simultaneously. (18) analyzes the word 'marji‘' (reference) used in defining the relations: it is a verbal noun meaning 'returning', not a place noun, and it can be transitive with 'ila'. This linguistic point is important because the phrase 'marji‘ al-tabāyun ilā sālibatayn' means 'the reduction of total exclusion to two negatives' rather than 'the place of reference'. (19) then applies this: each relation is reduced to a specific combination of categorical propositions, with modality added: total exclusion reduces to two universal perpetual negatives; equality to two universal absolute affirmatives; absolute inclusion to one universal affirmative and one particular negative; inclusion from one aspect to one particular affirmative and two particular perpetual negatives. This maps the relations onto categorical forms for rigorous analysis.

تساوي
عموم مطلق
عموم من وجه
سالبة كلية دائمة
موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة

Key Takeaway
Each of the four relations between universals can be precisely expressed as a specific combination of categorical propositions with stated modality, enabling rigorous logical analysis.

Equal Terms Have Equal Contradictories: Existential Import
Author defends and refines the principle of equal contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary condition of existential import in the inference from equality of terms to equality of their contradictories, and evaluate objections that exploit this condition.

Core Idea
Equal terms have equal contradictories only when their contradictories have existential import.

This lesson tackles a subtle but crucial point in immediate inference: the principle that if two terms are equal (like 'human' and 'rational'), their contradictories ('non-human' and 'non-rational') must also be equal. But is this always true? The author defends the principle against an objection concerning non-existent subjects, forcing us to clarify the role of existential import. By the end, you will see how logical generalizations are bounded by the purposes of the art.

Guided Path
1
State the claim and its proof
The claim is: if terms A and B are equal (e.g., 'human' and 'rational'), then their contradictories not-A and not-B are also equal. The proof uses the principle of contradiction: if they were not equal, then some not-A would be B, violating the original equality.
2
Identify the objection's logical structure
The objection argues that the proof assumes that a modified negative (e.g., 'some non-human is not non-rational') entails its absolute affirmative ('some non-human is rational'), which fails when the subject does not exist. Example: 'Zayd is not a non-writer' does not imply 'Zayd is a writer' if Zayd does not exist.
This highlights the distinction between modified (ma'dula) and absolute (muhassala) propositions.
3
Analyze the existential requirement
The objection uses terms like 'thing' and 'possible' whose contradictories have no instances. Thus the inference only holds when the contradictories have existential import—i.e., there is at least one existing subject for not-A and not-B.
The subject must exist for the relationship between modified and absolute propositions to hold.
4
Evaluate the author's restriction
The author restricts the proof to cases where the equal terms are not universal (covering all things). Then the contradictories must apply to some existing entity, either external or mental. Generalization of logical rules is purpose-driven, not absolute.
This illustrates that logical principles are often conditional and context-sensitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲۲) قوله : اى كل ما صدق عليه أحد النقيضين حاصله انه اذا ثبت أن بين الانسان و الناطق مثلاً تساوياً فلابد وان يكون بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاانسان واللاناطق ايضاً تساو بمعنى ان يكون كل لا انسان لا ناطقاً وكل لا ناطق لا انساناً اذ لولم يصدق نصدق نقيضاهما وهما بعض اللاانسان ليس بلا ناطق وبعض اللاتناطق ليس بلا انسان فحينئذ يصدق بعض اللا انسان ناطق و بعض اللاناطق انسان ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين، فيصدق عين احدهما بدون عين الآخر لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين وهو خلاف الفرض، هذا. واعترض عليه بأن صدق :قولنا بعض اللاانسان ليس بلا ناطق و بعض اللاناطق ليس بلا انسان لا يستلزم صدق قولنا بعض اللا انسان ناطق و بعض اللاناطق انسان لما سيأتى من ان صدق السالبة : المعدولة المحمول لا يستلزم صدق الموجبة المحصلة الاترى ان صدق قولنا زيد ليس بلا كاتب لا يوجب صدق قولنا زيد كاتب الجواز ان يكون زيد معدوماً فلا يكون كاتباً ولا لا كاتباً.
لا يقال ان الموضوع فيا نحن فيه موجود فان اللاانسان واللاناطق صادقان على موجودات محققة كالشجر والحجر وغيرهما و السالبة المعدولة و الموجبة المحصلة مثلا زمان حين وجود الموضوع كما سيأتى. لانا نقول : ليس الكلام في خصوص اللا انسان و اللاناطق ونحوهما بل في نقيضى المتساو بين مطلقا فما تقول في مثل الشيء والممكن العام فانها متساويان لصدقهما على جميع المفهومات و لا يصدق نقيضاهما و هما اللاشيء واللاممكن على شيء اصلاً كما تقدم فلايتم البرهان المذكورح البتة، فاذا قلت: لو لم يصدق كل لا شيء لا ممكن لصدق بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا ممكن فيصدق بعض اللاشيء ممكن اتجه المنع المذكور فان الموضوع ليس بموجود قطعاً . واجيب بانا تخص البحث بما اذا لم يكن المتساويان شاملين لجميع الاشياء ذهن و خارجاً فلابد ان يصدق نقيضاهما على موجود اما خارجى او ذهني فيتم حالدليل و ينسد السبيل وتعميم القواعد انما يجب بحسب الاغراض المقصودة والمقاصد المطلوبة من الفن كما سبق.
(22) His saying: i.e., everything to which one of the two contradictories applies. Its summary is that if it is established that between, for example, 'human' and 'rational' there is equality, then it is necessary that between their contradictories, namely 'non-human' and 'non-rational', there is also equality, meaning that every non-human is not-rational and every non-rational is not-human. Because if they were not true, then their contradictories would be true, namely 'some non-human is not non-rational' and 'some non-rational is not non-human'. Then it would be true that 'some non-human is rational' and 'some non-rational is human' necessarily due to the impossibility of both contradictories being false. So one (of the two) would be true without the other, due to the impossibility of both being true, which is contrary to the assumption. This (is the proof). And it was objected: The truth of our statement 'some non-human is not non-rational' and 'some non-rational is not non-human' does not entail the truth of our statement 'some non-human is rational' and 'some non-rational is human', because (as will come) the truth of a negative with a modified predicate (saliba ma'dulat al-mahmul) does not entail the truth of a positive with an absolute predicate (mujaba muhassala). Do you not see that the truth of our statement 'Zayd is not a non-writer' does not necessitate the truth of our statement 'Zayd is a writer' because it is possible that Zayd is non-existent, so he is neither a writer nor a non-writer. It cannot be said that the subject in our case exists, for 'non-human' and 'non-rational' are true of existing things like tree, stone, etc., and the negative with modified predicate and the positive with absolute predicate, for example, are (only) at the time of the existence of the subject, as will come. For we say: The discussion is not specifically about 'non-human' and 'non-rational' and their likes, but about the contradictories of two equal terms in general. What do you say about something like 'thing' and 'absolute possible'? For they are equal since they are true of all concepts, yet their contradictories, namely 'non-thing' and 'non-possible', are not true of anything at all, as has preceded. So the aforementioned proof fails entirely. For if you said: If 'every non-thing is not-possible' were not true, then 'some non-thing is not non-possible' would be true, so 'some non-thing is possible' would be true. The aforementioned objection applies, because the subject is definitely non-existent. And it was answered: We restrict the discussion to cases where the two equal terms are not inclusive of all things, both mentally and externally. Then their contradictories must be true of some existent, either external or mental. Thus the proof holds and the path is blocked. Generalization of rules is only required according to the intended purposes and desired objectives of the art, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) defending a logical proof. It begins by restating a lemma: if two terms like 'human' and 'rational' are equal in extension, then their contradictories 'non-human' and 'non-rational' must also be equal. The proof uses the principle that if they were not equal, then some non-human would be rational and some non-rational would be human, which contradicts the original equality. An objection is raised: the truth of a modified negative (e.g., 'some non-human is not non-rational') does not entail the corresponding affirmative with absolute predicate because the subject might not exist. For example, 'Zayd is not a non-writer' does not mean 'Zayd is a writer' if Zayd does not exist. The objector further argues that if we take universal terms like 'thing' and 'possible', their contradictories have no instances, so the proof fails. The answer restricts the proof to cases where the equal terms do not cover all things, ensuring that the contradictories have existential import. The commentator concludes that logical rules are generalized only according to the needs of the art. This discussion highlights the importance of existential import in logical conversions.

Key Terms
السالبة المعدولة المحمول
الموجبة المحصلة
نقيض

Key Takeaway
The principle that equal terms have equal contradictories holds only when the contradictories have existential import; logical generalizations are purpose-bound.

Linguistic vs Terminological Conversion ('Aks)
Author distinguishes linguistic vs terminological conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between linguistic and terminological conversion ('aks) and identify which is negated in the author's statement 'wa la aks'.

Core Idea
The denial of conversion is of linguistic 'aks, not terminological.

You might wonder: if the converse of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative, why does the author say 'and no conversion'? The answer lies in distinguishing between two types of conversion: linguistic and terminological. This lesson clarifies that distinction and applies it to the author's statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the universal affirmative proposition being discussed: 'Every muqawwim of the higher is muqawwim of the lower.'
2
Recall terminological conversion rule
For a universal affirmative (A), terminological conversion yields a particular affirmative (I). So the converse would be 'Some muqawwim of the lower is muqawwim of the higher.' This is true.
3
Understand linguistic conversion rule
Linguistic conversion preserves quantity and quality. Thus the converse of a universal affirmative would also be universal: 'Every muqawwim of the lower is muqawwim of the higher.' This is false in the given context.
4
Identify which conversion is negated
The author's 'wa la aks' denies linguistic conversion, not terminological. The objection mistakenly assumed terminological, but the denial is of linguistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١٦) قونه اى كلياً .... اشارة الى دفع ما قديتوهم وهو ان يقال: ان عكس الموجبة مطلقاً، ای سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية ، موجبة جزئية كما سيجي انشاء الله تعالى وقولنا : «المقوم للعالى مقوم السافل موجبة كلية لان اللام كما تقدم للاستغراق وهو بمعنى «كل» كما هو ظاهر و عکسه موجبة جزئية اعنى بعض مقوم للسائل مقوم للعالى و هذا صحيح كما هو ظاهر فان الحساس مقوم للسافل اعنى الانسان و مقوم للحيوان ايضاً و هو العالى. نفات کی موتری علوم اداری وحاصل الجواب ان مراد المصنف بالعكس في قوله ولا عكس العكس اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي والعكس اللغوى للموجبة كنفسها ان كليا فكلى وان جزئياً فجزئى فاته عكس الجزئين مع الاتفاق في الكم والكيف جميعاً ولاشك في عدم صحة العكس بهذا المعنى في هذا المقام فلذا نفي المصنف العكس. (میرزا محمد علی) (١١٧) تفسير لقول المصنف: «ولا عكس اى للنفى و المنفى جميعاً و اشارة الى ان قوله: «ای كلياً»، قيد للمنفى فان ليس كل من أسوار السالبة الجزئية كما سيجيء و رفع الايجاب الكلى سلب جزئى هذا الخ (محمد على) (١١٨) فان قلت ليس كل ك بعض ليس من اسوار السالبة الجزئية، فكيف يكون معنى العكس الكلى ذلك ؟ قلت: قوله ليس معنى اذ لنفى و هو لا عكس و المنفى و هو العكس الكلى، ما بعد ليس فتدبر. فان قلت لم قيد العكس بالكل مع انه محل اللفظ المصطلح على المعنى اللغوى و هو بعيد؟ قلت: لان العكس الاصطلاحي ثابت هيهنا فلا يصح نفيه. فان قلت: لم لم يصح العكس الكلى هيهنا فلم يكن الناطق مقوماً للعالى كما هو مقوم للسافل ؟ :قلت اذ ليس فى السافل وراء ماهية العالى الا الفصول المقومة للسائل فاذا فرضت مشتركة بينه و بين العالى يلزم عدم الفرق بين السافل و العالى وايضاً ليس كل ما هو جزء الكل جزء الجزء والا لكان الكل جزء الجزء اذ الكل عين جميع اجزائه تأمل. (عبد الرحيم)
(116) His statement: 'i.e., universal...' is an indication to repel what might be imagined: that it is said: 'The converse of the affirmative proposition, absolutely whether universal or particular, is an affirmative particular' as will come, Allah willing. And our statement: 'The constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' is a universal affirmative because the lam (ال) as previously is for generality, and it means 'every', as is apparent. Its converse would be a particular affirmative, i.e., 'some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher'. And this is correct as is apparent, for 'al-hissas' (sensation?) is constitutive of the lower, i.e., human, and also constitutive of the animal, which is the higher. ... And the summary of the answer is that the author meant by 'aks' in his statement 'and no aks' the linguistic 'aks, not the terminological 'aks. The linguistic 'aks of an affirmative is like itself: if universal, then universal; if particular, then particular. So its 'aks of the universal with the agreement in quantity and quality is not valid. And there is no doubt about the invalidity of the 'aks in this sense in this context. Therefore the author denied the 'aks. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (117) Interpretation of the author's statement: 'And no 'aks' i.e., for the negation and the negated together, and an indication that his statement 'i.e., universal' is a condition for the negated. For 'not every' is among the quantifiers of the particular negative, as will come. And raising the universal affirmative is a particular negative, etc. (Muhammad Ali) (118) If you say: 'Not every' (laysa kull) is like 'some' (ba'd), it is not among the quantifiers of the particular negative, so how can the meaning of the universal 'aks be that? I say: His statement 'not' (laysa) is meaning, because the negation is 'la 'aks' (no 'aks) and the negated is the universal 'aks, what comes after 'laysa' — so reflect. If you say: Why did he qualify the 'aks with 'universal' while it is a place for the terminological term in the linguistic sense, and that is far? I say: Because the terminological 'aks is established here, so it is not correct to negate it. If you say: Why is the universal 'aks not correct here, such that 'natiq' is not constitutive of the higher as it is constitutive of the lower? I say: Because in the lower, beyond the essence of the higher, there are only the differentiae that constitute the lower. So if they are assumed to be common between it and the higher, then there would be no difference between the lower and the higher. Also, not everything that is part of the whole is part of the part; otherwise the whole would be part of the part, for the whole is identical to all its parts. Reflect. (Abdul Rahim)
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This extended passage (notes 116-118) addresses the denial of conversion ('aks) in the statement 'wa la 'aks' from the main text. The author explains that the imagined objection is that the converse of the universal affirmative 'Every constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' should be the particular affirmative 'Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher', which is true (e.g., sensibility is constitutive of human and animal). But the author denies conversion. The answer is that 'aks here means linguistic 'aks, not terminological. In linguistic 'aks, the converse has the same quantity and quality as the original. So the universal would become universal, but that would mean 'Every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher', which is false. Therefore, the denial is of the linguistic universal 'aks. Then note 117 interprets that 'wa la 'aks' negates both the negation and the negated, and 'i.e., universal' modifies the negated. Note 118 is a question-answer exchange: it asks why 'not every' is used, and explains that the negation of the universal 'aks is a particular negative. It further asks why the universal 'aks is not correct here: because if the constitutive (e.g., natiq) were common to both higher and lower, they would not be distinct; and not every part of the whole is part of the part. This reinforces the specific logical rule.

Key Terms
عكس
موجبة كلية
سلب جزئى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Linguistic 'aks.
C) Both linguistic and terminological.
D) Only the particular 'aks.

Key Takeaway
The denial of conversion in 'wa la aks' refers to linguistic conversion, which would preserve the universal quantity and is invalid in this context.

10 min
Why Universal Linguistic Conversion Fails for Muqawwim
Author explains why universal linguistic aks fails

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the universal linguistic conversion of 'Every muqawwim of the higher is muqawwim of the lower' is invalid using logical and ontological arguments.

Core Idea
Universal linguistic conversion would make the differentia common, destroying the distinction between higher and lower.

The preceding lesson established that the denial targets linguistic conversion. But why exactly is the universal converse false? The author provides two reasons: it would erase the difference between the higher and lower, and it violates the part-whole principle. This lesson unpacks those reasons.

Guided Path
1
Recall the original proposition
The original universal affirmative: 'Every muqawwim of the higher is muqawwim of the lower.' This is true because the constitutive attributes of the higher (e.g., genus) are also constitutive of the lower (e.g., species).
2
Consider the linguistic converse
Linguistic conversion would give: 'Every muqawwim of the lower is muqawwim of the higher.' This would mean every differentia of the lower is also constitutive of the higher. But the differentia (e.g., natiq for human) is what distinguishes the lower from the higher.
3
First argument: destroying distinction
If the differentia of the lower were common to the higher, then the lower would not be distinct from the higher. The essence (mahiyya) of the higher does not include the differentiae of the lower beyond its own essence.
4
Second argument: part-whole principle
Not every part of the whole is a part of the part. The higher is a whole relative to the lower. The differentia of the lower is a part of the lower (species) but not a part of the higher (genus) in the same way; otherwise the whole would be part of the part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١٦) قونه اى كلياً .... اشارة الى دفع ما قديتوهم وهو ان يقال: ان عكس الموجبة مطلقاً، ای سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية ، موجبة جزئية كما سيجي انشاء الله تعالى وقولنا : «المقوم للعالى مقوم السافل موجبة كلية لان اللام كما تقدم للاستغراق وهو بمعنى «كل» كما هو ظاهر و عکسه موجبة جزئية اعنى بعض مقوم للسائل مقوم للعالى و هذا صحيح كما هو ظاهر فان الحساس مقوم للسافل اعنى الانسان و مقوم للحيوان ايضاً و هو العالى. نفات کی موتری علوم اداری وحاصل الجواب ان مراد المصنف بالعكس في قوله ولا عكس العكس اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي والعكس اللغوى للموجبة كنفسها ان كليا فكلى وان جزئياً فجزئى فاته عكس الجزئين مع الاتفاق في الكم والكيف جميعاً ولاشك في عدم صحة العكس بهذا المعنى في هذا المقام فلذا نفي المصنف العكس. (میرزا محمد علی) (١١٧) تفسير لقول المصنف: «ولا عكس اى للنفى و المنفى جميعاً و اشارة الى ان قوله: «ای كلياً»، قيد للمنفى فان ليس كل من أسوار السالبة الجزئية كما سيجيء و رفع الايجاب الكلى سلب جزئى هذا الخ (محمد على) (١١٨) فان قلت ليس كل ك بعض ليس من اسوار السالبة الجزئية، فكيف يكون معنى العكس الكلى ذلك ؟ قلت: قوله ليس معنى اذ لنفى و هو لا عكس و المنفى و هو العكس الكلى، ما بعد ليس فتدبر. فان قلت لم قيد العكس بالكل مع انه محل اللفظ المصطلح على المعنى اللغوى و هو بعيد؟ قلت: لان العكس الاصطلاحي ثابت هيهنا فلا يصح نفيه. فان قلت: لم لم يصح العكس الكلى هيهنا فلم يكن الناطق مقوماً للعالى كما هو مقوم للسافل ؟ :قلت اذ ليس فى السافل وراء ماهية العالى الا الفصول المقومة للسائل فاذا فرضت مشتركة بينه و بين العالى يلزم عدم الفرق بين السافل و العالى وايضاً ليس كل ما هو جزء الكل جزء الجزء والا لكان الكل جزء الجزء اذ الكل عين جميع اجزائه تأمل. (عبد الرحيم)
(116) His statement: 'i.e., universal...' is an indication to repel what might be imagined: that it is said: 'The converse of the affirmative proposition, absolutely whether universal or particular, is an affirmative particular' as will come, Allah willing. And our statement: 'The constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' is a universal affirmative because the lam (ال) as previously is for generality, and it means 'every', as is apparent. Its converse would be a particular affirmative, i.e., 'some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher'. And this is correct as is apparent, for 'al-hissas' (sensation?) is constitutive of the lower, i.e., human, and also constitutive of the animal, which is the higher. ... And the summary of the answer is that the author meant by 'aks' in his statement 'and no aks' the linguistic 'aks, not the terminological 'aks. The linguistic 'aks of an affirmative is like itself: if universal, then universal; if particular, then particular. So its 'aks of the universal with the agreement in quantity and quality is not valid. And there is no doubt about the invalidity of the 'aks in this sense in this context. Therefore the author denied the 'aks. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (117) Interpretation of the author's statement: 'And no 'aks' i.e., for the negation and the negated together, and an indication that his statement 'i.e., universal' is a condition for the negated. For 'not every' is among the quantifiers of the particular negative, as will come. And raising the universal affirmative is a particular negative, etc. (Muhammad Ali) (118) If you say: 'Not every' (laysa kull) is like 'some' (ba'd), it is not among the quantifiers of the particular negative, so how can the meaning of the universal 'aks be that? I say: His statement 'not' (laysa) is meaning, because the negation is 'la 'aks' (no 'aks) and the negated is the universal 'aks, what comes after 'laysa' — so reflect. If you say: Why did he qualify the 'aks with 'universal' while it is a place for the terminological term in the linguistic sense, and that is far? I say: Because the terminological 'aks is established here, so it is not correct to negate it. If you say: Why is the universal 'aks not correct here, such that 'natiq' is not constitutive of the higher as it is constitutive of the lower? I say: Because in the lower, beyond the essence of the higher, there are only the differentiae that constitute the lower. So if they are assumed to be common between it and the higher, then there would be no difference between the lower and the higher. Also, not everything that is part of the whole is part of the part; otherwise the whole would be part of the part, for the whole is identical to all its parts. Reflect. (Abdul Rahim)
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This extended passage (notes 116-118) addresses the denial of conversion ('aks) in the statement 'wa la 'aks' from the main text. The author explains that the imagined objection is that the converse of the universal affirmative 'Every constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' should be the particular affirmative 'Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher', which is true (e.g., sensibility is constitutive of human and animal). But the author denies conversion. The answer is that 'aks here means linguistic 'aks, not terminological. In linguistic 'aks, the converse has the same quantity and quality as the original. So the universal would become universal, but that would mean 'Every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher', which is false. Therefore, the denial is of the linguistic universal 'aks. Then note 117 interprets that 'wa la 'aks' negates both the negation and the negated, and 'i.e., universal' modifies the negated. Note 118 is a question-answer exchange: it asks why 'not every' is used, and explains that the negation of the universal 'aks is a particular negative. It further asks why the universal 'aks is not correct here: because if the constitutive (e.g., natiq) were common to both higher and lower, they would not be distinct; and not every part of the whole is part of the part. This reinforces the specific logical rule.

مقوم
فصل

Key Takeaway
The universal linguistic conversion fails because it would make the differentia of the lower common to the higher, violating the hierarchical structure of definitions.

Linguistic vs. Technical Conversion
Author rebuts the doubt that conversion of universal affirmative yields particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish linguistic conversion from technical conversion and determine which is being used in a logical passage.

Core Idea
Conversion has two meanings: one preserves quantity/quality, the other changes them; confusion leads to errors.

You may have learned that the converse of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative. But what happens when a text says 'no conversion' for such a statement? In this passage, the hashia author resolves the apparent contradiction by clarifying that the base text meant 'linguistic conversion', which keeps the quantity and quality unchanged. This distinction is essential for correctly interpreting logical arguments, especially in Islamic sciences where precise terminology is paramount.

Guided Path
1
Identify the doubt
The doubt is that a universal affirmative's converse should be a particular affirmative (standard technical conversion). The base text says 'nor conversion', which seems to deny this. Recognize that the example given: 'المقوم للعالى مقوم السافل' (Every constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower) would technically convert to 'بعض مقوم للسافل مقوم للعالى' (Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher). This is valid, so why does the author negate conversion?
2
Locate the resolution
The commentator states: 'مراد المصنف بالعكس في قوله ولا عكس العكس اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي' meaning the author intended linguistic conversion, not technical. Write down the two definitions: linguistic conversion keeps quantity and quality identical (universal->universal, particular->particular); technical conversion turns a universal affirmative into a particular affirmative.
Key terms: عكس لغوي, عكس اصطلاحي
3
Apply the distinction to the example
Test the example with linguistic conversion: 'المقوم للعالى مقوم السافل' (universal affirmative) would become another universal affirmative: 'المقوم للسافل مقوم للعالى'. Is this new statement true? In the given example, 'sensitive' is constitutive of both man and animal, but it is not true that every constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher (e.g., 'rationality' is constitutive of man but not of animal). So the linguistic converse is false, hence the base text's negation is correct.
This shows why the author correctly negated conversion when interpreted linguistically.
4
Generalize the rule
Whenever you encounter 'عكس' in a logical text, determine from context whether it refers to the standard technical conversion (which changes quantity) or the linguistic conversion (which preserves form). This avoids misinterpretation.
In this passage, the context of 'ولا عكس' forced the commentator to specify linguistic conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۶) قونه اى كلياً .... اشارة الى دفع ما قديتوهم وهو ان يقال: ان عكس الموجبة مطلقاً، ای سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية ، موجبة جزئية كما سيجي انشاء الله تعالى وقولنا : «المقوم للعالى مقوم السافل موجبة كلية لان اللام كما تقدم للاستغراق وهو بمعنى «كل» كما هو ظاهر و عکسه موجبة جزئية اعنى بعض مقوم للسائل مقوم للعالى و هذا صحيح كما هو ظاهر فان الحساس مقوم للسافل اعنى الانسان و مقوم للحيوان ايضاً و هو العالى. نفات کی موتری علوم اداری وحاصل الجواب ان مراد المصنف بالعكس في قوله ولا عكس العكس اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي والعكس اللغوى للموجبة كنفسها ان كليا فكلى وان جزئياً فجزئى فاته عكس الجزئين مع الاتفاق في الكم والكيف جميعاً ولاشك في عدم صحة العكس بهذا المعنى في هذا المقام فلذا نفي المصنف العكس. (میرزا محمد علی)
(116) His statement: 'i.e., universal'... is an indication of repelling what might be doubted, namely that it is said: the converse of the affirmative, absolutely, whether it is universal or particular, is a particular affirmative, as will come insha'Allah. And our statement: 'The constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' is a universal affirmative because the lam (ال) as mentioned is for generalization and it means 'every', as is apparent. Its converse would be a particular affirmative, i.e., 'Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher', and this is correct, as is apparent, because 'sensitive' is constitutive of the lower, i.e., man, and is constitutive of the animal as well, which is the higher. [??] The summary of the answer is that what the author means by 'conversion' (عكس) in his statement 'nor conversion' is the linguistic conversion, not the technical one. The linguistic conversion of the affirmative is like itself: if universal then universal, if particular then particular. For it converts the two with agreement in quantity and quality altogether. There is no doubt about the incorrectness of conversion in this sense in this context, so the author negated conversion.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary addressing a potential misunderstanding. The doubt is that in standard logic, the converse of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative. For example, the statement 'Every constitutive of the higher is constitutive of the lower' would yield 'Some constitutive of the lower is constitutive of the higher'. This actually holds, as shown with the example of 'sensitive' (al-hassas) as constitutive of man and animal. However, the author (al-musannif) in the base text says 'nor conversion' (ولا عكس). To resolve this, the commentator explains that the intended meaning of 'conversion' here is linguistic (عكس لغوي), not the technical conversion (عكس اصطلاحي). In linguistic conversion, the quantity and quality remain the same: a universal affirmative stays universal. Understood this way, the author's negation is correct because the universal converse does not hold in this context (hence 'nor conversion'). This distinction preserves the consistency of the base text.

عكس اصطلاحي

Parsing the Scope of Negation in Logical Phrases
Commentator interprets 'لا عكس' as applying to both negation and the negated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scope of negation in phrases like 'لا عكس' and distinguish between negating the act and negating the statement.

Core Idea
Negation in logic can apply to both the operation and the proposition.

In the study of immediate inference, it is crucial to understand exactly what a negation negates. This lesson examines a commentator's parsing of the phrase 'ولا عكس' (nor conversion) from a classical logic text. The commentator clarifies that the negation applies not only to the act of conversion but also to the statement being converted. By understanding this, you will avoid a common misunderstanding that could affect your analysis of logical conversions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Negated Phrase
Recognize that the phrase being discussed is 'ولا عكس'. What is the grammatical subject of the negation? The particle 'لا' negates the following action or statement.
2
Distinguish Two Possible Targets of Negation
Note that negation can apply to the act (النفى) or to the proposition (المنفى). The commentator claims it applies to both.
3
Examine the Restriction in the Example
The author adds 'ای كلياً' (i.e., universal) as a restriction for the negated. This indicates that the proposition being negated is a universal statement.
4
Connect to Standard Logical Rules
Recall that 'not every' is a quantifier for particular negative propositions, and negating a universal affirmative yields a particular negative. This confirms the scope interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١٧) تفسير لقول المصنف: «ولا عكس اى للنفى و المنفى جميعاً و اشارة الى ان قوله: «ای كلياً»، قيد للمنفى فان ليس كل من أسوار السالبة الجزئية كما سيجيء و رفع الايجاب الكلى سلب جزئى هذا الخ (محمد على)
(117) Interpretation of the author's statement: 'Nor conversion', i.e., for the negation and the negated together, and an indication that his statement 'i.e., universal' is a restriction for the negated. For 'not every' is among the quantifiers of the particular negative, as will come, and the removal of the universal affirmative is a particular negative, etc.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This short passage is a parsing exercise that clarifies the scope of the negation in the author's phrase 'nor conversion' (ولا عكس). The commentator (Muhammad Ali) explains that the negation applies both to the act of conversion (النفى) and to the statement that is being negated (المنفى). That is, the author is negating both the possibility of conversion itself and the specific universal statement that was under discussion. Furthermore, the phrase 'i.e., universal' (ای كلياً) is a qualification (قيد) for the negated statement, indicating that what is being negated is a universal proposition. The commentator then relates this to the standard logical treatment of 'not every' as a quantifier of particular negative, and that negating a universal affirmative yields a particular negative. This connects the discussion to broader logical rules.

Key Terms

منفى
سالب جزئي

Key Takeaway
The scope of negation in logical phrases must be carefully parsed; it can cover both the operation and the proposition.

The Three Quantifiers of Particular Negation
The author distinguishes the meanings of three quantifier phrases for particular negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the direct and implied meanings of 'laysa ba‘d', 'ba‘d laysa', and 'laysa kull' in categorical propositions.

Core Idea
The arrangement of 'laysa', 'ba‘d', and 'kull' flips the logical force of the negation.

This lesson introduces the three quantifier phrases used for particular negative propositions in classical logic. While they might appear interchangeable, each has a distinct logical function: direct indication (bi-l-mutabaqa) vs. implication (bi-l-iltizam). Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting negated statements in Islamic theological and legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three phrases
Locate 'ليس بعض' (laysa ba‘ḍ), 'بعض ليس' (ba‘ḍ laysa), and 'ليس كل' (laysa kull) in the Arabic passage. Note their literal translations.
2
Analyze 'laysa ba‘ḍ' and 'ba‘ḍ laysa'
According to the standard view, these two directly indicate particular negation (salb juz’ī). Example: 'ليس بعض الإنسان بكاتب' directly means 'some man is not a writer'. They also imply the negation of universal affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-kullī) because if some are not, it is not true that all are.
3
Analyze 'laysa kull'
This phrase directly negates universal affirmation. Example: 'ليس كل إنسان كاتباً' directly means 'it is not the case that every man is a writer', which logically implies that some are not.
4
Map the implication relations
For the first two, direct = particular negation; implied = negation of universal affirmation. For 'laysa kull', direct = negation of universal affirmation; implied = particular negation. This is the standard distinction.
Note that the negation of universal affirmation is equivalent to particular negation (it cannot be that all are if at least one is not).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض و بعض ليس وليس كل : الفرق بين : الامور الثلاثة : ان الاولين يدلان على السلب الجزئى بالمطابقة و على رفع الايجاب الكلى بالالتزام والاخير اعنى ليس كل بالعكس أما الأول فلان قولنا ليس بعض الانسان بكاتب» او بعضه ليس بكاتب» يدل على سلب الكتابة عن بعض افراد الانسان بالمطابقة و هو معنى السلب الجزئى و يلزمه رفع الايجاب الكلى لانه اذا سلب المحمول عن بعض افراد الموضوع يسلب عن كل افراده ايضاً فان الايجاب الكلى يرتفع بالسلب الجزئى. و اما الثانى اعنى ان ليس كل يدل على رفع الايجاب الكلى بالمطابقة و على السلب الجزئى بالالتزام فلان النفى اذا دخل على جملة فيها امر زايد على اثبات شيء لشيء او نفيه عنه انما يتوجه الى ذلك الامر الزايد دون اصل الحكم بل يفيد بمفهومه ثبوته و المفهوم من قولنا : «كل انسان كاتب ثبوت الكتابة لكل واحد واحد من افراد الانسان و هو الايجاب الكلى فاذا ادخلنا عليه «ليس وقلنا ليس كل انسان كاتباً كان معناه الصريح المطابق ان ليس يثبت الكتابة لكل واحد واحد من افراد الانسان و هو رفع الايجاب الكلى وذلك اما بان يكون الكتابة مسلوبة عن كل واحد و هو السلب الكلى او مسلوبة عن البعض ثابتة للبعض الآخر وعلى كلا التقديرين يلزمه السلب الجزئي البتة بخلاف السلب الكلى فانه لا دلالة للعام على الخاص قطعاً بل احتمالاً و لهذا اختص ليس كل سوراً بالسلب الجزئى اخذاً بالمتيقن المقطوع وتركاً للمحتمل المشكوك.
ولا يذهب عليك ان ليس رفع الايجاب الكلى اعم من السلب الجزئى حتى يقال انه لا دلالة للعام على الخاص فيكون السلب الجزئى مثل السلب الكلى فى الاحتمال فلا يكون لاختصاص ليس كل سوراً بالسلب الجزئى جهة وانه لا يلزم من وجود العام وجود الخاص فكيف يكون لازماً له والحال ان اللازم يمتنع وجود ملزومه بدونه بل هو مساو له ؟ لان معنى السلب الجزئى هو سلب الحكم عن البعض اعم من ان يكون مسلوباً عن البعض الآخر أيضاً او ثابتاً له و هو معنى رفع الايجاب الكلى، نعم هو اعم من السلب عن البعض مع الاثبات للاخر كما سبق و اين هو من السلب الجزئى؟ بل السلب الجزئى ايضاً اعم منه.
هذا محصل الفرق على ما هو المذكور فى كتب القوم واقول :
ان الفرق المذكور ظاهر بالنسبة الى بعض «ليس و اما بالنسبة الى «ليس بعض فلا، فإن مفهومه الصريح رفع الايجاب الجزئى كما ان مفهوم ليس كل هو رفع الايجاب الكلى بناء على القاعدة المذكورة.
والتحقيق ان يقال ان ليس كل» و «ليس بعض ان اعتبر سلبهما بالنسبة الى القضية التي بعد هما فمفهوم «ليس كل بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الكلى السلب الجزئى و مفهوم «ليس بعض» بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الجزئى و هو السلب الكلى وان اعتبر بالنسبة إلى المحمول فالمفهوم المطابق لـ «ليس كل» هو السلب الكلى و لـ «ليس بعض هو السنب الجزئى وبعبارة أخرى أوضح من ذلك، اذا اعتبر تأخر «بعض» و «كل» عن النفى فـ ليس كل لرفع الايجاب الكلى و ليس بعض» ترفع الايجاب الجزئى و اذا لم يعتبر تأخر هما عنه فى «ليس كل السلب الكلى و ليس بعض السلب الجزئى فتأمل. و كيف كان لا يستقيم الفرق المذكور فى كتبهم، هذا هو الفرق بين الاولين و الاخير واما الفرق بين الأول والثانى فهو ان الاول اعنى: «ليس بعض» قد يذكر للسلب الكلى وقد يذكر للسلب الجزئى بالاعتبارين المذكورين ولا يذكر للايجاب البتة لأن وضع حرف السلب على رفع ما بعده فيمتنع ان يحصل الايجاب به.
والثاني اعنى بعض ليس لايذكر للسلب الكلى، لان النفى لم يدخل على بعض بل البعض هو الموضوع وحرف السلب اذا توسط بين الشيئين يقتضى سلب ما بعده عما قبله فيقتضى هنا سلب المحمول عن البعض فلا يكون الاجزئياً، وقد يذكر للايجاب اذا جعلت حرف السلب جزء من المحمول على ما هو حكم الموجبة المعدونة المحمول على ما سيأتي.
His statement: 'And the quantifier of the particular negative is 'not some', 'some not', and 'not every': the difference between the three matters: the first two indicate particular negation by direct correspondence (bi-l-mutabaqa) and the negation of universal affirmation by implication (bi-l-iltizam), while the last, i.e., 'not every', is the opposite. As for the first, because our saying 'not some man is a writer' or 'some of him is not a writer' indicates the negation of writing from some individuals of man by direct correspondence, and that is the meaning of particular negation, and it necessarily implies the negation of universal affirmation because if the predicate is negated from some individuals of the subject, it is not negated from all its individuals as well, for universal affirmation is negated by particular negation. As for the second, i.e., 'not every', it indicates the negation of universal affirmation by direct correspondence and particular negation by implication, because when negation enters a sentence that contains an additional element over the affirmation or negation of something for something, it is directed to that additional element, not the original judgment, but rather its meaning affirms it. The meaning of our saying 'every man is a writer' is the affirmation of writing for every single individual of man, which is universal affirmation. So when we insert 'not' and say 'not every man is a writer', its explicit direct meaning is that it does not affirm writing for every single individual of man, which is the negation of universal affirmation. And that is either because writing is negated from every one, which is universal negation, or negated from some and affirmed for others, and in both cases it necessarily entails particular negation, unlike universal negation, for the general has no indication of the specific definitively, only probabilistically. Hence 'not every' is specifically made a quantifier for particular negation, taking the certain and definite and leaving the probable and doubtful.
Nor should it escape you that 'the negation of universal affirmation' is more general than 'particular negation', so that it might be said that the general has no indication of the specific, thus particular negation would be like universal negation in probability, and there would be no basis for specifying 'not every' as a quantifier for particular negation. And that the existence of the general does not entail the existence of the specific; how then could it be necessary for it, given that the necessary cannot exist without its necessary condition, rather it is equal to it? For the meaning of particular negation is the negation of the judgment from some, whether it is also negated from others or affirmed for them, and that is the meaning of the negation of universal affirmation. Indeed, it is more general than negation from some with affirmation for the others as before, and where is it from particular negation? Rather, particular negation is also more general than it.
This is the gist of the difference as mentioned in the books of the people (logicians). And I say: The mentioned difference is evident with respect to 'ba‘d laysa', but with respect to 'laysa ba‘d', it is not, for its explicit meaning is the negation of particular affirmation, just as the meaning of 'laysa kull' is the negation of universal affirmation, based on the mentioned rule.
The investigation is to say that 'laysa kull' and 'laysa ba‘d', if their negation is considered with respect to the proposition that follows them, then the direct meaning of 'laysa kull' is the negation of universal affirmation (which is particular negation), and the direct meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' is the negation of particular affirmation (which is universal negation). And if considered with respect to the predicate, then the direct meaning of 'laysa kull' is universal negation, and of 'laysa ba‘d' is particular negation. In other words, clearer than that: if the posteriority of 'some' and 'every' to the negation is considered, then 'laysa kull' is for the negation of universal affirmation, and 'laysa ba‘d' negates particular affirmation. And if their posteriority to it is not considered, then in 'laysa kull' it is universal negation, and in 'laysa ba‘d' it is particular negation. So reflect. However it may be, the difference mentioned in their books is not correct. That is the difference between the first two and the last. As for the difference between the first and the second: the first, i.e., 'laysa ba‘d', may be used for universal negation and may be used for particular negation according to the two considerations mentioned, and is never used for affirmation because the placement of the negation particle is to negate what follows it, so it is impossible for affirmation to result from it.
The second, i.e., 'ba‘d laysa', is not used for universal negation, because negation did not enter upon 'some'; rather 'some' is the subject, and when the negation particle intervenes between two things, it necessitates the negation of what follows it from what precedes it, so here it necessitates the negation of the predicate from some, and thus it is particular. And it may be used for affirmation if the negation particle is made part of the predicate, according to the rule of the affirmative with a modified predicate to be discussed later.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the standard logical distinction between three quantifier phrases used for particular negative propositions: 'laysa ba‘d' (not some), 'ba‘d laysa' (some not), and 'laysa kull' (not every). The author begins by stating the common explanation: the first two ('laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa') directly indicate particular negation (i.e., negation from some individuals) and imply the negation of universal affirmation (i.e., it is not true that all are such). The last ('laysa kull') does the opposite: it directly negates universal affirmation and implies particular negation. The author then elaborates on the reasoning: for 'laysa ba‘d' or 'ba‘d laysa', saying 'not some man is a writer' directly negates writing from some, and because negating from some implies that not all are writers, universal affirmation is negated by implication. For 'laysa kull', the negation enters upon the universal quantifier 'every', so the explicit meaning is that the universal affirmation is denied. This denial can be either because all are negated (universal negation) or only some are negated, but in either case particular negation follows necessarily. However, the author notes a problem: 'the negation of universal affirmation' is more general than 'particular negation', so it might be thought that 'laysa kull' could also indicate universal negation. But the author resolves this by clarifying that particular negation (salb juz'i) is defined as the negation of the predicate from some individuals, regardless of whether the rest are negated or affirmed. This is exactly the meaning of the negation of universal affirmation (raf' al-ijab al-kulli). Thus, the two are equivalent in this context. The author then criticizes the standard view: while the difference works for 'ba‘d laysa', it fails for 'laysa ba‘d', because the explicit meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' is the negation of particular affirmation (i.e., universal negation) according to the rule that negation targets what follows it. The author's own investigation ('tahqiq') provides a more precise analysis: if we consider the negation with respect to the whole proposition that follows 'laysa' and 'ba‘d' and 'kull', then 'laysa kull' directly negates universal affirmation (which equals particular negation), and 'laysa ba‘d' directly negates particular affirmation (which equals universal negation). If we consider the negation with respect to the predicate alone, then 'laysa kull' is universal negation and 'laysa ba‘d' is particular negation. The choice depends on whether we treat 'ba‘d' and 'kull' as following the negation or not. Finally, the author distinguishes between 'laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa': the former can be used for either universal or particular negation depending on the consideration, while the latter is always particular (negation from some) and can occasionally be used for affirmation when the negation is part of the predicate. This detailed analysis helps avoid confusion in propositional logic. For example, in Islamic legal reasoning, one might say 'laysa kullu khamrin haram' (not every intoxicant is forbidden), which would mean that some intoxicants are not forbidden, while 'ba‘d al-khamr laysa haram' (some intoxicants are not forbidden) directly states the same particular negation. Understanding these nuances is crucial for precise argumentation in fiqh and kalam.

Key Terms
ليس بعض (laysa ba‘ḍ): A quantifier phrase that, when considered with respect to the proposition following, directly negates particular affirmation (i.e., universal negation), and when considered with respect to the predicate, directly indicates particular negation. [Note: Standard logic often treats it simply as a particular negative; the author's refinement distinguishes two interpretations.]
بعض ليس (ba‘ḍ laysa): A quantifier phrase that directly indicates particular negation (negation from some individuals) and does not indicate universal negation; it may indicate affirmation if the negation is made part of the predicate. [Note: Commonly treated as synonymous with 'laysa ba‘ḍ', but the author distinguishes them.]
ليس كل (laysa kull): A quantifier phrase that, when considered with respect to the proposition following, directly negates universal affirmation (which is equivalent to particular negation), and when considered with respect to the predicate, directly indicates universal negation. [Note: Stable across texts.]
السلب الجزئى (as-salb al-juz’ī): The negation of the predicate from some individuals of the subject, whether or not the rest are also negated or affirmed. [Note: No significant shift.]
رفع الايجاب الكلى (raf‘ al-ījāb al-kullī): The negation of the universal affirmative proposition, meaning it is not the case that every individual has the predicate. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the standard view, what does 'laysa kull' directly indicate?
A) Universal negation (salb kullī)
B) Particular negation (salb juz’ī)
C) Negation of universal affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-kullī)
D) Negation of particular affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-juz’ī)

Key Takeaway
The standard distinction holds that 'laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa' directly indicate particular negation and imply universal negation, while 'laysa kull' does the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the proposition 'laysa kullu nabīdhin muskīr' (not every nabidh is intoxicating), what is the direct meaning and what is implied? Give an equivalent proposition using 'ba‘ḍ laysa'.
?
Translate the following into Arabic using the appropriate quantifier: 'Some scholars are not grammarians.' Explain whether you used 'laysa ba‘ḍ' or 'ba‘ḍ laysa' and why.

Immediate Inference

5 min
Refining the Distinction: The Author's Critique and Tahqiq
The author distinguishes the meanings of three quantifier phrases for particular negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the author's critical refinement to correctly interpret 'laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa' in both proposition-perspective and predicate-perspective.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' flips depending on whether 'ba‘d' is considered part of the proposition or part of the predicate.

The standard view has a flaw: it does not properly account for 'laysa ba‘d'. The author demonstrates that 'laysa ba‘d' directly negates particular affirmation (not particular negation) when considered with respect to the following proposition, and only indicates particular negation when considered with respect to the predicate. This lesson walks through his analysis and the resulting twofold interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Recap the standard view's problem with 'laysa ba‘d'
The author says the standard difference is clear for 'ba‘d laysa' but not for 'laysa ba‘d'. The explicit meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' is the negation of particular affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-juz’ī), just as 'laysa kull' negates universal affirmation.
2
Understand the principle: negation targets what follows it
When negation enters a sentence with an additional element (like 'kull' or 'ba‘d'), it targets that additional element. Thus, in 'laysa kull', it targets the universal quantifier; in 'laysa ba‘d', it targets the particular quantifier.
3
Apply the principle from the proposition-perspective
Consider the negation with respect to the whole proposition after the quantifier phrase. 'Laysa kull' directly indicates negation of universal affirmation (i.e., particular negation). 'Laysa ba‘d' directly indicates negation of particular affirmation (i.e., universal negation).
4
Apply the principle from the predicate-perspective
Now consider the negation with respect to the predicate alone. 'Laysa kull' directly indicates universal negation (the predicate is negated from every individual). 'Laysa ba‘d' directly indicates particular negation (the predicate is negated from some).
5
Compare and reflect on the two perspectives
The choice of perspective depends on whether we treat 'ba‘d' and 'kull' as following the negation (proposition-perspective) or not (predicate-perspective). The author notes that the standard view only works for the predicate-perspective.

Source Text
