قوله و سور السالبة الجزئية ليس بعض و بعض ليس وليس كل : الفرق بين : الامور الثلاثة : ان الاولين يدلان على السلب الجزئى بالمطابقة و على رفع الايجاب الكلى بالالتزام والاخير اعنى ليس كل بالعكس أما الأول فلان قولنا ليس بعض الانسان بكاتب» او بعضه ليس بكاتب» يدل على سلب الكتابة عن بعض افراد الانسان بالمطابقة و هو معنى السلب الجزئى و يلزمه رفع الايجاب الكلى لانه اذا سلب المحمول عن بعض افراد الموضوع يسلب عن كل افراده ايضاً فان الايجاب الكلى يرتفع بالسلب الجزئى. و اما الثانى اعنى ان ليس كل يدل على رفع الايجاب الكلى بالمطابقة و على السلب الجزئى بالالتزام فلان النفى اذا دخل على جملة فيها امر زايد على اثبات شيء لشيء او نفيه عنه انما يتوجه الى ذلك الامر الزايد دون اصل الحكم بل يفيد بمفهومه ثبوته و المفهوم من قولنا : «كل انسان كاتب ثبوت الكتابة لكل واحد واحد من افراد الانسان و هو الايجاب الكلى فاذا ادخلنا عليه «ليس وقلنا ليس كل انسان كاتباً كان معناه الصريح المطابق ان ليس يثبت الكتابة لكل واحد واحد من افراد الانسان و هو رفع الايجاب الكلى وذلك اما بان يكون الكتابة مسلوبة عن كل واحد و هو السلب الكلى او مسلوبة عن البعض ثابتة للبعض الآخر وعلى كلا التقديرين يلزمه السلب الجزئي البتة بخلاف السلب الكلى فانه لا دلالة للعام على الخاص قطعاً بل احتمالاً و لهذا اختص ليس كل سوراً بالسلب الجزئى اخذاً بالمتيقن المقطوع وتركاً للمحتمل المشكوك.
ولا يذهب عليك ان ليس رفع الايجاب الكلى اعم من السلب الجزئى حتى يقال انه لا دلالة للعام على الخاص فيكون السلب الجزئى مثل السلب الكلى فى الاحتمال فلا يكون لاختصاص ليس كل سوراً بالسلب الجزئى جهة وانه لا يلزم من وجود العام وجود الخاص فكيف يكون لازماً له والحال ان اللازم يمتنع وجود ملزومه بدونه بل هو مساو له ؟ لان معنى السلب الجزئى هو سلب الحكم عن البعض اعم من ان يكون مسلوباً عن البعض الآخر أيضاً او ثابتاً له و هو معنى رفع الايجاب الكلى، نعم هو اعم من السلب عن البعض مع الاثبات للاخر كما سبق و اين هو من السلب الجزئى؟ بل السلب الجزئى ايضاً اعم منه.
هذا محصل الفرق على ما هو المذكور فى كتب القوم واقول :
ان الفرق المذكور ظاهر بالنسبة الى بعض «ليس و اما بالنسبة الى «ليس بعض فلا، فإن مفهومه الصريح رفع الايجاب الجزئى كما ان مفهوم ليس كل هو رفع الايجاب الكلى بناء على القاعدة المذكورة.
والتحقيق ان يقال ان ليس كل» و «ليس بعض ان اعتبر سلبهما بالنسبة الى القضية التي بعد هما فمفهوم «ليس كل بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الكلى السلب الجزئى و مفهوم «ليس بعض» بالمطابقة هو رفع الايجاب الجزئى و هو السلب الكلى وان اعتبر بالنسبة إلى المحمول فالمفهوم المطابق لـ «ليس كل» هو السلب الكلى و لـ «ليس بعض هو السنب الجزئى وبعبارة أخرى أوضح من ذلك، اذا اعتبر تأخر «بعض» و «كل» عن النفى فـ ليس كل لرفع الايجاب الكلى و ليس بعض» ترفع الايجاب الجزئى و اذا لم يعتبر تأخر هما عنه فى «ليس كل السلب الكلى و ليس بعض السلب الجزئى فتأمل. و كيف كان لا يستقيم الفرق المذكور فى كتبهم، هذا هو الفرق بين الاولين و الاخير واما الفرق بين الأول والثانى فهو ان الاول اعنى: «ليس بعض» قد يذكر للسلب الكلى وقد يذكر للسلب الجزئى بالاعتبارين المذكورين ولا يذكر للايجاب البتة لأن وضع حرف السلب على رفع ما بعده فيمتنع ان يحصل الايجاب به.
والثاني اعنى بعض ليس لايذكر للسلب الكلى، لان النفى لم يدخل على بعض بل البعض هو الموضوع وحرف السلب اذا توسط بين الشيئين يقتضى سلب ما بعده عما قبله فيقتضى هنا سلب المحمول عن البعض فلا يكون الاجزئياً، وقد يذكر للايجاب اذا جعلت حرف السلب جزء من المحمول على ما هو حكم الموجبة المعدونة المحمول على ما سيأتي.
His statement: 'And the quantifier of the particular negative is 'not some', 'some not', and 'not every': the difference between the three matters: the first two indicate particular negation by direct correspondence (bi-l-mutabaqa) and the negation of universal affirmation by implication (bi-l-iltizam), while the last, i.e., 'not every', is the opposite. As for the first, because our saying 'not some man is a writer' or 'some of him is not a writer' indicates the negation of writing from some individuals of man by direct correspondence, and that is the meaning of particular negation, and it necessarily implies the negation of universal affirmation because if the predicate is negated from some individuals of the subject, it is not negated from all its individuals as well, for universal affirmation is negated by particular negation. As for the second, i.e., 'not every', it indicates the negation of universal affirmation by direct correspondence and particular negation by implication, because when negation enters a sentence that contains an additional element over the affirmation or negation of something for something, it is directed to that additional element, not the original judgment, but rather its meaning affirms it. The meaning of our saying 'every man is a writer' is the affirmation of writing for every single individual of man, which is universal affirmation. So when we insert 'not' and say 'not every man is a writer', its explicit direct meaning is that it does not affirm writing for every single individual of man, which is the negation of universal affirmation. And that is either because writing is negated from every one, which is universal negation, or negated from some and affirmed for others, and in both cases it necessarily entails particular negation, unlike universal negation, for the general has no indication of the specific definitively, only probabilistically. Hence 'not every' is specifically made a quantifier for particular negation, taking the certain and definite and leaving the probable and doubtful.
Nor should it escape you that 'the negation of universal affirmation' is more general than 'particular negation', so that it might be said that the general has no indication of the specific, thus particular negation would be like universal negation in probability, and there would be no basis for specifying 'not every' as a quantifier for particular negation. And that the existence of the general does not entail the existence of the specific; how then could it be necessary for it, given that the necessary cannot exist without its necessary condition, rather it is equal to it? For the meaning of particular negation is the negation of the judgment from some, whether it is also negated from others or affirmed for them, and that is the meaning of the negation of universal affirmation. Indeed, it is more general than negation from some with affirmation for the others as before, and where is it from particular negation? Rather, particular negation is also more general than it.
This is the gist of the difference as mentioned in the books of the people (logicians). And I say: The mentioned difference is evident with respect to 'ba‘d laysa', but with respect to 'laysa ba‘d', it is not, for its explicit meaning is the negation of particular affirmation, just as the meaning of 'laysa kull' is the negation of universal affirmation, based on the mentioned rule.
The investigation is to say that 'laysa kull' and 'laysa ba‘d', if their negation is considered with respect to the proposition that follows them, then the direct meaning of 'laysa kull' is the negation of universal affirmation (which is particular negation), and the direct meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' is the negation of particular affirmation (which is universal negation). And if considered with respect to the predicate, then the direct meaning of 'laysa kull' is universal negation, and of 'laysa ba‘d' is particular negation. In other words, clearer than that: if the posteriority of 'some' and 'every' to the negation is considered, then 'laysa kull' is for the negation of universal affirmation, and 'laysa ba‘d' negates particular affirmation. And if their posteriority to it is not considered, then in 'laysa kull' it is universal negation, and in 'laysa ba‘d' it is particular negation. So reflect. However it may be, the difference mentioned in their books is not correct. That is the difference between the first two and the last. As for the difference between the first and the second: the first, i.e., 'laysa ba‘d', may be used for universal negation and may be used for particular negation according to the two considerations mentioned, and is never used for affirmation because the placement of the negation particle is to negate what follows it, so it is impossible for affirmation to result from it.
The second, i.e., 'ba‘d laysa', is not used for universal negation, because negation did not enter upon 'some'; rather 'some' is the subject, and when the negation particle intervenes between two things, it necessitates the negation of what follows it from what precedes it, so here it necessitates the negation of the predicate from some, and thus it is particular. And it may be used for affirmation if the negation particle is made part of the predicate, according to the rule of the affirmative with a modified predicate to be discussed later.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the standard logical distinction between three quantifier phrases used for particular negative propositions: 'laysa ba‘d' (not some), 'ba‘d laysa' (some not), and 'laysa kull' (not every). The author begins by stating the common explanation: the first two ('laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa') directly indicate particular negation (i.e., negation from some individuals) and imply the negation of universal affirmation (i.e., it is not true that all are such). The last ('laysa kull') does the opposite: it directly negates universal affirmation and implies particular negation. The author then elaborates on the reasoning: for 'laysa ba‘d' or 'ba‘d laysa', saying 'not some man is a writer' directly negates writing from some, and because negating from some implies that not all are writers, universal affirmation is negated by implication. For 'laysa kull', the negation enters upon the universal quantifier 'every', so the explicit meaning is that the universal affirmation is denied. This denial can be either because all are negated (universal negation) or only some are negated, but in either case particular negation follows necessarily. However, the author notes a problem: 'the negation of universal affirmation' is more general than 'particular negation', so it might be thought that 'laysa kull' could also indicate universal negation. But the author resolves this by clarifying that particular negation (salb juz'i) is defined as the negation of the predicate from some individuals, regardless of whether the rest are negated or affirmed. This is exactly the meaning of the negation of universal affirmation (raf' al-ijab al-kulli). Thus, the two are equivalent in this context. The author then criticizes the standard view: while the difference works for 'ba‘d laysa', it fails for 'laysa ba‘d', because the explicit meaning of 'laysa ba‘d' is the negation of particular affirmation (i.e., universal negation) according to the rule that negation targets what follows it. The author's own investigation ('tahqiq') provides a more precise analysis: if we consider the negation with respect to the whole proposition that follows 'laysa' and 'ba‘d' and 'kull', then 'laysa kull' directly negates universal affirmation (which equals particular negation), and 'laysa ba‘d' directly negates particular affirmation (which equals universal negation). If we consider the negation with respect to the predicate alone, then 'laysa kull' is universal negation and 'laysa ba‘d' is particular negation. The choice depends on whether we treat 'ba‘d' and 'kull' as following the negation or not. Finally, the author distinguishes between 'laysa ba‘d' and 'ba‘d laysa': the former can be used for either universal or particular negation depending on the consideration, while the latter is always particular (negation from some) and can occasionally be used for affirmation when the negation is part of the predicate. This detailed analysis helps avoid confusion in propositional logic. For example, in Islamic legal reasoning, one might say 'laysa kullu khamrin haram' (not every intoxicant is forbidden), which would mean that some intoxicants are not forbidden, while 'ba‘d al-khamr laysa haram' (some intoxicants are not forbidden) directly states the same particular negation. Understanding these nuances is crucial for precise argumentation in fiqh and kalam.

Key Terms
ليس بعض (laysa ba‘ḍ): A quantifier phrase that, when considered with respect to the proposition following, directly negates particular affirmation (i.e., universal negation), and when considered with respect to the predicate, directly indicates particular negation. [Note: Standard logic often treats it simply as a particular negative; the author's refinement distinguishes two interpretations.]
بعض ليس (ba‘ḍ laysa): A quantifier phrase that directly indicates particular negation (negation from some individuals) and does not indicate universal negation; it may indicate affirmation if the negation is made part of the predicate. [Note: Commonly treated as synonymous with 'laysa ba‘ḍ', but the author distinguishes them.]
ليس كل (laysa kull): A quantifier phrase that, when considered with respect to the proposition following, directly negates universal affirmation (which is equivalent to particular negation), and when considered with respect to the predicate, directly indicates universal negation. [Note: Stable across texts.]
رفع الايجاب الجزئى (raf‘ al-ījāb al-juz’ī): The negation of the particular affirmative proposition, meaning it is not the case that some individuals have the predicate. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's tahqiq, what does 'laysa ba‘d' directly indicate when considered with respect to the proposition that follows it?
A) Particular negation (salb juz’ī)
B) Universal negation (salb kullī)
C) Negation of particular affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-juz’ī)
D) Negation of universal affirmation (raf‘ al-ījāb al-kullī)

Key Takeaway
The correct interpretations depend on whether we consider the quantifier as part of the proposition or as affecting the predicate; the standard view is only valid for the latter.

The Dialectic of Proposition Division: Objection and Answer
Critique and refinement of the division of propositions into external, real, and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical objection to the threefold division of propositions and evaluate the adequacy of the responses using the principle of man' al-khuluw.

Core Idea
The division ensures subject existence in at least one mode, not exclusive categorization.

In classical logic, propositions are divided by the mode of existence of their subject: external, real, and mental. But does this division cover all cases? An objection from arithmetic propositions challenges its exhaustiveness. This lesson walks you through the objection, the author's answers, and the refined logical principle that resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the passage where the objector claims that propositions like 'Four multiplied by itself yields sixteen' do not fit any of the three divisions because the subject exists both mentally and externally. Note that the external proposition is defined as judgment on actually existing external subjects, the real proposition on potentially existing external subjects, and the mental proposition on mental subjects only.
The objector argues that such arithmetic examples are not included in any single category.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer reclassifies the arithmetic proposition as mental because the judgment is only on mental individuals (the concept of numbers), even though it is true externally. Identify the potential contradiction noted: this answer conflicts with a later gloss about subjects that have no possible external individuals.
This answer implies the arithmetic proposition is purely mental, which may not hold for all such cases.
3
Understand the second answer (preferred)
The author offers a more robust answer: the division is about 'man' al-khuluw' (prevention of emptiness), meaning an affirmative proposition must have its subject existing in at least one of these modes, but it may involve multiple modes. The categories are not mutually exclusive. How does this principle resolve the objection?
This avoids the need to fit the proposition into a single exclusive type.
4
Clarify the real proposition definition
The real proposition (haqiqiyya) is redefined to include subjects potentially existing in the outside, whether actually existent or hypothetical. Examples: 'Every phoenix is a bird' (non-existent subject) and 'Every human is an animal' (existing subjects) both fall under it. How does this broader definition resolve the second objection?
This definition encompasses both actual and potential external individuals.

يجاب عن الأول : بانالانسلم ان الحكم فى نحو قولهم: الاربعة اذا ضربت في نفسها يحصل سنة عشر على جميع الأفراد الذهنية و الخارجية المحققة والمقدرة بل على الافراد الذهنية فقط و ان كان الحكم صادقاً حقاً بالنسبة الى الافراد الخارجية ايضاً فان غرضهم من امثال ذلك، الافادة و التعليم وهو يحصل بصدق الحكم على الافراد الذهنية فقط فحينئذ تكون داخلة تحت القضية الذهنية فلا يلزم محذور. ولا يخفى ان هذا على فرض سبيل منع الجمع بمعنى ان الموجبة لابد ان يكون موضوعها موجوداً بنحو من هذه الانحاء المذكورة لامحالة واما ان كل واحد من هذه الانواع يجب ان يكون منفرداً عن الاخيرين فليس بمراد كما لا يخفى. و عن الثانى بان او على جميع الافراد سواء كانت موجودة او معدومة كقولنا : «كل انسان حیوان فانه بمعنى ان كل ماصدق عليه الانسان في الخارج صدق عليه الحيوان فيه، سواء كان موجوداً او معدوماً فليتأمل فان هذا المقام زحلفة من زحاليف المنطقيين.
His saying: (by consideration of) the existence of its subject, it has three divisions. It was objected that this division is not exhaustive, for it is possible that the judgment in a proposition is upon a subject existing both in the mind and in the outside, actually and potentially, like the saying of the arithmeticians: 'Four when multiplied by itself yields sixteen,' and 'Sixteen when divided by four yields four,' and other propositions used in arithmetic. Without doubt, such like are not included in any of the three divisions, because the apparent meaning from the author and the glossator is that the external proposition is that in which the judgment is upon a subject existing in the outside only actually, and the real proposition is that in which the judgment is upon a subject existing in the outside only potentially, and similarly the mental proposition is that in which the judgment is upon a subject existing in the mind only. Also, the real proposition according to what is mentioned in the books of the community is divided into two types: they said that the real proposition is that in which judgment is upon individuals potential with respect to the outside, or upon absolute individuals whether actual or potential. And therefore they criticized some investigators who defined the real proposition as that in which judgment is upon all individuals, actual and potential, because that is not necessary; for the real proposition may have the judgment only upon potential individuals, so reflect. And the answer to the first objection is that we do not accept that the judgment in such as their saying 'Four when multiplied by itself yields sixteen' is upon all mental and external individuals, actual and potential, but rather only upon mental individuals, even if the judgment is true with respect to external individuals as well, for their purpose in such examples is instruction and teaching, and that is achieved by the truth of the judgment upon mental individuals only. Then they fall under the mental proposition, so no objection arises. But it is not hidden that this, if granted, contradicts what will come from the glossator in his saying: 'And this they considered only in subjects that have no individuals possible of existence in the outside.' So it is better to say: the discussion is on the way of prevention of emptiness (man' al-khuluw), not on the way of truth, nor on the way of prevention of conjunction (man' al-jam'), meaning that an affirmative proposition must have its subject existing in one of these mentioned ways necessarily, but that each one of these types must be isolated from the others is not intended, as is not hidden. And as for the second objection, that what is meant by the subject existing in the outside potentially is whatever its existence is potential, whether it exists actually in the outside or is non-existent with potential existence in it, so it includes what the judgment is upon non-existent potential individuals, like our saying 'Every phoenix is a bird', or upon all individuals whether existent or non-existent, like our saying 'Every human is an animal', for it means that every individual of which 'human' is true in the outside, 'animal' is true of him in it, whether existent or non-existent. So let one reflect, for this station is one of the slippery places of the logicians.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the division of propositions into three types based on the mode of existence of the subject: external (khārijiyya), real (haqīqiyya), and mental (dhihniyya). The commentator raises an objection that this division is not exhaustive. The objector gives an example from arithmetic: 'Four when multiplied by itself yields sixteen.' The judgment applies to the number four whether it is considered mentally or externally, actual or potential, so such a proposition does not fit neatly into any of the three exclusive categories as commonly understood. The objector also notes that the 'real proposition' itself is sometimes defined in two different ways: as judgment on potential external individuals only, or on all individuals (actual and potential). The author then provides two answers. The first answer argues that in the arithmetic example, the judgment is actually only on mental individuals (the numbers as concepts), even though it is true of external ones; hence it falls under the mental proposition, so no problem arises. However, the commentator notes a potential contradiction with a later statement by the glossator about subjects that have no possible external individuals. Thus a second, more robust answer is preferred: the intent of the division is not that each proposition must exclusively belong to one type; rather, an affirmative proposition necessarily requires its subject to exist in some mode among these three, but it is possible that a proposition involves more than one mode simultaneously (e.g., a subject existing both in the mind and externally in different respects). The second answer clarifies that the 'real proposition' includes subjects that are 'potentially existing' in the outside, whether they actually exist or are merely hypothetical. Thus, examples like 'Every phoenix is a bird' (where phoenix has no actual external existence) and 'Every human is an animal' (where humans exist actually) both fall under the real proposition because the judgment is about individuals potentially existing externally. The passage ends with a caution that this topic is a subtle pitfall for logicians.

Key Terms
القضية الخارجية
القضية الحقيقية
القضية الذهنية
منع الخلو

Key Takeaway
The division of propositions into external, real, and mental is not exclusive but ensures that the subject exists in at least one of these modes (man' al-khuluw), allowing a proposition to involve multiple modes simultaneously.

Two Meanings of Necessity in Conditioned Universals
Distinguishing two senses of ضرورة in المشروطة العامة

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of necessity in a conditioned universal proposition and identify the correct example for each sense.

Core Idea
In conditioned universals, necessity may attach to the composite or to the subject alone.

This passage from Mirza Muhammad Ali's supercommentary clarifies a crucial ambiguity: when we say 'every X is Y as long as X is Z', is the necessity of Y for X relative to the composite of X and Z, or to X alone? The commentator distinguishes two meanings and corrects a mis-example, showing the importance of precision in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
The passage opens by stating that the predicate's necessity can be either for the subject during the time of the description's existence or not. Recognize this as the starting point of the distinction.
The first meaning is exemplified by 'every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed'.
2
Examine the examples
First example: 'Every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed.' Second example: 'Every writer is human as long as he writes.' Notice the difference in what is necessary: darkness vs. humanity.
The eclipse is temporary; humanity is permanent.
3
Understand the difference in relation
In the first meaning, necessity is for the composite of the subject's essence and the attributive description. In the second, necessity is for the subject's essence alone, with the description as a mere circumstance.
The author adopts the second meaning.
4
See the overlap and divergence
The two meanings sometimes coincide (e.g., 'eclipsed moon is dark') but can diverge: the first is true without the second in 'every writer moves fingers as long as writing'; the second is true without the first in 'every writer is human as long as writing'.
This shows they are 'general and specific in one respect'.
5
Apply to correct the example
The commentator criticizes the earlier example of 'moving fingers' because movement is not necessary for the writer's essence. The correct example for the second meaning is 'every writer is human', since human is necessary of the essence.
Writing is not necessary for the subject, so what depends on writing cannot be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوصف ضرورياً له فى زمان ثبوته له ام لا الاول كقولنا كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفاً فان الانحساف ضرورى له في زمان ثبوته و هو وقت الحيلولة على ما سيأتى والثانى كقولنا كل كاتب انسان مادام كاتباً. والفرق بين المعنيين ان الضرورة فى الأول بالنسبة الى ذات الموضوع الموصوف بالوصف العنوانى اعنى: الى مجموع الذات والوصف و فى الثانى بالنسبة الى ذات الموضوع من حيث هو هو والوصف معتبر على أنه ظرف لها لاجزء لما نسبت اليه والنسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه التصادقهما في نحو قولنا: بالضرورة كل منخسف مظلم مادام منخسفاً مما يكون الوصف العنوانى ضرورى الثبوت لذات الموضوع عند ثبوته له وصدق الاول بدون الثانى فى نحو قولنا : بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً و صدق الثاني بدون الاول في نحو قولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب انسان مادام كاتباً. والمعتبر عند المصنف هو المعنى الثانى كما هو صريح عبارته هنا و ظاهر كلامه في مبحث التناقض حيث حكم بان نقيض المشروطة العامة «الحينية المطلقة» على ماسيأتي، فالأولى ان يمثل المحشى بنحو: كل كاتب انسان مادام كاتباً، فان المثال الذي ذكره لا يصح . الى هذا المعنى فان التحرك ليس بضرورى الثبوت لذات الكاتب فى شيء من الاوقات فان الكتابة التى هى شرط تحقق الضرورة غير ضرورية لذات الموضوع فكيف يكون التحرك الذى هو مشروط بها ضرورياً؟ فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
The description is necessarily [true] for it at the time of its being established for it or not. The first, as our saying: 'Every eclipsed [moon] is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' for the eclipse is necessary for it at the time of its being established, which is the time of the earth's interposition, as will come. And the second, as our saying: 'Every writer is human as long as he is writing.' And the difference between the two meanings is that the necessity in the first is with respect to the essence of the subject described by the attributive description, i.e., to the composite of essence and description; and in the second, with respect to the essence of the subject in itself, and the description is considered as a circumstance for it, not as a part of that to which the relation is attributed. The relation between them is general and specific in one respect: they coincide in our saying: 'Necessarily, every eclipsed [moon] is dark as long as it is eclipsed,' where the attributive description is necessarily true of the subject's essence when it is established for it; and the first is true without the second in our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is writing'; and the second is true without the first in our saying: 'Necessarily, every writer is human as long as he is writing.' And what is considered by the author is the second meaning, as is clear from his expression here and apparent from his words in the chapter on contradiction, where he rules that the contradictory of the conditioned universal is the 'absolute temporal' [الحينية المطلقة], as will come. So it is better for the commentator to give an example like: 'Every writer is human as long as he is writing'; for the example he mentioned is not correct according to this meaning, because movement is not necessary of the essence of the writer at any time; for writing, which is the condition for realizing the necessity, is not necessary for the subject's essence; so how can movement, which is conditioned by it, be necessary? So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical discussion by the hashiya commentator distinguishing two senses of necessity in conditioned universal propositions (المشروطة العامة). The author begins by identifying two possibilities for the predicate's necessity relative to the subject under a condition: either the predicate is necessarily true of the subject during the time the condition holds, or it is not. The first meaning is exemplified by 'every eclipsed moon is dark as long as it is eclipsed' – here, the eclipse is necessary for the subject during the time of interposition. The second meaning is exemplified by 'every writer is human as long as he is writing' – here, being human is necessary of the writer in himself, and writing is merely a temporal circumstance. The difference is that in the first, necessity attaches to the composite of the subject's essence and the attributive description; in the second, it attaches to the subject's essence alone. The commentator then discusses the relation between the two: they are 'general and specific in one respect' (عموم من وجه), meaning they overlap but differ in extension. He provides examples where one is true without the other. He then asserts that the author of the matn adopts the second meaning, as evidenced by his ruling that the contradictory of the conditioned universal is the 'absolute temporal' proposition. The commentator then criticizes the example given by the previous commentator – that example being 'every writer is moving his fingers' – because movement is not necessarily true of the writer's essence; writing itself is not necessary for the subject, so what depends on writing cannot be necessary. Hence the correct example for the second meaning is 'every writer is human.'

Key Terms
الضرورة
المشروطة العامة
الوصف العنواني

Key Takeaway
The two meanings of ضرورة in المشروطة العامة depend on whether necessity is attributed to the composite of essence and description or to the essence alone with description as circumstance.

Scope of Negation with the Necessity Qualifier
Author analyzes the placement of 'بالضرورة' in negative propositions and its effect on meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether 'بالضرورة' qualifies the negation or the predicate in a negative modal proposition and explain the rule for negation scope.

Core Idea
In negative propositions, 'بالضرورة' qualifies the negation, not the predicate, contrary to a common misunderstanding.

This lesson examines a subtle but crucial point in interpreting modalized negative propositions. The commentator clarifies that the qualifier 'بالضرورة' (necessarily) attaches to the negation itself, not to the predicate. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly parsing logical statements in Islamic semantics and avoiding misinterpretation of modal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Read the first part of the passage: the commentator cites an earlier statement that in a modalized proposition, the affirmative or negative relation might be considered necessary. This is the claim being refined.
2
Locate the correction
The commentator asserts that 'بالضرورة' is a qualifier (قيد) of the negation, not the negated. This changes the meaning: it is the necessity that is denied, not the predicate.
3
Understand the objection
An objector cites a well-known semantic rule: when negation enters a sentence with an extra element, it negates that extra element, implying the original event is affirmed. For example, 'ما جاءني زيد راكباً' means 'Zaid did not come riding' (negation of riding, implying he came).
This rule is from 'ulama al-ma'ani (masters of semantics).
4
Analyze the response
The commentator responds by distinguishing cases: the rule applies when the qualifier is considered after the negation, but in our case, the qualifier (بالضرورة) is considered subsequent to the negation, so the rule does not apply. The negation here directly negates necessity.
Thus, the apparent contradiction is resolved by noting the order of qualifier and negation.
5
Apply the general principle
The commentator states that this ruling applies to all negative modalized propositions (الموجهات السوالب). Practice with another example to reinforce the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «ولاشيء منه بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً»: لا يخفى ان قوله: «بالضرورة قيد للنفى لا المنفى حتى يكون المعنى نفى الضرورة عن نسبة المحمول الى الموضوع مادام الوصف و يكون المحمول جايز الثبوت بالنسبة الى الموضوع فلا يصح ما ذكره (ره) قبيل هذا اى قد يكون الحكم في القضية الموجهة بان النسبة الثبوتية او السلبية ضرورية ... و على هذا القياس حكم جميع الموجهات السوالب فلا تغفل وكن من هذا على ذكر. قيل: : ان ذلك ينا فى القاعدة المشهورة المقررة عند ارباب المعانى من ان النقى اذا دخل على جملة مشتملة على أمر زايد على اثبات شيء لشيء او نفيه عنه توجه الى ذلك الأمر الزايد و افاد بمثوله ثبوت اصل المعنى مثلاً اذا قلنا ما جائنى زيد راكباً يكون المعنى نفى الركوب لا المجيء. قلنا: قد تقدم ان هذه القاعدة جارية حين اعتبر النفى متأخراً عن القيد او لم يعتبر القيد متأخراً عنه على خلاف في ذلك و أما اذا اعتبر القيد متأخراً عنه فلاوما نحن فيه من هذا القبيل وما ذكر من التنافى انما يأتى لو لم يكن من هذا القبيل او كان من القبيل الاول. (میرزا محمد علی)
His saying: 'And nothing of it is with moving fingers necessarily as long as he is writing' – it is not hidden that his saying 'necessarily' is a qualifier of the negation, not the negated, so that the meaning is the negation of necessity from the relation of the predicate to the subject as long as the description [holds], and the predicate becomes possible with respect to the subject. So what he mentioned earlier is not correct, i.e., that the ruling in a modalized proposition might be that the affirmative or negative relation is necessary... And on this measure is the ruling of all negative modalized propositions, so do not neglect and be mindful of this. It was said: This contradicts the famous rule established among masters of semantics that when negation enters a sentence that contains an extra element beyond affirming or negating something of something, it is directed to that extra element, and by its meaning implies the affirmation of the original meaning; for example, when we say 'Zaid did not come riding,' the meaning is negation of riding, not of coming. We say: It has preceded that this rule applies when the negation is considered subsequent to the qualifier, or when the qualifier is not considered subsequent to it, with disagreement about that. But if the qualifier is considered subsequent to it, then it is not so, and our case is of this type. And what was mentioned of contradiction only occurs if it were not of this type, or if it were of the first type. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical point in interpreting modalized negative propositions. The commentator examines the statement 'ولاشيء منه بساكن الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً' and argues that the word 'بالضرورة' (necessarily) is a qualifier (قيد) of the negation itself, not of the negated predicate. The intended meaning is that the necessity is denied from the relation of predicate to subject under the condition, making the predicate merely possible. This contradicts an earlier claim that the ruling might be that the relation is necessary. The commentator then addresses an objection based on a well-known semantic rule: when negation enters a sentence with an extra element (like 'riding'), it negates that extra element, implying the original event is affirmed. For example, 'ما جاءني زيد راكباً' means 'Zaid did not come riding' (negation of riding), implying he came but not riding. The commentator responds that this rule applies when the qualifier is considered after the negation or when the qualifier is not considered subsequent, but in the present case, the qualifier (بالضرورة) is considered subsequent to the negation, so the rule does not apply. Thus, the negation here negates necessity itself, not the predicate.

بالضرورة
النفي
الموجهات السوالب

A) The predicate 'كاتب'
B) The negation 'ليس'
C) The subject 'الإنسان'
D) The condition 'مادام'

Key Takeaway
The qualifier 'بالضرورة' in negative propositions attaches to the negation, making the meaning that the necessity of the relation is denied, not that the relation itself is necessarily negated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Immediate Inference
intermediate
5 min
Necessity vs. Perpetuity: Absolute Generality
Author explains the absolute generality relation between necessity and perpetuity propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessity (darura) and perpetuity (dawam) by identifying the condition of essential impossibility and applying this distinction to modal propositions in Kalam and logic.

Core Idea
Necessity implies perpetuity but not vice versa; only necessity has essential impossibility.

This lesson clarifies a crucial distinction in modal logic between two types of universal propositions: necessary (darura) and perpetual (dawam). The commentator explains that the relation is one of absolute generality: necessity always implies perpetuity, but not the reverse. The key is the condition of essential impossibility. Understanding this distinction is vital for evaluating modal arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modal terms
Locate 'al-ḍarūra' and 'al-dawām' in the Arabic passage. Note that necessity implies the predicate holds with impossibility of separation, while perpetuity only implies constant holding.
2
Recognize the logical relation
The commentator states 'al-ʿumūm al-muṭlaq' (absolute generality): every instance of necessity is an instance of perpetuity, but not vice versa.
3
Understand the condition of essential impossibility
Necessity requires 'al-istiḥāla al-nāshiʾa min dhāt al-mawḍūʿ' (impossibility arising from the subject's essence). Perpetuity may allow separation from an external cause.
4
Verify via contradictories
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is the 'general possible' (al-mumkina al-ʿāmma), which is broader than the contradictory of a perpetual proposition, proving the asymmetry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصله ان النسبة بينهما هي العموم المطلق فانه كلما صدقت الضرورة صدق الدوام من غير عكس فان الدوام قد يكون غير مستحيل فلا يكون ضرورة البتة فانها لابد وان يكون مستحلية فان المراد من الاستحالة الاستحالة الناشئة ذات الموضوع لا المطلقة الناشئة منها او من غيرها والالكانت الضرورية والدائمة متساويتين وهو باطل قطعاً فان نقيض الضرورية و هو الممكنة العامة اعم من نقيض الدائمة.
The summary is that the relation between them is absolute generality: whenever necessity is true, perpetuity is true, without the reverse. For perpetuity may be without impossibility [of separation], so it is not necessity at all, for necessity must be with impossibility. Indeed, what is meant by impossibility is the impossibility arising from the essence of the subject, not the absolute impossibility arising from it or from something else; otherwise, the necessary and the perpetual would be equivalent, which is absolutely false. For the contradictory of the necessary, which is the general possible, is more general than the contradictory of the perpetual.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two key modal concepts: الضرورة (necessity) and الدوام (perpetuity). The relation between them is 'absolute generality' (العموم المطلق): every necessary proposition is perpetual, but not every perpetual proposition is necessary. Perpetuity only means that the predicate holds at all times, but the opposite could still be possible; necessity requires the impossibility of the opposite (استحالة الانفكاك). The commentator clarifies that the required impossibility is one that arises from the essence of the subject itself (الاستحالة الناشئة من ذات الموضوع), not an absolute impossibility that could come from an external cause. If that distinction were not made, necessity and perpetuity would coincide, which is false. The proof is that the contradictory of a necessary proposition is the 'general possible' (الممكنة العامة), which is broader than the contradictory of a perpetual proposition.

Key Terms
الضرورة
الدوام
الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relation between a necessary proposition (ḍarūriyya) and a perpetual proposition (dāʾima)?
A) They are equivalent.
B) Necessity implies perpetuity, but not vice versa.
C) Perpetuity implies necessity, but not vice versa.
D) They are completely unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Necessity (al-ḍarūra) is stronger than perpetuity (al-dawām): it adds the condition of essential impossibility of the opposite, which perpetuity lacks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'God is existent.' Is this necessary or perpetual? Justify using the condition of essential impossibility.
?
